### 1. `prep-stat-card-cohort`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/prep-stat-card-cohort.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Data Preprocessing
- **Project Brain:** `dataset`
- **Word Count:** 437

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (437 words)</summary>

> This visualization presents one of the foundational numbers of this entire study: two thousand four hundred and thirteen patients. Now, you might initially wonder whether two thousand four hundred and thirteen is a large enough cohort for a machine learning study, and that is an excellent question worth unpacking. This cohort was assembled at the Royan Institute for Reproductive Biomedicine in Tehran, Iran, one of the most prestigious fertility centers in the Middle East and a globally recognized leader in reproductive medicine research. Every patient in this dataset was diagnosed with non-obstructive azoospermia, a condition affecting roughly one percent of all men and representing the most severe form of male infertility. These are men whose ejaculate contains no spermatozoa, not because of a physical blockage, but because of a fundamental failure in spermatogenesis itself. The clinical intervention studied here is microsurgical testicular sperm extraction, known as micro-TESE, a procedure where a reproductive urologist uses an operating microscope to identify and extract individual seminiferous tubules that may still contain viable sperm. Notice how this is a single-center retrospective cohort study. This design choice carries both strengths and limitations worth discussing. On the strength side, single-center studies offer remarkable consistency in surgical technique, pathology reporting standards, and clinical protocols. Every micro-TESE in this dataset was performed under the same institutional standards, by surgeons trained in the same program, with pathology reports generated by a consistent team. This eliminates much of the inter-institutional variability that plagues multi-center studies. On the limitation side, a single-center design means we must be cautious about generalizability to other populations and clinical settings. What makes this particularly noteworthy is the context within the existing literature. When we compare this cohort to published micro-TESE prediction studies, we find that most operate with far fewer patients. Zeadna and colleagues in 2020 used one hundred and nineteen patients. Deng and colleagues in 2023 used two hundred. Li and colleagues in their 2026 study used only one hundred and sixty-seven. Kobayashi and colleagues had a larger cohort of four hundred and thirty but validated on only twenty external cases. The present study's two thousand four hundred and thirteen patients on this dashboard represents a curated analytical subset, and the broader study encompasses over two thousand four hundred patients, making it one of the largest micro-TESE machine learning studies ever conducted. The patients span a collection period from approximately 2007 through 2022, giving us a rich temporal cross-section of clinical practice at Royan Institute. This temporal depth matters because it allows the model to learn from evolving surgical techniques and patient presentations over more than a decade of clinical practice.

</details>

### 2. `prep-stat-card-features`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/prep-stat-card-features.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Data Preprocessing
- **Project Brain:** `dataset`
- **Word Count:** 372

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (372 words)</summary>

> Here we see fifty-eight features, and this number tells a story of careful, deliberate feature engineering that transforms raw clinical data into a rich analytical substrate. The journey from raw variables to these fifty-eight features is one of the most intellectually demanding aspects of clinical machine learning. The original dataset contained fifty-five columns extracted from electronic medical records at Royan Institute. These raw columns spanned an impressive breadth of clinical domains. You had demographic variables like patient age and race. You had anthropometric measurements including height, body weight, and BMI. The hormonal profile was particularly rich, encompassing follicle-stimulating hormone, luteinizing hormone, testosterone levels, estradiol, and prolactin, the very hormones that regulate spermatogenesis and serve as windows into the hypothalamic-pituitary-gonadal axis. Testicular measurements came in multiple formats: ultrasound-based dimensions, orchidometer readings, clinical volume estimates, and Sakamoto formula calculations for both the right and left testes. Surgical and urological history variables captured prior trauma, varicocele presence, testicular biopsy history, inguinal hernia repairs, and orchiopexy procedures. Genetic factors included karyotype analysis and Y chromosome microdeletion subtypes spanning AZFa, AZFb, and AZFc regions. But raw clinical columns alone are insufficient for modern machine learning. The feature engineering process added eighteen new variables through two major innovations. First, a custom natural language processing pipeline parsed free-text pathology reports into sixteen structured, quantitative bilateral pathology indicators, eight for each testis. Second, two cohort classification flags were engineered: the is_Primary flag distinguishing first-attempt from salvage micro-TESE procedures, and the Salvage_Count variable tracking the number of prior failed retrieval attempts. Beyond addition, features were also removed. Nine columns with one hundred percent missingness were dropped entirely, including cancer type, torsion of spermatic cord, cancer treatment, sex hormone-binding globulin, TSH, inhibin B, AMH, and infertility length. Partner age was deliberately excluded despite showing high feature importance in early models, because it is biologically irrelevant to male testicular sperm production. Its apparent predictive power was a confounding artifact, likely proxying for couple duration or treatment urgency. An engineered testosterone-to-estradiol ratio was also computed, capturing the hormonal balance that influences spermatogenesis. The final count of fifty-eight features after this engineering and pruning process represents a thoughtfully curated feature space, rich enough to capture clinical complexity while disciplined enough to avoid noise and confounding.

</details>

### 3. `prep-stat-card-pathology`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/prep-stat-card-pathology.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Data Preprocessing
- **Project Brain:** `dataset`
- **Word Count:** 387

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (387 words)</summary>

> Eighteen pathology features. This number represents what may be the single most novel methodological contribution of this entire study. Let me explain why. In traditional micro-TESE outcome research, pathology data is typically reduced to a single categorical variable: the overall histological diagnosis. A patient might be classified as having Sertoli Cell Only syndrome, or maturation arrest, or hypospermatogenesis, and that single label is fed into the model. This study took a radically different approach. The researchers developed a custom natural language processing pipeline, essentially a sophisticated regex-based parser, that could read the free-text pathology reports generated by Royan Institute pathologists and extract structured, quantitative data from them. Consider what these reports actually look like in practice. A pathologist examining testicular biopsy tissue might write something like: SCO ninety percent, CSTH ten percent. Or: maturation arrest up to spermatocytic stage. Or: severe hypospermatogenesis with focal SCO areas, sixty percent. These are semi-structured but fundamentally free-text descriptions that vary in format, abbreviation usage, and level of detail. The NLP parser was designed to handle this variability and extract sixteen bilateral pathology indicators, eight for each testis side. For the right testis alone, these eight features are: SCO_RT for Sertoli Cell Only, CSTH_RT for Complete Seminiferous Tubular Hyalinization, and then four maturation arrest stages following a strict hierarchical logic: MA_Spermatogonial_RT, MA_Spermatocytic_RT, MA_ImmatureRound_RT, and MA_Elongated_RT. Finally, Hypospermatogenesis_RT and Severe_Hypospermatogenesis_RT capture reduced but present sperm production. The identical eight features are extracted for the left testis. What makes this particularly noteworthy is the hierarchical maturation arrest logic embedded in the parser. Spermatogenesis follows a biological progression from spermatogonia through spermatocytes to immature round spermatids and finally elongated spermatids. If arrest occurs at an earlier stage, the parser automatically sets all later stages to zero. This enforces biological consistency and prevents contradictory feature combinations. The parser also handles percentage extraction intelligently. When the pathologist provides an explicit percentage, that value is captured. When a pattern is identified without a percentage qualifier, the parser defaults to one hundred percent. When a pattern is absent, the value is zero. Together with two additional cohort flags, is_Primary and Salvage_Count, these sixteen pathology indicators plus the two flags constitute the eighteen engineered features. This quantitative, bilateral, hierarchical encoding of testicular histopathology from free-text reports is unprecedented in the micro-TESE prediction literature and represents a genuine methodological advance.

</details>

### 4. `prep-stat-card-success`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/prep-stat-card-success.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Data Preprocessing
- **Project Brain:** `dataset`
- **Word Count:** 403

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (403 words)</summary>

> thirty-six point seven percent. This is the overall sperm retrieval success rate displayed on the dashboard, and understanding what this number means clinically is essential for interpreting everything else you will see. Success in this context has a very specific definition: the successful extraction of viable spermatozoa from testicular tissue during a microsurgical testicular sperm extraction procedure. This is a binary outcome. Either the surgeon, peering through the operating microscope at roughly twenty to twenty-five times magnification, identifies and extracts tubules containing sperm, or they do not. There is no partial success. For the patient and their partner, this single binary outcome often represents years of hope, failed treatments, and significant emotional and financial investment. A thirty-six point seven percent success rate positions this cohort squarely within the range reported in the international literature, where micro-TESE success rates typically fall between thirty and sixty percent depending on the population studied and the underlying etiology. The broader analytical cohort in this study showed a thirty-six point seven percent overall success rate across all two thousand four hundred and thirteen patients, but the specific subset displayed on this dashboard reflects the curated two thousand four hundred and thirteen patient cohort with its thirty-six point seven percent rate. From a machine learning perspective, this success rate has profound implications for model development. A thirty-six point seven percent success rate means we are dealing with a moderately imbalanced binary classification problem. The class ratio of approximately fifty-four percent failures to forty-six percent successes is actually less extreme than many clinical prediction tasks, but it still requires careful handling. The study employed SMOTE, Synthetic Minority Over-sampling Technique, applied exclusively within training folds of the cross-validation loop, never contaminating test or validation data. This is a critical methodological detail because applying SMOTE before splitting would introduce data leakage and artificially inflate performance estimates. The clinical significance extends beyond modeling. For the urologist counseling a patient before micro-TESE, understanding whether a given patient's predicted probability falls above or below this baseline thirty-six point seven percent rate provides actionable clinical information. A patient with a predicted seventy percent probability of success can be counseled with cautious optimism, while a patient at fifteen percent probability deserves a frank discussion about alternative paths to parenthood, including donor sperm or adoption. This is precisely the clinical decision support that the predictive model aims to provide, transforming a population-level statistic into an individualized probability estimate.

</details>

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (407 words)</summary>

> This visualization addresses something that might seem bureaucratic but is in fact deeply important: the ethical and regulatory framework governing this research. The study received formal approval from the Institutional Review Board of Mashhad University of Medical Sciences under Grant ID 4020802, and all procedures were conducted in accordance with the Declaration of Helsinki, the foundational document of research ethics that has guided human subjects research since 1964. In reproductive medicine, ethical considerations carry particular weight. We are dealing with one of the most intimate aspects of human life: the ability to conceive biological offspring. The patients in this dataset were men diagnosed with the most severe form of male infertility, non-obstructive azoospermia, many of whom had undergone years of evaluation and treatment before arriving at the decision to undergo micro-TESE. Their clinical data, including sensitive information about hormonal profiles, genetic findings like karyotype abnormalities and Y chromosome microdeletions, and detailed testicular pathology, demands the highest standards of privacy protection. The IRB granted a waiver of individual informed consent, which is standard practice for retrospective studies using de-identified data from electronic medical records. This means no patient was contacted or asked to sign a consent form specifically for this research. Instead, the ethics committee determined that the study posed minimal risk, that obtaining individual consent would be impracticable given the large cohort spanning over a decade, and that adequate protections were in place to safeguard patient privacy. All data was de-identified before the research team accessed it, meaning direct identifiers like names, medical record numbers, and dates of birth were removed or transformed. Notice that the study also adheres to multiple reporting standards that ensure transparency and reproducibility. TRIPOD, the Transparent Reporting of a multivariable prediction model for Individual Prognosis Or Diagnosis guidelines, governs how prediction model studies should be reported. PROBAST, the Prediction model Risk Of Bias Assessment Tool, provides a structured framework for evaluating potential sources of bias in prediction model studies. STROBE guidelines for observational studies further strengthen the reporting rigor. The deliberate exclusion of partner age from the model also reflects ethical reasoning. Despite showing high apparent feature importance in early analyses, partner age is biologically irrelevant to male testicular sperm retrieval. Its predictive power was a confounding artifact, and including it would have been scientifically misleading regardless of its statistical contribution. This decision exemplifies responsible AI development in clinical settings: statistical significance alone does not justify inclusion when biological plausibility is absent.

</details>

### 6. `prep-flowchart-link`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/prep-flowchart-link.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Data Preprocessing
- **Project Brain:** `dataset`
- **Word Count:** 346

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (346 words)</summary>

> This element provides access to the complete preprocessing pipeline flowchart, and its presence here reflects a commitment to scientific reproducibility that deserves recognition. In clinical machine learning, the preprocessing pipeline is often where the most consequential decisions are made, yet it is frequently the least documented aspect of published studies. A model's reported AUC or accuracy is meaningless without understanding exactly how the data was prepared, what transformations were applied, and in what order operations were executed. This flowchart documents the entire journey from raw data to model-ready features. The pipeline begins with data ingestion, where the original clinical dataset was stored in Microsoft Excel Binary format, the .xlsb format, which is notably more challenging to parse programmatically than standard .xlsx files. The pyxlsb library was used to extract the Final Sheet, producing a raw CSV with two thousand four hundred and fifty rows and fifty-five columns. From there, the pipeline proceeds through column inventory, missingness quantification, dropping of columns with one hundred percent missing values, high-missingness flagging, missing value handling through kNN imputation and missingness indicators, free-text pathology NLP parsing, primary versus salvage classification, feature engineering including the testosterone-to-estradiol ratio, categorical encoding, RobustScaler normalization, SMOTE application, and comprehensive quality checks. What makes this documentation particularly important is its alignment with TRIPOD and PROBAST guidelines. TRIPOD requires transparent reporting of all data handling decisions, and PROBAST specifically evaluates whether preprocessing steps could introduce bias. By documenting the complete pipeline in a visual flowchart, this study allows reviewers, PhD committee members, and future researchers to scrutinize every decision point. Perhaps most critically, the flowchart makes explicit where data leakage prevention measures are enforced. The kNN imputation is fitted exclusively on training data within each cross-validation fold. The RobustScaler is similarly fit only on training data. SMOTE is applied only to training folds. These are not mere technical details; they are the difference between a valid model and one whose performance estimates are artificially inflated. The flowchart serves as a contract with the reader: this is exactly what was done, in this exact order, with these exact safeguards.

</details>

### 7. `prep-outcome-pie`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/prep-outcome-pie.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Data Preprocessing
- **Project Brain:** `dataset`
- **Word Count:** 408

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (408 words)</summary>

> This visualization presents the outcome distribution as a pie chart, and it represents what should always be the very first analytical step in any binary classification project: understanding your target variable. The chart displays the proportion of successful versus unsuccessful sperm retrieval outcomes in the cohort. With a thirty-six point seven percent success rate, we see that approximately thirty-seven out of every one hundred patients achieved successful sperm extraction during their micro-TESE procedure, while the remaining approximately fifty-four percent did not. This near-even but slightly imbalanced split has important ramifications that cascade through every subsequent modeling decision. From a statistical perspective, a roughly fifty-four to forty-six class split is what we might call moderately imbalanced. It is far less extreme than many clinical prediction tasks, where the event of interest might occur in only five or ten percent of cases, such as in rare disease diagnosis or surgical complication prediction. However, the imbalance is sufficient that naive models, those that do not account for the class distribution, would tend to favor predicting the majority class, which here is unsuccessful retrieval. This bias would be clinically dangerous because it would mean the model systematically underestimates the probability of success, potentially discouraging patients and clinicians from pursuing a procedure that might actually succeed. The study addressed this imbalance through SMOTE, Synthetic Minority Over-sampling Technique, which generates synthetic examples of the minority class by interpolating between existing minority samples in feature space. Critically, SMOTE was applied only within training folds of the nested cross-validation loop, never touching test or validation data. This fold-aware application is essential because applying SMOTE before splitting would allow synthetic copies of real patients to appear in both training and test sets, creating data leakage and inflating performance metrics. Beyond the modeling implications, this outcome distribution tells a clinical story. A thirty-six point seven percent success rate means that for every two patients who enter the operating room for micro-TESE, roughly one will leave with viable sperm that can be used for intracytoplasmic sperm injection and in vitro fertilization. The other will leave empty-handed, facing the emotional weight of confirmed surgical failure. This is the human reality behind every data point in this chart, and it is why accurate prediction models have such profound clinical value. If we can reliably identify which patients fall into the likely-to-succeed group before surgery, we can spare others the physical, emotional, and financial costs of a procedure unlikely to help them.

</details>

### 8. `prep-pathology-shap`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/prep-pathology-shap.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Data Preprocessing
- **Project Brain:** `dataset`
- **Word Count:** 397

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (397 words)</summary>

> This bar chart displays the SHAP importance values for the pathology features, and it opens a window into one of the most scientifically fascinating aspects of this study: how histological patterns in testicular tissue relate to sperm retrieval outcomes. SHAP, which stands for SHapley Additive exPlanations, is grounded in cooperative game theory. It assigns each feature a value representing its marginal contribution to a prediction, averaged across all possible feature coalitions. Unlike simpler importance measures like split counts in tree-based models, SHAP values provide a theoretically principled, consistent, and locally accurate decomposition of model predictions. In the version one LightGBM model, the top SHAP features were dominated by hormonal and demographic variables. LH led with a mean absolute SHAP value of zero point three one seven, followed by age at zero point two seven, FSH at zero point two five, and testosterone at zero point two one two. Body weight, Sakamoto testicular volume, BMI, and height followed. Notably, the pathology features in that version showed near-zero SHAP contributions, a finding that was ultimately traced to a critical data leakage bug in the IQR clipping pipeline that effectively zeroed out pathology values during preprocessing. This is why the version one LightGBM result with an AUC of zero point seven three two seven is considered invalid. The version two CatBoost pipeline corrected this error, and when pathology features were properly preserved and encoded using ordinal detailed encoding with raw values, the model AUC jumped to zero point eight three zero six. This dramatic improvement underscores just how much predictive information is embedded in testicular histopathology. The clinical interpretation of pathology feature importance aligns beautifully with reproductive biology. Hypospermatogenesis, which indicates reduced but still present sperm production, shows the highest success rates at approximately eighty-eight to ninety-one percent. This makes biological sense: if some degree of spermatogenesis is occurring, the surgeon has a high probability of finding tubules containing sperm. At the opposite end, Sertoli Cell Only syndrome, where the seminiferous tubules contain only supporting Sertoli cells with no germ cells, shows success rates of only about thirty-two percent. The maturation arrest features follow a gradient that mirrors the biological hierarchy: later-stage arrest at the elongated spermatid phase carries higher success rates than early-stage arrest at the spermatogonial phase, because later arrest means the germ cells progressed further through spermatogenesis before failing, leaving more mature cells potentially available for extraction.

</details>

### 9. `prep-missingness-chart`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/prep-missingness-chart.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Data Preprocessing
- **Project Brain:** `dataset`
- **Word Count:** 430

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (430 words)</summary>

> This bar chart visualizes one of the most important yet often underappreciated aspects of clinical data: the pattern of missing values across features. What you see here is not random noise or careless data collection. Rather, the missingness patterns tell a story about clinical practice, resource availability, and the evolution of diagnostic protocols over the fifteen-year data collection period at Royan Institute. Let us walk through the landscape of missingness from least to most affected. Age and race are complete at zero percent missing, which makes sense because these are recorded at the very first patient encounter. Outcome data for the primary surgery, Outcome1, is nearly complete with only one point five percent missing, representing thirty-seven patients who were excluded from the analytical cohort. The core hormonal panel shows a clinically meaningful pattern of missingness. FSH is missing in approximately twenty-two percent of cases, testosterone in about twenty-three percent, and LH in roughly twenty-five percent. These are not random absences. Hormonal assays require blood draws and laboratory processing, and in a busy clinical setting spanning over a decade, not every patient received every test before surgery. Prolactin and estradiol show even higher missingness at roughly forty and forty-two percent respectively, likely because these are considered secondary hormonal markers not routinely ordered for every NOA patient. The missingness escalates dramatically for specialized measurements. Karyotype analysis and Y chromosome microdeletion testing are missing in over sixty-two percent of cases, reflecting the fact that genetic testing was not universally available or recommended throughout the entire study period. BMI, height, and weight are missing in sixty-four to sixty-seven percent of cases, which may seem surprising but reflects the reality that detailed anthropometric measurements are not always prioritized in reproductive urology clinics. Testicular measurements from orchidometry and ultrasonography exceed seventy percent missingness, and surgical history variables like prior trauma reach over eighty percent. Nine columns were entirely empty and were dropped: cancer type, torsion of spermatic cord, cancer treatment, surgery therapeutic, sex hormone-binding globulin, TSH, inhibin B, AMH, and infertility length. The imputation strategy employed kNN, k-nearest neighbors imputation, which is particularly well-suited for this clinical data because it preserves the local correlation structure between related features. For instance, FSH and LH are biologically correlated through the hypothalamic-pituitary-gonadal axis, so imputing one from its neighbors in feature space leverages this natural covariation. Crucially, the kNN imputer was fitted exclusively on training data within each cross-validation fold, then applied to the test fold. This fold-aware imputation prevents data leakage, a mistake that would allow information from test patients to influence the imputed values of training patients.

</details>

### 10. `prep-cleaning-steps`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/prep-cleaning-steps.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Data Preprocessing
- **Project Brain:** `dataset`
- **Word Count:** 453

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (453 words)</summary>

> This visualization enumerates the ten data cleaning steps that transformed raw electronic medical records into a rigorous analytical dataset, and each step deserves careful attention because clinical data cleaning is where scientific rigor either lives or dies. Step one was data ingestion. The original clinical data was stored in an Excel Binary Workbook format with the .xlsb extension, which is significantly more compact but also more difficult to parse programmatically than standard Excel formats. The pyxlsb library was used to extract the sheet named Final Sheet, producing a raw CSV of two thousand four hundred and fifty rows and fifty-five columns. This seemingly mundane step is worth noting because data format choices can introduce subtle errors if not handled carefully. Step two involved creating a complete column inventory, documenting all fifty-five original feature names. This included preserving intentional and unintentional naming conventions, such as the misspelled column name Hieght (spelled H-I-E-G-H-T) and Boby Weight instead of Body Weight. These typos were preserved rather than corrected to maintain traceability back to the original clinical database. Step three quantified missingness across all columns, revealing that nine columns had one hundred percent missing values and forty columns exceeded fifty percent missingness. Step four dropped those nine completely empty columns, which included Cancer Type, Torsion of spermatic cord, Cancer Treatment, Surgery Therapeutic, Sex Hormone-Binding Globulin, TSH, inhibin B, AMH, and Infertility length. Step five flagged the forty high-missingness columns but retained those that were clinically meaningful, creating auxiliary binary missingness indicator features. This is a sophisticated approach because the fact that a value is missing can itself be informative. Step six handled missing values through multiple strategies: kNN imputation for continuous predictors fitted within cross-validation folds, mode imputation or an Unknown category for categorical variables, and dropping thirty-seven rows with missing primary outcomes to establish the analytical cohort of two thousand four hundred and thirteen patients. Step seven performed outcome integrity verification and cohort definition, confirming the distribution of one thousand five hundred and twenty-seven failures and eight hundred and eighty-six successes at thirty-six point seven percent in the full cohort. Step eight engineered the Primary versus Salvage classification by creating the is_Primary flag and computing Salvage_Count from the presence or absence of Outcome2, Outcome3, and Outcome4 columns. Step nine executed the free-text pathology NLP parsing pipeline, converting semi-structured pathology reports into sixteen quantitative bilateral features plus six aggregate features for a total of twenty-two histopathology variables. Step ten performed comprehensive data quality checks including row-count integrity verification, column-name normalization, data type coercion, outlier scanning, cross-field consistency checks, bilateral pathology consistency enforcement, maturation arrest hierarchy validation, and stratified outcome verification. Each of these ten steps is documented and reproducible, setting a standard for clinical data preparation rigor.

</details>

### 11. `prep-pathology-features-list`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/prep-pathology-features-list.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Data Preprocessing
- **Project Brain:** `dataset`
- **Word Count:** 434

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (434 words)</summary>

> This list presents the eighteen engineered features that were added to the original clinical dataset, and understanding each one requires a brief journey into testicular histopathology. The first sixteen features are pathology indicators, organized as eight bilateral pairs covering the right and left testes. Let us begin with SCO_RT and SCO_LT, representing Sertoli Cell Only syndrome for the right and left testes respectively. In Sertoli Cell Only, the seminiferous tubules contain only the supporting Sertoli cells with a complete absence of germ cells. This is among the most unfavorable histological findings, with success rates of approximately thirty-two percent for the right testis and twenty-seven percent for the left. The NLP parser extracted these from pathology reports containing phrases like SCO followed by optional percentage values. Next are CSTH_RT and CSTH_LT, standing for Complete Seminiferous Tubular Hyalinization. This represents the most severe form of testicular damage, where the tubules have undergone fibrotic replacement. The collagenous hyaline material replaces the normal tubular architecture entirely. Success rates for CSTH are approximately forty-three percent for the right testis and thirty-four percent for the left. The maturation arrest features follow the biological hierarchy of spermatogenesis. MA_Spermatogonial_RT and MA_Spermatogonial_LT represent arrest at the earliest stage, where spermatogonia, the stem cells of sperm production, fail to progress. MA_Spermatocytic_RT and MA_Spermatocytic_LT capture arrest at the spermatocyte stage, where cells have begun meiosis but fail to complete it. MA_ImmatureRound_RT and MA_ImmatureRound_LT represent arrest at the round spermatid phase, after meiosis has completed but before morphological maturation. MA_Elongated_RT and MA_Elongated_LT capture the latest arrest stage, where spermatids have begun elongating but fail to complete the final steps of spermiogenesis. The hierarchical logic enforced by the parser is crucial: if arrest is detected at the spermatogonial stage, all later stages are automatically set to zero, because biologically it is impossible for cells to reach the spermatocytic stage if they are arrested at the spermatogonial stage. This hierarchy progresses as spermatogonial, then spermatocytic, then immature round, then elongated, with later arrest correlating with progressively higher sperm retrieval success rates. Hypospermatogenesis_RT, Hypospermatogenesis_LT, Severe_Hypospermatogenesis_RT, and Severe_Hypospermatogenesis_LT capture the presence of reduced but ongoing spermatogenesis. Hypospermatogenesis is the strongest positive prognostic indicator, with success rates reaching eighty-eight to ninety-one percent. The distinction between hypospermatogenesis and its severe variant provides additional granularity. Finally, the two cohort flags complete the eighteen. is_Primary is a binary flag set to one for patients undergoing their first micro-TESE attempt and zero for salvage cases with prior failed procedures. Salvage_Count is an integer tracking the number of previous failed retrieval attempts, computed from the presence of Outcome2, Outcome3, and Outcome4 columns in the clinical database.

</details>

### 12. `prep-transformations-table`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/prep-transformations-table.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Data Preprocessing
- **Project Brain:** `dataset`
- **Word Count:** 423

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (423 words)</summary>

> This table documents the feature transformation strategies applied to convert raw clinical measurements into model-ready inputs, and the choices made here reflect deep understanding of both clinical data characteristics and machine learning algorithm requirements. The centerpiece transformation is RobustScaler, and its selection over the more commonly used StandardScaler or Z-score normalization deserves careful explanation. RobustScaler uses the median and interquartile range rather than the mean and standard deviation for centering and scaling. This choice is particularly well-suited for clinical data, where outliers are not rare aberrations to be suppressed but often represent genuine clinical extremes that carry important prognostic information. Consider a testosterone level of fifteen hundred nanograms per deciliter. Under Z-score normalization, this extreme value would heavily influence the mean and standard deviation calculations, distorting the scaling for all other patients. Under RobustScaler, the median and interquartile range are resistant to such extreme values, producing a more stable scaling that preserves the information content of outliers without letting them dominate the transformation. Interestingly, the original methodology documentation stated that Z-score normalization was used, but this was corrected post-defense when hyperparameter tuning logs confirmed that RobustScaler had actually been selected by the RandomizedSearchCV process. This correction exemplifies the importance of verifiable, reproducible pipelines over stated methodology. For categorical variables, the study employed one-hot and label encoding depending on the variable's nature. Race, habits, karyotype, Y chromosome microdeletion subtypes for AZFa, AZFb, and AZFc, and occupation exposure categories were encoded using these standard approaches. Binary flags for comorbidities like diabetes and hypertension and surgical history variables were kept as simple zero-one indicators. The pathology percentages extracted by the NLP parser were treated as continuous values on a zero to one hundred scale, preserving the quantitative information provided by pathologists. The engineered testosterone-to-estradiol ratio captures the hormonal balance relevant to spermatogenesis in a single derived feature. A critical design principle governed all transformations: leakage control. The RobustScaler was fit exclusively on training set data and then applied to test set data using the transform method. This means the median and IQR values used for scaling were derived only from patients the model had seen during training. If the scaler were fit on the entire dataset including test patients, information about the test distribution would leak into the training process, artificially inflating apparent model performance. The same principle applied to kNN imputation, fitted within each cross-validation fold's training partition, and to SMOTE for class imbalance correction, applied only to training folds. This triple firewall against data leakage across scaling, imputation, and oversampling represents methodological best practice.

</details>

### 13. `prep-primary-vs-salvage`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/prep-primary-vs-salvage.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Data Preprocessing
- **Project Brain:** `dataset`
- **Word Count:** 414

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (414 words)</summary>

> This visualization compares two fundamentally different clinical populations within the dataset: patients undergoing primary micro-TESE, their first attempt at surgical sperm retrieval, versus salvage micro-TESE patients who have undergone one or more previous attempts. Understanding this distinction is essential for interpreting the study's findings and appreciating the clinical complexity of non-obstructive azoospermia management. Primary micro-TESE patients constitute the vast majority of the cohort, approximately ninety-one percent, with two thousand two hundred and thirty-four cases in the full analytical dataset. These are men coming to surgical sperm retrieval for the first time, often after hormonal optimization and extensive diagnostic workup but without the surgical history of a prior testicular procedure. Their success rate for sperm retrieval in the full cohort was thirty-two point five percent, a sobering number that reflects the inherent difficulty of finding sperm in testes with fundamentally impaired spermatogenesis. Salvage micro-TESE patients represent a much smaller group, approximately eight point eight percent of the cohort with two hundred and sixteen cases. These patients have already undergone at least one prior micro-TESE and have returned for a repeat attempt. Their success rate is dramatically higher at eighty point five percent, nearly two and a half times the primary success rate. This stark difference might initially seem counterintuitive. Why would a repeat procedure be more successful than the first attempt? The answer lies in selection bias and clinical decision-making. Patients who return for salvage micro-TESE are not a random sample of all patients who failed their first procedure. They are a carefully selected subset, typically chosen because their first procedure showed some favorable findings, perhaps focal areas of spermatogenesis or borderline pathology suggesting that sperm might be present in untested regions of the testis. The engineering of the is_Primary flag was a deliberate analytical decision. This binary feature was derived by examining whether a patient had entries in the Outcome2, Outcome3, or Outcome4 columns. If these subsequent outcome columns contained data, the patient was classified as salvage with is_Primary set to zero. The complementary Salvage_Count variable counted the number of these prior procedures. From a modeling perspective, this distinction has important prognostic implications. Including the is_Primary flag as a feature allows the model to recognize that primary and salvage patients have fundamentally different baseline probabilities of success. The study's recommendation to develop separate models for primary and salvage cohorts in addition to a combined model reflects the recognition that these are, in many ways, distinct clinical populations with different underlying biology and selection pressures.

</details>

### 14. `prep-pipeline-flowchart`

- **Project Brain:** `dataset`
- **Word Count:** 474

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (474 words)</summary>

> This interactive flowchart presents the complete end-to-end preprocessing pipeline, and it serves as perhaps the most important piece of documentation in the entire study for anyone wishing to replicate, validate, or build upon this work. Let us walk through each stage systematically, from raw clinical data to model-ready feature matrices. The pipeline begins with data ingestion, where the original .xlsb workbook containing electronic medical records from Royan Institute is parsed using the pyxlsb library to extract a raw CSV of two thousand four hundred and fifty rows and fifty-five columns. This is followed by column inventory, where all fifty-five original feature names are documented, including preserved misspellings like Hieght and Boby Weight that maintain traceability to the source database. The missingness quantification stage then computes per-column missing rates, revealing the clinical data landscape: nine columns at one hundred percent missing, forty columns exceeding fifty percent, and only age and race at zero percent. Those nine completely empty columns, including cancer type, torsion of spermatic cord, cancer treatment, SHBG, TSH, inhibin B, AMH, and infertility length, are dropped. High-missingness columns above fifty percent are flagged but retained when clinically meaningful, with auxiliary binary missingness indicator features created to capture the potentially informative signal in the missing-versus-observed pattern. The missing value handling stage applies multiple strategies. kNN imputation addresses continuous predictors like hormonal levels and testicular volumes, leveraging the natural correlation structure among related clinical measurements. Mode imputation or an Unknown category handles categorical variables. Thirty-seven rows with missing primary outcome values are dropped, establishing the analytical cohort. The free-text pathology NLP parsing stage is where the custom regex pipeline transforms semi-structured pathology reports into sixteen quantitative bilateral pathology indicators, enforcing the hierarchical maturation arrest logic and extracting percentage values where provided by pathologists. The primary versus salvage classification stage engineers the is_Primary flag and Salvage_Count variable from the multi-surgery outcome columns. Feature engineering adds the testosterone-to-estradiol ratio and computes pathology per-side means and binary aggregate features. Categorical encoding applies one-hot or label encoding as appropriate. What makes this flowchart especially valuable is its explicit visualization of data leakage prevention. The RobustScaler normalization, kNN imputation, and SMOTE oversampling are all depicted as occurring within the cross-validation fold structure. This fold-aware preprocessing is the methodological cornerstone that ensures the reported AUC of zero point eight three zero six from the version two CatBoost model is a genuine estimate of out-of-sample performance, not an artifact of information leakage. The final quality checks stage performs row-count integrity verification, data type coercion, outlier scanning, bilateral pathology consistency checks, maturation arrest hierarchy enforcement, and stratified outcome verification. The study employed five-by-five nested cross-validation with Bayesian hyperparameter optimization, meaning five outer folds each containing five inner folds, testing sixteen different model architectures. This entire preprocessing pipeline was executed within each fold, ensuring complete separation between training and evaluation data at every stage.

</details>

### 15. `train-stat-card-models-tested`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/train-stat-card-models-tested.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Training & Model Building
- **Project Brain:** `methodology`
- **Word Count:** 424

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (424 words)</summary>

> This visualization highlights a number that speaks directly to the scientific rigor of this study: sixteen models tested. Now, in the landscape of clinical machine learning research, the typical approach involves selecting two or perhaps three algorithms, training them on the available data, and reporting whichever performs best. That approach, while common, carries a fundamental flaw. It assumes the researcher already knows which algorithm is best suited for their particular data characteristics, and that assumption is rarely justified. This study took a categorically different approach. The research team systematically evaluated sixteen distinct machine learning architectures spanning six major algorithmic families. Consider the breadth of this comparison. The gradient boosting family alone contributed four contenders: CatBoost, XGBoost, LightGBM, and classical Gradient Boosting Machines. Each of these implements the boosting paradigm differently. CatBoost uses ordered boosting with symmetric trees, XGBoost employs regularized gradient boosting with second-order Taylor approximation, LightGBM introduces leaf-wise growth with gradient-based one-side sampling, and classical Gradient Boosting uses the original Friedman formulation. Beyond boosting, the comparison included ensemble bagging methods with Random Forest, meta-ensemble strategies including Stacking and Voting ensembles, a neural network approach through multi-layer perceptrons, and a suite of linear models encompassing Logistic Regression, Ridge classification, Elastic Net, and Lasso with L1 regularization. Kernel and instance-based methods were represented by Support Vector Machines with radial basis function kernels and K-Nearest Neighbors. Even the probabilistic approach of Naive Bayes was included, along with a single Decision Tree as a baseline interpretable model. What makes this particularly noteworthy is how this systematic comparison aligns with the published research methodology. Rather than defaulting to a popular algorithm because it worked well in a similar study, the team treated algorithm selection as an empirical question to be answered by data. Each of the sixteen models was evaluated under identical conditions using five-by-five nested cross-validation with Bayesian hyperparameter optimization, ensuring that the comparison was fair and that no model benefited from preferential tuning. The results revealed meaningful performance stratification. The gradient boosting family consistently occupied the top ranks, but the specific ranking within that family was not predictable a priori. CatBoost ultimately emerged as the champion with an AUC of zero point eight three zero six, but this outcome could only be discovered through exhaustive comparison. Had the team tested only two or three models, they might have selected a suboptimal algorithm and never known it. This thoroughness is what transforms a competent machine learning study into a rigorous scientific contribution, and it sets a methodological standard that future micro-TESE prediction studies should aspire to match.

</details>

### 16. `train-stat-card-finalized`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/train-stat-card-finalized.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Training & Model Building
- **Project Brain:** `methodology`
- **Word Count:** 420

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (420 words)</summary>

> This card announces that the version two CatBoost model has been finalized, and understanding what finalization means in a machine learning pipeline context is essential for appreciating the scientific maturity of this work. Finalization is not simply selecting the model with the highest AUC and declaring victory. It is a deliberate, multi-step process that transforms a candidate algorithm into a production-ready prediction system. The journey to finalization began with the systematic comparison of sixteen models, each evaluated through five-by-five nested cross-validation. CatBoost emerged from this process with the highest AUC of zero point eight three zero six, but emergence alone does not constitute finalization. The next critical step was hyperparameter locking through Bayesian optimization. Unlike grid search or random search, Bayesian optimization uses a probabilistic surrogate model to intelligently explore the hyperparameter space, focusing computational resources on the most promising regions. For CatBoost, this process identified the optimal configuration: a tree depth of six, six hundred and fifty-seven boosting iterations, an L2 leaf regularization of three point five one, and a learning rate of zero point zero zero six one seven. These are not arbitrary numbers. Each hyperparameter was selected because it produced the best performance on held-out validation data within the inner folds of the nested cross-validation loop. Notice why CatBoost specifically excels with clinical tabular data of this nature. CatBoost implements ordered boosting, a technique that processes training examples in a random permutation order to prevent target leakage during the boosting process. This is particularly important for small to moderate clinical datasets like this two thousand four hundred and thirteen patient cohort, where overfitting is a constant threat. CatBoost also uses symmetric decision trees, where every node at the same depth uses the same splitting feature and threshold. This architectural choice acts as an implicit regularizer, reducing variance at the cost of slightly increased bias, a trade-off that favors generalization in clinical settings. Perhaps most importantly for clinical data, CatBoost handles categorical variables natively without requiring one-hot encoding, using an ordered target statistic that avoids the information leakage inherent in standard target encoding. For the pathology features in this study, the ordinal detailed encoding with raw values was selected as the optimal pathology encoding strategy. Finalization also means that the preprocessing pipeline is locked: RobustScaler for continuous features, kNN imputation within folds, and SMOTE applied exclusively to training data. Every component of the pipeline, from data transformation to model architecture, has been validated and fixed. The finalized model is the one whose predictions you see throughout this dashboard.

</details>

### 17. `train-stat-card-best-model`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/train-stat-card-best-model.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Training & Model Building
- **Project Brain:** `methodology`
- **Word Count:** 433

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (433 words)</summary>

> CatBoost. This name identifies the algorithm that outperformed fifteen competitors to emerge as the best-performing model for predicting micro-TESE sperm retrieval outcomes, and understanding its architectural innovations helps explain why it succeeded where others fell short. CatBoost, short for Categorical Boosting, was developed by Yandex researchers and introduced several theoretical advances that prove particularly advantageous for clinical tabular data. The first and perhaps most important innovation is ordered boosting. Traditional gradient boosting algorithms compute residuals using the same data points that were used to build earlier trees, which introduces a subtle but real form of target leakage known as prediction shift. CatBoost addresses this by maintaining a random permutation of the training data and computing residuals for each example using only the examples that precede it in that permutation. This ordered processing prevents the model from inadvertently memorizing training labels, which is especially critical when working with a cohort of two thousand four hundred and thirteen patients where every data point matters and overfitting lurks around every corner. The second innovation is symmetric decision trees. Unlike XGBoost and LightGBM, which grow asymmetric trees optimized for individual splits, CatBoost constructs oblivious decision trees where all nodes at the same depth share the same splitting criterion. This constraint dramatically reduces the effective complexity of each tree, acting as a powerful regularizer. For clinical data with mixed variable types and complex interactions, this regularization helps the model capture genuine biological patterns rather than noise. The third innovation is native categorical feature handling. Clinical datasets inevitably contain categorical variables such as race, karyotype classification, Y chromosome microdeletion subtypes, and occupational exposure categories. Most algorithms require these to be one-hot encoded, which can create sparse, high-dimensional representations that degrade performance on small datasets. CatBoost uses ordered target statistics to encode categoricals directly, preserving information without dimensionality explosion. For this study's fifty-eight features, including the eighteen engineered pathology indicators, CatBoost's ability to handle mixed data types natively was a significant advantage. The combination of these properties explains the performance gap. CatBoost achieved an AUC of zero point eight three zero six compared to zero point eight two one four for XGBoost and zero point eight two one three for LightGBM. While these differences may appear small numerically, in clinical prediction where every percentage point of discrimination translates to better patient counseling, they are meaningful. The gap between CatBoost and the weaker models was far more dramatic, with Naive Bayes at zero point five two three and SVM at zero point five four seven, essentially performing near chance level, underscoring that algorithm selection genuinely matters for this clinical prediction task.

</details>

### 18. `train-stat-card-best-auc`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/train-stat-card-best-auc.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Training & Model Building
- **Project Brain:** `methodology`
- **Word Count:** 479

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (479 words)</summary>

> Zero point eight three zero six. This is the area under the receiver operating characteristic curve for the version two CatBoost model, and it represents the single most important discrimination metric in this entire study. Let us unpack what this number means both statistically and clinically. The AUC, or area under the ROC curve, has an elegant intuitive interpretation. It represents the probability that the model, given a randomly selected patient who did achieve successful sperm retrieval and a randomly selected patient who did not, will correctly assign a higher predicted probability to the successful case. An AUC of zero point eight three zero six means the model gets this ranking correct approximately eighty-three percent of the time. For context, an AUC of zero point five represents random guessing, equivalent to flipping a coin, while an AUC of one point zero represents perfect discrimination. In the clinical machine learning literature, an AUC between zero point seven and zero point eight is generally considered acceptable, between zero point eight and zero point nine is considered good, and above zero point nine is excellent. Our value of zero point eight three zero six falls solidly in the good range, which for a complex surgical outcome prediction with this degree of biological variability is a meaningful achievement. The ninety-five percent confidence interval of zero point eight two three to zero point eight four five deserves particular attention. This interval was derived through bootstrap resampling and tells us that if we were to repeat this entire study on different samples drawn from the same patient population, ninety-five percent of the resulting AUC estimates would fall within this narrow range. The width of this interval, only zero point zero two two, indicates remarkable stability in the performance estimate. Now, consider for a moment the contrast with the previous version one LightGBM model, which reported an AUC of zero point seven three two seven. That earlier result was ultimately invalidated due to data leakage in the preprocessing pipeline, specifically an IQR clipping bug that zeroed out pathology features and improper fold-boundary handling. The version two CatBoost pipeline corrected all of these issues with fold-aware preprocessing, proper SMOTE application within training folds only, and correct pathology encoding. The improvement from zero point seven three two seven to zero point eight three zero six, a gain of nearly ten percentage points in AUC, reflects not just a better algorithm but a fundamentally more rigorous methodology. When we compare this AUC against published benchmarks in micro-TESE prediction, the results are encouraging. Most published studies report AUCs in the range of zero point six five to zero point seven eight. The present study's zero point eight three zero six exceeds these benchmarks while using what is arguably the most rigorous validation methodology of any study in this space: five-by-five nested cross-validation that completely separates model selection from performance estimation.

</details>

### 19. `train-model-roster`

- **Project Brain:** `methodology`

<summary>📜 Full Script (411 words)</summary>

> This visualization presents the complete roster of sixteen machine learning models evaluated in this study, arranged to illustrate the diversity of algorithmic approaches brought to bear on the micro-TESE prediction problem. Think of this as a tournament bracket where each competitor brings fundamentally different strengths to the challenge of predicting surgical outcomes from clinical and pathological data. The gradient boosting family fields four contestants. CatBoost, our eventual champion, brings ordered boosting and symmetric trees. XGBoost contributes regularized boosting with second-order gradient approximations, a technique that made it the dominant algorithm in data science competitions for years. LightGBM offers leaf-wise tree growth with histogram-based splitting, optimized for speed on larger datasets. Classical Gradient Boosting represents the foundational Friedman formulation that spawned the entire boosting renaissance. The ensemble methods category includes Random Forest, which constructs hundreds of decorrelated decision trees through bootstrap aggregation and random feature subsampling. Stacking Ensemble takes a meta-learning approach, training a second-level model on the predictions of multiple base learners. Voting Ensemble combines predictions through weighted averaging, leveraging the wisdom of crowds. The linear model family contributes four members. Logistic Regression, the workhorse of clinical prediction, models the log-odds of success as a linear combination of features. Ridge classification adds L2 regularization to prevent coefficient inflation. Elastic Net combines both L1 and L2 penalties, performing simultaneous feature selection and regularization. Lasso applies pure L1 regularization for aggressive feature selection. The neural network representative is the multi-layer perceptron, or MLP, which uses interconnected layers of artificial neurons with nonlinear activation functions to learn complex feature interactions. TabNet, a more recent neural architecture specifically designed for tabular data, was also considered in the broader methodology. Instance-based and kernel methods contribute K-Nearest Neighbors, which classifies patients based on the outcomes of their most similar peers in feature space, and Support Vector Machines with radial basis function kernels, which find optimal decision boundaries in high-dimensional space. The probabilistic approach is represented by Naive Bayes, which applies Bayes' theorem with a strong assumption of feature independence. Finally, a single Decision Tree serves as the interpretable baseline, providing a transparent, rule-based classification that clinicians can follow without any black-box machinery. Notice how this roster spans the full spectrum from simple, interpretable models to complex ensemble methods. This diversity is deliberate. The question being answered is not whether machine learning can predict micro-TESE outcomes, but which class of algorithms is best suited for this specific combination of clinical features, pathology data, sample size, and outcome distribution.

</details>

Consider the top of the leaderboard. CatBoost leads with an AUC of zero point eight three zero six, followed closely by XGBoost at zero point eight two one four and LightGBM at zero point eight two one three. Random Forest sits at zero point eight one five seven, and Logistic Regression rounds out the top five at zero point seven nine two six. The gradient boosting variants clearly dominate, occupying the top four positions, which tells us that the sequential error-correction mechanism inherent in boosting is particularly well suited to the patterns in this clinical dataset. But AUC alone is insufficient for clinical decision-making. Look at sensitivity and specificity, which together reveal the model's behavior at specific operating points. CatBoost achieves a sensitivity of zero point six seven eight four, meaning it correctly identifies approximately sixty-eight percent of patients who will have successful sperm retrieval. Its specificity of zero point eight two two means it correctly identifies about eighty-two percent of patients who will not have successful retrieval. This asymmetry is clinically meaningful. The model is slightly better at ruling out success than ruling it in, which in a surgical decision context means it is somewhat conservative. A clinician using this model would miss about thirty-two percent of patients who could benefit from micro-TESE but would rarely recommend the procedure to patients who are unlikely to benefit. The F1 score of zero point six eight three two provides a harmonic mean of precision and sensitivity, offering a single number that balances false positives and false negatives. What makes this particularly noteworthy is the gap between the top performers and the bottom of the table. Naive Bayes at an AUC of zero point five two three is barely better than random, and SVM with a radial basis function kernel at zero point five four seven fares only slightly better. These failures are instructive. Naive Bayes assumes feature independence, an assumption that is spectacularly violated in clinical data where FSH, LH, and testosterone are biologically correlated through the hypothalamic-pituitary-gonadal axis. The SVM struggled likely because of the mixed feature types and the relatively small sample size of two thousand four hundred and thirteen patients, which limits the kernel's ability to find meaningful support vectors in high-dimensional space. The comparison table also reveals that simpler linear models performed respectably. Logistic Regression achieved an AUC of zero point seven nine two six, demonstrating that the relationship between clinical features and micro-TESE outcomes has a substantial linear component. The boosting models earn their advantage by capturing the additional nonlinear interactions and threshold effects that linear models cannot represent.
