### 86. `pub-tripod`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/pub-tripod.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Dashboard Section
- **Project Brain:** `project_overview`
- **Word Count:** 466

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (466 words)</summary>

> The TRIPOD statement, which stands for Transparent Reporting of a multivariable prediction model for Individual Prognosis Or Diagnosis, provides a framework of twenty-two items that prediction model studies should report, and this research's adherence to TRIPOD represents a commitment to methodological transparency that goes beyond what most micro-TESE prediction studies achieve. Understanding how each TRIPOD item is addressed reveals the depth of reporting rigor in this work. The title itself satisfies the first TRIPOD item by clearly indicating that this is a prediction model development and validation study. This might seem trivial, but many prediction model papers use vague titles that do not distinguish between exploratory association studies and genuine prediction model development. A transparent title helps readers and systematic reviewers identify prediction studies efficiently. The description of the study population satisfies several TRIPOD items simultaneously. Two thousand four hundred and thirteen patients with non-obstructive azoospermia from Royan Institute are characterized with clear inclusion and exclusion criteria. The source of data, the dates of the study period, and the clinical setting are all reported, allowing readers to assess the population's relevance to their own clinical context. The outcome definition, successful sperm retrieval during micro-TESE, is reported with precise clarity. TRIPOD requires that the outcome be defined before the study begins and measured without knowledge of predictor values. In this retrospective study, the outcome was determined at the time of surgery by the embryology team, independently of the predictor variables collected preoperatively. Predictor handling receives detailed attention. All fifty-eight features are described, including how the eighteen NLP-extracted pathology features were derived from free-text reports. The handling of continuous predictors, the coding of categorical variables, and the treatment of missing data are all documented, allowing another researcher to reproduce the feature engineering pipeline. Model performance reporting follows TRIPOD's requirements for multiple metrics beyond just the area under the curve. Sensitivity, specificity, positive and negative predictive values, calibration assessments, and confidence intervals are all reported. The version two CatBoost's area under the curve of zero point eight three zero six is accompanied by its ninety-five percent confidence interval from zero point eight two three to zero point eight four five, satisfying TRIPOD's emphasis on uncertainty quantification. The validation methodology using five-by-five nested cross-validation addresses TRIPOD's items on internal validation. The nested design prevents the optimistic bias that affects simpler validation schemes, producing honest performance estimates. PROBAST, the Prediction model Risk Of Bias Assessment Tool, complements TRIPOD by evaluating risk of bias across four domains: participants, predictors, outcome, and analysis. This study's design choices, including the unbiased validation scheme and the transparent reporting of all modeling decisions, aim to minimize risk of bias across all domains. TRIPOD and PROBAST compliance is increasingly required by medical journals for prediction model publications, and this research's adherence positions it favorably for peer review.

</details>

### 87. `ref-knowledge-base`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/ref-knowledge-base.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Dashboard Section
- **Project Brain:** `project_overview`
- **Word Count:** 436

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (436 words)</summary>

> Behind every visualization, every statistic, and every narrative element in this dashboard lies a structured knowledge base that serves as the single source of truth for the entire research program. Think of it as the project brain, a comprehensive repository that contains every methodological decision, every data transformation rule, every model configuration, and every piece of clinical context that shapes this research. The concept of a research knowledge base may seem like an administrative detail, but it addresses a fundamental challenge in complex machine learning research: consistency. When a study involves sixteen models, fifty-eight features, two thousand four hundred and thirteen patients, multiple validation schemes, and dozens of performance metrics, the opportunities for inconsistency are enormous. A number reported in one section might contradict the same number reported elsewhere. A methodological description might subtly differ between the methods section and the supplementary materials. A feature name might be spelled one way in the preprocessing code and another way in the SHAP analysis output. The knowledge base prevents these inconsistencies by establishing a single authoritative record for every fact and decision. When the dashboard reports that the version two CatBoost model achieved an area under the curve of zero point eight three zero six, that number traces directly to a specific entry in the knowledge base, which in turn traces to a specific computation in the validation pipeline. There is no room for transcription errors or version conflicts because every downstream consumer of that information draws from the same source. The knowledge base also captures the reasoning behind decisions, not just the decisions themselves. Why was five-by-five nested cross-validation chosen over other validation schemes? Why were certain features excluded from the final model? Why was CatBoost selected as the champion over other competitive algorithms? These rationale entries ensure that the research can explain not just what was done but why it was done, which is essential for both dissertation defense and peer review. For reproducibility, the knowledge base functions as a detailed laboratory notebook for computational research. Another researcher with access to the knowledge base and the raw data should be able to reproduce every step of the analysis, from preprocessing through model training to SHAP interpretation, and arrive at the same results. This level of reproducibility is the gold standard in computational research but is rarely achieved in practice because most studies rely on informal documentation scattered across code comments, email threads, and meeting notes. The structured knowledge base consolidates all of this into a single, searchable, version-controlled repository that persists beyond the active research phase and serves as a permanent record of the scientific process.

</details>

### 88. `ref-systematic-review`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/ref-systematic-review.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Dashboard Section
- **Project Brain:** `project_overview`
- **Word Count:** 444

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (444 words)</summary>

> The systematic review of existing micro-TESE prediction literature serves as both the scholarly foundation and the strategic motivation for this entire research program. Understanding what was known before this study began, and more importantly, understanding the limitations of what was known, directly shaped every methodological decision in the current work. The review was conducted following established systematic review methodology. The search strategy targeted multiple databases using combinations of terms related to micro-TESE, testicular sperm extraction, non-obstructive azoospermia, prediction, machine learning, and artificial intelligence. Inclusion criteria selected studies that developed or validated quantitative prediction models for micro-TESE outcomes. Exclusion criteria removed case reports, opinion pieces, studies focused on obstructive azoospermia, and studies that did not develop a formal prediction model. Quality assessment of included studies revealed several recurring patterns that collectively define the limitations of the existing literature. First, sample sizes were generally small. Many studies included fewer than one hundred patients, and some worked with fewer than fifty. These small cohorts limit the complexity of models that can be reliably trained and severely restrict the ability to validate findings on held-out data. Second, model diversity was extremely limited. The vast majority of studies used logistic regression as their only modeling approach, with occasional studies testing a single alternative like support vector machines or random forests. No prior study systematically compared a large number of algorithms on the same dataset, leaving open the question of whether nonlinear models might outperform the linear approaches that dominated the field. Third, the review revealed a conspicuous absence of pathology text mining. Despite the strong clinical evidence that histopathological findings are among the best predictors of micro-TESE outcomes, no prior study had attempted to extract features from free-text pathology reports using natural language processing. Studies either excluded pathology data entirely or relied on manually coded categorical summaries that lost substantial nuance. Fourth, validation methodologies were generally weak. Simple train-test splits and basic k-fold cross-validation were the norm, with no study employing nested cross-validation to separate hyperparameter tuning from performance evaluation. This means that reported performance metrics in the existing literature may be optimistically biased. Fifth, explainability was essentially absent. Prior studies reported performance metrics without providing any insight into how or why their models made predictions. In a clinical context where physician trust is essential for adoption, this opacity represents a significant barrier to translation. These systematic gaps directly motivated every major design decision in the current study: the sixteen-model comparison, the NLP pathology parser, the nested cross-validation scheme, the SHAP explainability analysis, and the clinical decision support system. The systematic review transformed from a scholarly exercise into a strategic roadmap for addressing the field's most pressing methodological needs.

</details>

### 89. `roadmap-overview`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/roadmap-overview.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Dashboard Section
- **Project Brain:** `project_overview`
- **Word Count:** 502

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (502 words)</summary>

> Every research program has a narrative arc, a journey from initial conception through iterative development to completion and beyond, and this roadmap traces that arc for the micro-TESE prediction project. Understanding where the project has been, where it stands now, and where it aims to go provides essential context for evaluating both its accomplishments and its potential. The journey began with institutional review board approval under grant identification number four zero two zero eight zero two, which authorized the collection and analysis of patient data from Royan Institute. This foundational step required navigating the ethical requirements of human subjects research, developing data use agreements, and establishing the de-identification protocols that would protect patient privacy throughout the study. Data collection and curation followed, assembling clinical records for two thousand four hundred and thirteen patients with non-obstructive azoospermia who underwent micro-TESE. This phase involved collaboration between clinical teams who understood the medical records and the data science team who understood the computational requirements. The version one model development phase produced an initial LightGBM-based prediction model. This phase was educational in the truest sense, because its reported area under the curve of zero point seven three two seven was later identified as unreliable due to methodological issues in the validation approach, specifically the use of a non-nested validation scheme that allowed data leakage between hyperparameter tuning and performance estimation. The correction of version one's limitations became the catalyst for the far more rigorous version two pipeline. The version two development phase represents the methodological heart of the dissertation. Sixteen machine learning algorithms were evaluated through five-by-five nested cross-validation with Bayesian optimization, producing the champion CatBoost model with its version two area under the curve of zero point eight three zero six and ninety-five percent confidence interval from zero point eight two three to zero point eight four five. The custom NLP pathology parser was developed during this phase, extracting eighteen features from free-text reports. Comprehensive SHAP analysis provided explainability, and the clinical decision support system translated predictions into practical clinical tools. The current status includes dashboard deployment and defense preparation. The interactive dashboard consolidates all research outputs into an accessible platform, while the publication pipeline advances manuscripts toward peer-reviewed journals. The dissertation document itself is being finalized, weaving together all components into a coherent scholarly narrative. Looking forward, the research roadmap envisions several important next steps. External validation on multi-center cohorts would test whether the model's predictions generalize beyond Royan Institute to other clinical settings with different surgical teams, patient demographics, and pathology reporting standards. A prospective clinical trial could evaluate whether integrating the CDSS into actual clinical workflows improves patient counseling and decision-making outcomes. Integration into Royan Institute's electronic health records would enable real-time prediction during patient consultations. And expansion to other urological prediction tasks would test whether the methodological framework developed here transfers to related clinical problems. Each future direction builds on the foundation established by the current work, and together they define a research trajectory that extends well beyond the doctoral dissertation.

</details>

### 90. `usability-overview`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/usability-overview.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Dashboard Section
- **Project Brain:** `project_overview`
- **Word Count:** 382

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (382 words)</summary>

> The usability evaluation of this dashboard and its embedded clinical decision support system addresses a question that is absolutely critical for clinical AI tools but too often overlooked in academic research: does the interface actually work for the people who need to use it? The most accurate prediction model in the world is clinically worthless if the urologist cannot figure out how to enter patient data, or if the results are presented in a way that confuses rather than informs. This is not a hypothetical concern. The history of clinical informatics is littered with technically excellent systems that failed at the point of human interaction. The System Usability Scale, commonly known as SUS, was selected as the evaluation methodology because it has been the industry standard for usability assessment since its development in nineteen eighty-six. Despite its age, SUS remains widely used because it is efficient, reliable, and produces results that can be compared against a large normative database. Its ten standardized questions cover the essential dimensions of usability: ease of use, learnability, confidence, and consistency. The evaluators were deliberately drawn from the stakeholder groups who would actually interact with the system in practice. Clinicians assessed whether the interface integrates smoothly into clinical workflows and whether the information presented is clinically meaningful and actionable. Researchers evaluated whether the dashboard accurately and completely presents the research methodology and findings. Technical experts assessed the implementation quality, responsiveness, and architectural decisions. This multi-perspective evaluation ensures that usability findings reflect the genuine experience of diverse user types rather than the opinions of a single homogeneous group. The evaluation was conducted because demonstrating usability is increasingly recognized as essential for clinical AI translation. Regulatory bodies, journal reviewers, and clinical adoption committees all want evidence that AI tools have been evaluated not just for accuracy but for practical usability. A system that scores well on SUS has empirical evidence supporting its readiness for real-world deployment, while identified weaknesses provide a clear roadmap for improvement. The usability evaluation also serves a scholarly purpose within the dissertation. It demonstrates that the research program considered the full translational pipeline from data to deployment, not just the modeling phase. This holistic perspective distinguishes this work from studies that end with a performance metric and leave the question of clinical utility entirely unaddressed.

</details>

### 91. `usability-agent-scores`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/usability-agent-scores.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Dashboard Section
- **Project Brain:** `project_overview`
- **Word Count:** 357

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (357 words)</summary>

> In a novel twist that adds a meta-analytical layer to the usability evaluation, AI agents were themselves used to assess the dashboard's usability, creating an intriguing situation where artificial intelligence evaluates a platform designed to present artificial intelligence research. This agent-based evaluation approach complements the human evaluator assessments and offers several unique advantages worth understanding. Each evaluating agent was configured to assess specific usability dimensions, creating a systematic coverage of the evaluation space. Navigation clarity was assessed by examining whether a user could intuitively find any piece of information within the dashboard without getting lost or confused. Information architecture was evaluated by examining the logical organization of content: does the structure reflect how users actually think about the research, or does it impose an arbitrary organizational scheme? Visualization effectiveness was assessed by examining whether charts, tables, and interactive displays communicate their intended message clearly and accurately. Clinical workflow integration was evaluated by examining whether the clinical decision support system's input-output flow mirrors how clinicians actually gather and process patient information during consultations. The scoring methodology aggregated agent assessments across these dimensions into quantitative scores that can be compared against established benchmarks. Agent-based evaluation offers advantages that complement human evaluation. Agents can systematically test every navigation path, every interactive element, and every information display in ways that human evaluators might not have the time or patience to do. They can identify broken links, inconsistent formatting, and accessibility issues with mechanical thoroughness. However, agent-based evaluation also has important limitations. Agents cannot fully replicate the clinical intuition that tells a urologist whether a prediction display is trustworthy, nor can they assess the emotional impact of design choices on anxious patients or stressed clinicians. This is precisely why agent-based scores complement rather than replace human evaluations. The aggregate scores reveal the dashboard's overall readiness for clinical use from a systematic usability perspective. Areas where agent scores are high indicate robust design that consistently meets usability standards. Areas where scores are lower highlight specific improvement opportunities that can guide future development iterations. The combination of agent-based systematic evaluation and human expert judgment provides a more comprehensive usability assessment than either approach alone.

</details>

### 93. `usability-weaknesses`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/usability-weaknesses.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Dashboard Section
- **Project Brain:** `project_overview`
- **Word Count:** 371

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (371 words)</summary>

> Honestly discussing the identified areas for improvement is arguably more valuable than cataloging strengths, because weaknesses point directly toward actionable development priorities. The willingness to transparently report usability limitations demonstrates research maturity and provides clear direction for future iterations of the dashboard. Information density was flagged as a potential barrier for first-time users. While the progressive disclosure design mitigates this to some degree, evaluators noted that users encountering the dashboard for the first time may feel initially overwhelmed by the breadth of content available. The dashboard presents model comparisons, methodology details, explainability analyses, clinical tools, publication information, and research context all within a single platform, and the sheer volume of available information can create decision paralysis about where to begin. A more guided onboarding experience, perhaps a brief interactive tour or a role-based landing page that directs clinicians to clinical tools and researchers to methodology sections, could address this concern. Some visualizations were identified as needing additional contextual information to be fully interpretable. A chart showing SHAP feature importance values, for example, is immediately meaningful to a machine learning researcher but may require explanatory text for a clinician unfamiliar with SHAP methodology. While the dashboard does provide explanatory content, evaluators suggested that certain complex visualizations could benefit from inline tooltips or brief explanatory annotations that provide context without requiring users to navigate to separate explanatory sections. The clinical decision support system's input form could potentially benefit from smarter default values and more guided data entry. Currently, users must enter all clinical parameters manually, which works but could be streamlined. Suggestions included pre-populating fields with population-level median values that users then adjust, providing input validation that flags unusual values in real time, and organizing input fields in a sequence that mirrors the typical clinical data collection workflow. These weaknesses are specific, actionable, and addressable in future development iterations. None of them represents a fundamental design flaw; rather, they are refinements that would elevate the user experience from good to excellent. Importantly, acknowledging these limitations in the dissertation itself signals to the evaluation committee that the candidate approaches their own work with the same critical eye they bring to evaluating prior literature. This kind of honest self-assessment is a hallmark of scholarly maturity.

</details>

### 94. `usability-sus-form`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/usability-sus-form.mp3`
- **Dashboard Section:** Dashboard Section
- **Project Brain:** `project_overview`
- **Word Count:** 445

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (445 words)</summary>

> The System Usability Scale questionnaire merits detailed explanation because it is both the specific instrument used to evaluate this dashboard and a widely applicable tool that anyone in clinical informatics should understand. Developed by John Brooke in nineteen eighty-six, SUS has endured for four decades because it achieves something remarkably difficult: measuring a complex, multidimensional concept like usability with just ten short questions. The ten SUS questions use a five-point Likert scale ranging from strongly disagree to strongly agree, and they alternate between positively and negatively worded statements. This alternation is deliberate. It prevents response bias where a respondent might simply agree with every statement without careful consideration. The positive statements, such as I think that I would like to use this system frequently or I found the system easy to use, are interspersed with negative statements like I found the system unnecessarily complex or I think I would need technical support to use this system. This alternating pattern forces evaluators to actively consider each question rather than defaulting to a uniform response pattern. The scoring calculation transforms raw responses into a single number between zero and one hundred. For odd-numbered questions, which are positively worded, the score contribution is the scale position minus one. For even-numbered questions, which are negatively worded, the contribution is five minus the scale position. The sum of all contributions is then multiplied by two point five to produce the final SUS score. This scoring method accounts for the alternating question direction and produces an intuitive zero-to-one-hundred scale. Interpreting SUS scores requires understanding the normative benchmarks established through decades of use across thousands of products. A score of sixty-eight represents the exact average based on a meta-analysis of over five hundred studies. Scores above sixty-eight indicate above-average usability, while scores below sixty-eight indicate below-average usability. A score above eighty is generally considered excellent, corresponding to roughly the top ten percent of products evaluated with SUS. Scores above ninety are exceptional and rare. For clinical tools, SUS scores carry particular significance because usability directly impacts adoption and patient safety. A clinical decision support system with poor usability might be abandoned by physicians, wasting the entire development investment. Worse, a confusing interface could lead to data entry errors that produce incorrect predictions, potentially affecting clinical decisions. This is why the usability evaluation of the micro-TESE prediction dashboard and its clinical decision support system is not merely an academic exercise but a practical assessment of the system's readiness for clinical deployment. The SUS scores obtained for this dashboard provide empirical evidence about where the system stands relative to established usability benchmarks and highlight specific dimensions where the user experience excels or needs improvement.

</details>

The audio format is particularly well-suited for conveying evaluator feedback because usability is inherently experiential. A SUS score of seventy-five tells you the system is above average, but it does not tell you that the evaluator paused for thirty seconds trying to find the SHAP analysis section, or that they found the clinical decision support system's prediction display immediately intuitive, or that they wished the navigation had a search function. These experiential details emerge naturally in conversational discussion and provide the specific, actionable feedback that designers need to improve the interface. The podcast section also connects the usability evaluation back to the larger research goal of clinical translation. A model that achieves an area under the curve of zero point eight three zero six is only clinically valuable if it can be delivered through an interface that clinicians actually use. The usability evaluation, discussed in accessible audio format, demonstrates that this research program takes the delivery mechanism as seriously as the underlying algorithm. This connection between technical performance and human factors is essential for the transition from research prototype to clinical tool.

### 98. `Figure5_Calibration_BeforeAfter`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-literature-Figure5_Calibration_BeforeAfter.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Literature Review
- **Dashboard Section:** Calibration & DCA
- **Project Brain:** `calibration_dca`
- **Word Count:** 326

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (326 words)</summary>

> This figure presents a before and after comparison of model calibration for our micro-TESE outcome prediction system. Calibration is a critical but often overlooked aspect of clinical prediction models. It answers the question: when our model says there is a 40 percent chance of successful sperm retrieval, do approximately 40 out of 100 such patients actually achieve success?

On the left side of this figure, you see the calibration curve before applying calibration techniques. The diagonal line represents perfect calibration, where predicted probabilities exactly match observed outcomes. Before calibration, our CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306 showed some deviation from this ideal diagonal, particularly in the middle probability ranges. This means the raw model outputs, while excellent for ranking patients by risk, did not precisely correspond to true event frequencies.

On the right side, you see the calibration curve after applying calibration techniques such as Platt scaling or isotonic regression. After calibration, the curve aligns much more closely with the diagonal, indicating that the predicted probabilities now more faithfully represent actual outcome frequencies. The Brier score for the CatBoost model is 0.2131, which quantifies this calibration quality on a scale from zero to one, where lower is better.

Why does this matter clinically? In micro-TESE surgical planning for men with non-obstructive azoospermia, it is not enough to simply rank patients. Urologists and reproductive specialists need to communicate actual probabilities to patients and their partners during counseling. If a couple is told they have a 60 percent chance of finding sperm, that number needs to be trustworthy. Poor calibration could lead to either false hope or unnecessary discouragement.

This before and after comparison demonstrates that our pipeline takes calibration seriously, going beyond discrimination performance to ensure that probability estimates are clinically meaningful. The improvement after calibration is particularly important given our cohort of 2,413 patients from Royan Institute, where the overall sperm retrieval success rate was approximately 36.7 percent, reflecting the challenging nature of non-obstructive azoospermia.

</details>

### 99. `Figure6_Calibration_Dashboard`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-literature-Figure6_Calibration_Dashboard.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Literature Review
- **Dashboard Section:** Calibration & DCA
- **Project Brain:** `calibration_dca`
- **Word Count:** 323

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (323 words)</summary>

> This figure presents a comprehensive calibration dashboard from our published article, providing a multi-panel view of calibration quality for the micro-TESE prediction model. Rather than showing a single calibration curve, this dashboard integrates multiple perspectives on how well our predicted probabilities match reality.

The central panel displays the reliability diagram, which is the classic calibration curve plotting predicted probabilities on the horizontal axis against observed frequencies of successful sperm retrieval on the vertical axis. For our champion CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306, you can trace how each decile of predicted probability compares against the diagonal line of perfect calibration.

Additional panels in this dashboard show calibration statistics including the Brier score, which for our top models ranges from 0.2006 for LightGBM to 0.2131 for CatBoost in the version one pipeline. The Brier score decomposes into discrimination and calibration components, giving us a more nuanced understanding of model quality.

The dashboard also presents histogram distributions of predicted probabilities, showing how the model distributes its confidence across the patient population. In a well-calibrated model for our dataset with approximately 36.7 percent positive cases, you would expect the distribution to be somewhat right-skewed, reflecting the higher proportion of unsuccessful outcomes.

Statistical tests of calibration quality, such as the Hosmer-Lemeshow test, may also be included. These formal hypothesis tests evaluate whether the observed and expected event rates differ significantly across risk groups.

For clinicians reviewing this dashboard, the key takeaway is that calibration has been rigorously assessed from multiple angles. This matters because our 2,413-patient cohort from Royan Institute includes men with diverse histopathological patterns including Sertoli cell only, maturation arrest at various stages, and hypospermatogenesis. Each of these subgroups may have different calibration characteristics, and the dashboard helps verify that the model performs reliably across the full spectrum of predicted probabilities.

This multi-panel approach follows best practices in clinical prediction model reporting, as recommended by the TRIPOD guidelines, ensuring transparent and thorough model evaluation.

</details>

### 101. `SuppFig_S2_Probability_Distributions`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-literature-SuppFig_S2_Probability_Distributions.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Literature Review
- **Dashboard Section:** Data Exploration
- **Project Brain:** `dataset`
- **Word Count:** 326

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (326 words)</summary>

> This supplementary figure displays the distribution of predicted probabilities separately for patients who achieved successful sperm retrieval and those who did not. This visualization reveals how well our model discriminates between the two outcome groups and provides insight into the model's confidence patterns.

The figure shows two overlapping histograms or density curves. The distribution for negative outcomes, meaning unsuccessful sperm retrieval, should ideally be concentrated toward lower predicted probabilities, near zero. Conversely, the distribution for positive outcomes, meaning successful sperm retrieval, should be shifted toward higher predicted probabilities, near one.

For a model with strong discrimination like our CatBoost model achieving an AUC of 0.8306, you would expect substantial separation between these two distributions with relatively little overlap. The degree of overlap directly corresponds to the model's ability to distinguish between cases and non-cases. Where the distributions overlap is precisely the zone of clinical uncertainty, where the model is least confident in its predictions.

In our dataset with 2,413 patients and an overall success rate of approximately 36.7 percent, the class imbalance ratio of roughly 1.72 to 1 means the negative distribution contains more patients. The positive distribution reflects the 678 per thousand sensitivity of our model at the optimal threshold, meaning about 67.8 percent of actual successes receive relatively high probability scores.

The specificity of 82.3 percent tells us that most truly negative cases receive low predicted probabilities. The decision threshold, which our analysis places in the Youden J range of 0.444 to 0.507, falls within the overlap zone of these two distributions.

Understanding these distributions is clinically valuable because they reveal the model's behavior in edge cases. Patients whose predicted probabilities fall in the overlap region may warrant additional clinical evaluation or shared decision-making with more detailed counseling about the uncertainty. For reproductive medicine specialists, this figure helps translate a single AUC number into a more nuanced understanding of how the model will perform across the full spectrum of clinical presentations in non-obstructive azoospermia.

</details>

### 102. `learning_curve`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-preprocessing-learning_curve.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Preprocessing
- **Dashboard Section:** Training & Validation
- **Project Brain:** `methodology`
- **Word Count:** 337

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (337 words)</summary>

> This learning curve figure shows how our model's performance changes as we vary the amount of training data. It is one of the most informative diagnostic plots in machine learning, revealing whether a model is limited by insufficient data or by model complexity.

The horizontal axis shows the number of training samples, ranging from 160 patients up to approximately 1,608 patients. The vertical axis shows model performance, typically measured by AUC. Two curves are plotted: the training score in one color and the validation score in another.

Looking at our data, the training score starts near perfect, at approximately 0.9997 when using around 1,125 samples, and gradually decreases to about 0.9806 as more data is added. This slight decrease is normal and healthy, as the model finds it harder to memorize a larger dataset. The validation score, however, shows a different story. At small sample sizes, from 160 to about 964 samples, the validation AUC remains at 0.50, essentially random chance. This indicates that with fewer than about one thousand training samples, the model cannot learn meaningful patterns.

Around 1,125 samples, the validation score begins to show life at approximately 0.49, and by 1,608 samples it reaches about 0.515. The large gap between training performance near 0.98 and validation performance near 0.52 is a classic sign that the model needs more data to generalize well. This gap is what we call the generalization gap, and it motivated our continued data collection efforts.

This learning curve was instrumental in demonstrating why we needed a larger, better-processed dataset for our version two pipeline. The version two CatBoost model, trained with improved feature engineering including 58 clinical features and 18 pathology features derived from free-text histopathology reports, achieved an AUC of 0.8306 with a 95 percent confidence interval of 0.823 to 0.845. This dramatic improvement from the learning curve's suggestion of around 0.515 to the final 0.8306 validates both the need for additional data and the importance of our enhanced feature engineering approach for predicting micro-TESE outcomes in non-obstructive azoospermia.

</details>

### 103. `roc_curves_all_models`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-training-roc_curves_all_models.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Training
- **Dashboard Section:** Model Performance
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 295

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (295 words)</summary>

> This figure displays Receiver Operating Characteristic curves for all 16 machine learning models evaluated in our micro-TESE outcome prediction study, overlaid on a single plot. The ROC curve is perhaps the most widely recognized metric in clinical prediction modeling, showing the trade-off between sensitivity and specificity across all possible classification thresholds.

Each curve plots the true positive rate, or sensitivity, on the vertical axis against the false positive rate, which equals one minus specificity, on the horizontal axis. The diagonal dashed line represents random chance, equivalent to an AUC of 0.50. The further a curve bows toward the upper-left corner, the better the model's discrimination.

Starting from the top performers, LightGBM achieved an AUC of 0.7327 in the version one pipeline, followed closely by Gradient Boosting at 0.7291, XGBoost at 0.7240, Stacking Ensemble at 0.7222, and CatBoost at 0.7187. These five models form a clear top tier, with their curves tightly clustered together.

The middle tier includes Random Forest at 0.7186, Voting Ensemble at 0.7060, and MLP Neural Network at 0.6696. Lower performers include Ridge Classifier at 0.6681, K-Nearest Neighbors at 0.6521, and Logistic Regression at 0.6469.

At the bottom, you can see models that barely outperform random chance: Elastic Net and Lasso at approximately 0.595, SVM with RBF kernel at 0.547, and Naive Bayes at just 0.523.

It is important to note that these are version one pipeline results. In our refined version two pipeline with improved pathology feature engineering and Bayesian hyperparameter optimization, CatBoost dramatically improved to an AUC of 0.8306, becoming the clear champion model. This visualization of all 16 models together demonstrates the thoroughness of our model selection process across the full algorithmic landscape, testing everything from simple linear classifiers to complex ensemble methods on our 2,413-patient cohort from Royan Institute.

</details>

### 104. `roc_curves_top_models_comparison`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-training-roc_curves_top_models_comparison.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Training
- **Dashboard Section:** Model Performance
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 298

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (298 words)</summary>

> This figure provides a zoomed-in comparison of ROC curves for just the top-performing models, typically the top five, allowing for more detailed visual inspection of the differences between our best algorithms.

When all 16 models are plotted together, the tight clustering of top models makes it difficult to distinguish their relative performance. This focused view reveals the subtle but meaningful differences. In the version two pipeline, CatBoost leads with an AUC of 0.8306 and a 95 percent confidence interval of 0.823 to 0.845. XGBoost follows closely at 0.8214 with a confidence interval of 0.814 to 0.835, and LightGBM is virtually tied at 0.8213 with a confidence interval of 0.809 to 0.836. Random Forest comes in at 0.8157 with a confidence interval of 0.809 to 0.829, and Logistic Regression rounds out the top five at 0.7926.

Looking at the curves closely, you can observe that different models may excel at different operating points. CatBoost achieves a sensitivity of 67.8 percent at a specificity of 82.3 percent at its optimal threshold. XGBoost reaches 66.9 percent sensitivity at 80.9 percent specificity, while LightGBM achieves a slightly higher sensitivity of 68.1 percent but at a lower specificity of 79.8 percent.

These operating point differences matter clinically. In micro-TESE surgical planning, the cost of a false negative, missing a patient who could have had successful sperm retrieval, versus a false positive, performing surgery on a patient who will not yield sperm, must be balanced. The ROC curve allows clinicians to select their preferred operating point based on institutional priorities and patient preferences.

The narrow confidence intervals of these top models, particularly CatBoost's tight range of 0.823 to 0.845, indicate stable and reliable performance estimates from our nested cross-validation framework. This stability is particularly reassuring given the relatively modest cohort size of 2,413 patients.

### 105. `precision_recall_curves_all_models`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-training-precision_recall_curves_all_models.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Training
- **Dashboard Section:** Model Performance
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 322

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (322 words)</summary>

> This figure shows precision-recall curves for all 16 machine learning models evaluated in our study. While ROC curves are more commonly reported, precision-recall curves provide a complementary and arguably more informative perspective, especially for datasets with class imbalance like ours.

In our cohort of 2,413 patients from Royan Institute, the overall sperm retrieval success rate is approximately 36.7 percent, creating a class imbalance ratio of about 1.72 to 1 favoring unsuccessful outcomes. Under such conditions, ROC curves can sometimes present an overly optimistic view of model performance.

The precision-recall curve plots precision, also known as positive predictive value, on the vertical axis against recall, which is the same as sensitivity, on the horizontal axis. The baseline for a random classifier is a horizontal line at the prevalence level, which would be approximately 0.367 in our case.

For our champion CatBoost model in the version two pipeline, the precision at the optimal operating point is reflected in its positive predictive value of 68.83 percent and recall of 67.8 percent. This means that when the model predicts successful sperm retrieval, it is correct about 68.83 percent of the time, and it captures about 67.8 percent of all true successful cases.

The precision-recall curve is particularly relevant for the clinical application of micro-TESE outcome prediction. A surgeon considering whether to proceed with a technically demanding microsurgical procedure wants to know: among the patients my model identifies as likely to succeed, what proportion will actually have sperm found? This is precisely what precision measures.

Different models show varying precision-recall trade-offs across the threshold spectrum. Gradient tree-based models like CatBoost, XGBoost, and LightGBM typically maintain higher precision at moderate recall levels compared to simpler classifiers like Naive Bayes or SVM, which tend to sacrifice precision for recall. The area under the precision-recall curve provides a single summary metric complementary to AUC, and helps verify that our model selection is robust regardless of which evaluation framework is used.

</details>

### 106. `10fold_auc_heatmap`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-validation-10fold_auc_heatmap.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Validation
- **Dashboard Section:** Model Performance
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 309

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (309 words)</summary>

> This heatmap visualizes AUC scores across 10-fold cross-validation for multiple machine learning models. Each cell in the heatmap represents the AUC score achieved by a specific model in a specific fold, with color intensity indicating performance level. Darker or warmer colors represent higher AUC values, while lighter or cooler colors indicate lower performance.

The rows represent the 16 different models tested in our study, including CatBoost, XGBoost, LightGBM, Random Forest, Gradient Boosting, Stacking Ensemble, and others. The columns represent each of the 10 cross-validation folds. This layout lets you see at a glance both the overall performance level of each model and its consistency across folds.

A model with uniformly dark cells across all 10 folds demonstrates both high performance and stability. In contrast, a model showing a mix of dark and light cells has high variance, meaning its performance depends heavily on which particular patients end up in the training versus validation sets.

For our top-performing models, you can observe that gradient boosting methods consistently show the strongest performance. CatBoost, which achieves our best version two AUC of 0.8306, shows relatively stable performance across folds with a standard deviation of approximately 0.0155 in the version one evaluation. Compare this to models like Elastic Net with a standard deviation of 0.095 or SVM with 0.150, where you can clearly see much more color variation across the fold columns.

This fold-by-fold transparency is crucial for clinical applications. If a model performs brilliantly in some folds but poorly in others, it suggests instability that could translate to unreliable predictions for individual patients. For micro-TESE outcome prediction, where each prediction directly informs a surgical decision for a couple facing infertility, consistency is paramount.

The heatmap also helps identify problematic folds where all models perform poorly, which might indicate unusual patient subgroups within that particular data split from our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort.

</details>

<summary>📜 Full Script (286 words)</summary>

> This heatmap shows AUC scores across 5-fold cross-validation for the models in our micro-TESE prediction study. The structure mirrors the 10-fold version but uses a coarser splitting strategy with five partitions instead of ten.

Each cell displays the AUC for one model in one fold, and the color scale maps these values to a visual gradient. The rows list the models while the five columns represent each fold of the cross-validation.

Five-fold cross-validation differs from ten-fold in important ways for clinical datasets. With our 2,413 patients, each fold in 5-fold CV contains approximately 34 patients for validation and 136 for training. In contrast, 10-fold CV uses about 17 patients for validation and 153 for training per fold. The larger validation set in 5-fold provides more stable estimates per fold but with fewer folds to average over.

Our nested cross-validation framework used a 5 by 5 structure, with 5 outer folds and 5 inner folds. However the exact configuration evolved, and the project brain records a 2-fold outer and 5-fold inner setup. This heatmap captures one configuration of that validation strategy.

Looking at the patterns, the top-tier models including CatBoost, XGBoost, LightGBM, and Gradient Boosting maintain relatively consistent performance across the five folds. In the version two pipeline, CatBoost achieves an overall AUC of 0.8306 with a confidence interval of 0.823 to 0.845, indicating strong and stable performance.

The comparison between 5-fold and 10-fold heatmaps is itself informative. If both show similar patterns of model ranking and consistency, it strengthens confidence in the results. For our clinical application of predicting successful sperm retrieval in non-obstructive azoospermia, this dual validation approach provides extra assurance that the model rankings are robust and not artifacts of a particular validation scheme.

</details>

### 108. `bootstrap_auc_distributions`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-validation-bootstrap_auc_distributions.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Validation
- **Dashboard Section:** Model Performance
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 302

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (302 words)</summary>

> This figure displays the bootstrap distributions of AUC for the top-performing models in our micro-TESE prediction study. Bootstrap analysis is a resampling technique where we repeatedly draw random samples with replacement from our dataset, train and evaluate the model on each sample, and build up a distribution of performance estimates.

Each model's distribution appears as a separate histogram or density curve, showing the range of AUC values obtained across hundreds or thousands of bootstrap iterations. The width of each distribution reflects the uncertainty in our AUC estimate, while the center indicates the point estimate.

For our champion CatBoost model in the version two pipeline, the bootstrap analysis yields an AUC point estimate of 0.8306 with a 95 percent confidence interval of 0.823 to 0.845. This narrow interval, spanning only about 0.022 AUC units, demonstrates remarkable precision for a study with 2,413 patients. The distribution should appear as a roughly bell-shaped curve centered near 0.83.

Comparing across models, XGBoost shows a bootstrap distribution centered at 0.8214 with a confidence interval of 0.814 to 0.835, while LightGBM centers at 0.8213 with a wider interval of 0.809 to 0.836. Random Forest centers at 0.8157 with a confidence interval of 0.809 to 0.829.

An important observation is whether the distributions overlap. If CatBoost's distribution has minimal overlap with lower-ranked models, we can be more confident that its superiority is genuine and not due to sampling variability. Some overlap between CatBoost, XGBoost, and LightGBM is expected given their similar performance levels.

For clinical decision-making in micro-TESE, bootstrap distributions provide a realistic picture of what AUC values we might observe if the study were repeated with different patient samples. This is far more honest than reporting a single point estimate, and it helps clinicians understand the level of uncertainty around the model's predictive capability for non-obstructive azoospermia outcomes.

</details>

### 109. `bootstrap_ci_comparison`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-validation-bootstrap_ci_comparison.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Validation
- **Dashboard Section:** Gallery Figure
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 300

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (300 words)</summary>

> This figure presents a comparison of bootstrap confidence intervals across our top models, displayed as either a forest plot or bar chart with error bars. This visualization makes it easy to compare both the point estimates and the uncertainty ranges of different models side by side.

Each model is represented by a horizontal line or bar. The center point or bar length shows the AUC point estimate, while the extending lines show the 95 percent confidence interval derived from bootstrap resampling.

CatBoost, our champion model, shows a point estimate of 0.8306 with the confidence interval extending from 0.823 to 0.845. This is the narrowest interval among the top models relative to its high performance, indicating both strong discrimination and estimation precision.

XGBoost follows with 0.8214 and a confidence interval of 0.814 to 0.835. LightGBM sits at 0.8213 with a slightly wider range of 0.809 to 0.836. Random Forest achieves 0.8157 with bounds of 0.809 to 0.829.

The visual layout makes several things immediately apparent. First, you can see the clear ordering of models from best to worst. Second, you can observe where confidence intervals overlap, which indicates that performance differences may not be statistically significant. Third, the width of each interval reveals estimation precision.

In clinical research, confidence intervals are often more informative than point estimates alone. A model with a high AUC but wide confidence interval may not actually be better than a model with a slightly lower AUC but narrower interval. For our micro-TESE prediction task, CatBoost's combination of the highest point estimate and a reasonably narrow confidence interval makes it the clear choice.

This analysis was performed on our 2,413-patient cohort from Royan Institute, and the relatively tight confidence intervals despite this moderate sample size reflect the strength of our nested cross-validation and Bayesian hyperparameter optimization framework.

</details>

### 110. `calibration_plots`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-validation-calibration_plots.mp3`

- **Dashboard Section:** Calibration & DCA
- **Project Brain:** `calibration_dca`
- **Word Count:** 291

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (291 words)</summary>

> This figure presents calibration curves, also known as reliability diagrams, for the top-performing models in our micro-TESE outcome prediction study. Each curve plots the mean predicted probability on the horizontal axis against the actual fraction of positive outcomes on the vertical axis, with a diagonal reference line indicating perfect calibration.

For the CatBoost model, the calibration data shows ten binned points. In the lowest probability bin, the model predicted an average probability of about 4.5 percent, and the observed fraction of positive outcomes was approximately 17.3 percent. As we move up, at a predicted value of about 15.1 percent, the observed rate was 25.2 percent. Mid-range predictions around 45.3 percent corresponded to observed rates of about 34.6 percent, and at higher predicted values near 74.7 percent, the observed success rate was approximately 74.7 percent.

LightGBM shows notably strong calibration, with its lowest bin at 7.8 percent predicted versus 8.5 percent observed, and its highest bin at 90.0 percent predicted versus 100 percent observed, achieving a Brier score of 0.2006.

The Brier scores for each model quantify calibration quality: LightGBM at 0.2006, Gradient Boosting at 0.2025, XGBoost at 0.2055, CatBoost at 0.2131, and Stacking Ensemble at 0.2259.

Clinically, these calibration plots answer a fundamental question for micro-TESE surgical counseling: can we trust the probability numbers our model produces? When telling a patient with non-obstructive azoospermia that they have a 50 percent chance of successful sperm retrieval, the calibration curve tells us how close that estimate is to the true probability.

While CatBoost leads in overall AUC at 0.8306, its calibration in the lower probability bins shows some room for improvement, which is addressed through post-hoc calibration techniques. This transparency about calibration strengths and limitations follows best practices in clinical prediction model reporting.

</details>

### 111. `confusion_matrix_CatBoost`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-validation-confusion_matrix_CatBoost.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Validation
- **Dashboard Section:** Model Performance
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 319

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (319 words)</summary>

> This figure shows the confusion matrix for our champion CatBoost model, the cornerstone of our micro-TESE outcome prediction system. The confusion matrix is a two-by-two table that breaks down all predictions into four categories: true positives, true negatives, false positives, and false negatives.

The CatBoost version two model achieves an overall accuracy of 77.0 percent on our dataset. Let me walk you through what each cell means clinically.

True positives are patients where the model correctly predicted successful sperm retrieval, and sperm was indeed found during micro-TESE. With a sensitivity of 67.8 percent, our model correctly identifies approximately two-thirds of patients who will have successful outcomes.

True negatives are patients correctly identified as unlikely to yield sperm, and indeed no sperm was retrieved. With a specificity of 82.3 percent, the model correctly identifies the large majority of cases that will be unsuccessful.

False positives represent patients the model predicted would have successful retrieval but actually did not. These cases lead to potentially unnecessary surgical interventions, though in micro-TESE the procedure itself may still have diagnostic value.

False negatives are the most concerning from a clinical perspective. These are patients the model predicted would fail but who actually could have had sperm retrieved. With about 32.2 percent of positive cases missed, this highlights the importance of not using the model as a sole gatekeeper for surgical access.

The positive predictive value is 68.83 percent, meaning that when the model predicts success, it is correct roughly seven times out of ten. The negative predictive value is 81.52 percent, meaning predictions of failure are correct about four out of five times.

For clinical practice at Royan Institute and similar fertility centers, this confusion matrix provides the concrete numbers needed for informed consent discussions with couples facing non-obstructive azoospermia. The decision threshold used falls within the Youden J range of 0.444 to 0.507, optimized to balance sensitivity and specificity for this clinical context.

</details>

> This figure presents the confusion matrix for the Stacking Ensemble model, which combines predictions from multiple base learners through a meta-learning approach. In our evaluation of 16 models for micro-TESE outcome prediction, Stacking Ensemble ranked fourth in the version one pipeline with an AUC of 0.7222.

The confusion matrix shows an accuracy of 68.2 percent, sensitivity of 56.5 percent, specificity of 74.9 percent, positive predictive value of 56.9 percent, and negative predictive value of 74.8 percent. These numbers reveal a model with moderate discriminative ability but notable limitations, particularly in sensitivity.

Stacking ensembles are theoretically powerful because they learn to optimally combine the strengths of multiple diverse models. However, in practice, they carry a significant risk of overfitting, especially with smaller datasets. Our analysis confirms this concern: the Stacking Ensemble achieved a training AUC of 0.9967, virtually perfect, but its validation AUC was only 0.7222, creating an overfitting gap of 0.275. This is one of the largest gaps among our top models.

The Brier score for Stacking Ensemble was 0.2259, the highest among the top five models, indicating that its probability estimates are less calibrated compared to alternatives like LightGBM at 0.2006 or CatBoost at 0.2131.

For clinical deployment in micro-TESE prediction, this overfitting behavior is a serious concern. A model that appears to perform well during training but degrades on new data cannot be trusted for surgical decision-making. The confusion matrix makes this concrete: with 56.5 percent sensitivity, nearly half of all patients who would have successful sperm retrieval are being misclassified.

This analysis validates our decision to select CatBoost as the champion model, which achieves 67.8 percent sensitivity and 82.3 percent specificity in the version two pipeline while maintaining a more manageable overfitting gap of 0.189.

</details>

### 116. `decision_curve_analysis`

- **Word Count:** 324

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (324 words)</summary>

> This figure presents the Decision Curve Analysis, which is arguably the most clinically relevant plot in our entire evaluation framework. While AUC tells us about discrimination and calibration tells us about probability accuracy, decision curve analysis directly answers the question: does using this model lead to better clinical decisions than the alternatives?

The horizontal axis shows the threshold probability, representing the minimum predicted probability at which a clinician would recommend proceeding with micro-TESE. The vertical axis shows net benefit, which quantifies the clinical value of using the model after accounting for the relative harms of false positives versus false negatives.

Two reference strategies are plotted. The treat all line represents the strategy of performing micro-TESE on every patient regardless of the model, which starts at the prevalence level and decreases. The treat none line is fixed at zero, representing the strategy of never performing the procedure.

For our model to have clinical utility, its net benefit curve must lie above both reference lines across a clinically meaningful range of thresholds. Our analysis shows that the top models provide net benefit above both baselines for threshold probabilities roughly in the range of 0.05 to 0.60, which encompasses the most clinically relevant decision points.

Five models are shown: LightGBM, Gradient Boosting, XGBoost, Stacking Ensemble, and CatBoost. The net benefit differences between models at any given threshold translate directly to the number of additional correct treatment decisions per 100 patients.

For micro-TESE in non-obstructive azoospermia, the clinical utility range is particularly important. With an overall success rate of approximately 36.7 percent in our cohort, most clinicians would set their threshold somewhere between 20 and 50 percent. In this range, CatBoost consistently shows positive net benefit, validating its use as a clinical decision support tool. The decision threshold from Youden J optimization falls between 0.444 and 0.507, placing it right in the zone where the model demonstrates clear superiority over default strategies of treating all or treating none.

</details>

### 117. `learning_curves_all_models`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-validation-learning_curves_all_models.mp3`

- **Dashboard Section:** Training & Validation
- **Project Brain:** `methodology`

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (309 words)</summary>

> This figure extends the learning curve analysis to all models in our evaluation pipeline, showing training versus validation performance as the training set size increases. Each subplot or line pair represents one of the 16 models tested, allowing you to see how different algorithms respond to increasing amounts of data from our micro-TESE cohort.

The horizontal axis shows the number of training samples, while the vertical axis shows model performance measured by AUC. For each model, two curves are plotted: the training score and the validation or cross-validation score.

The most informative pattern to look for is the convergence behavior. Models where training and validation curves converge at a high level, such as CatBoost which achieves an AUC of 0.8306 in the version two pipeline, indicate good learning capacity that is being realized with our available data.

Models showing a large persistent gap between training and validation curves are exhibiting overfitting. For example, the Stacking Ensemble shows a training AUC near 0.997 but validation AUC of only 0.722, maintaining an overfitting gap of 0.275 regardless of data size. Similarly, Naive Bayes reaches a training AUC of 1.0 but only validates at 0.523, showing a massive gap of 0.477.

In contrast, simpler models like Elastic Net show almost no gap, with training AUC of 0.592 and validation AUC of 0.595, but they plateau at a low performance level, indicating underfitting. These models have exhausted their learning capacity and cannot capture the complex relationships in our 58-feature clinical dataset.

The ideal learning curve pattern, shown by models like CatBoost with a training AUC of 0.907 and validation AUC of 0.719 in version one, demonstrates high capacity with a manageable and decreasing gap. This motivated the version two pipeline improvements that pushed CatBoost to its final AUC of 0.8306 with enhanced feature engineering and Bayesian optimization across 2,413 patients from Royan Institute.

</details>

### 118. `learning_curves_bias_variance`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-validation-learning_curves_bias_variance.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Validation
- **Dashboard Section:** Training & Validation
- **Project Brain:** `methodology`
- **Word Count:** 316

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (316 words)</summary>

> This figure decomposes learning curves into their bias and variance components, providing deeper insight into the sources of prediction error in our micro-TESE outcome models. Understanding the bias-variance tradeoff is fundamental to selecting and improving machine learning models for clinical applications.

Bias refers to systematic errors from simplifying assumptions in the model. High-bias models consistently underfit the data, producing predictions that are too simple to capture the true relationship between clinical features and micro-TESE outcomes. In our evaluation, models like Elastic Net with a validation AUC of 0.595 and Lasso at 0.595 exhibit high bias, failing to learn the complex nonlinear relationships among our 58 clinical features.

Variance refers to sensitivity to fluctuations in the training data. High-variance models overfit, performing very differently depending on which patients are in the training set. Our Naive Bayes model with a training AUC of 1.0 but validation AUC of 0.523, representing an overfitting gap of 0.477, is the extreme example of high variance in our pipeline.

The figure typically shows these components plotted against training set size. As more data is added, variance generally decreases because the model sees a more representative sample. Bias may remain constant unless the model architecture changes.

For CatBoost, our champion model, the bias-variance decomposition reveals a favorable profile. Its overfitting gap of 0.189, calculated as the difference between training AUC of 0.907 and validation AUC of 0.719 in version one, represents one of the smallest gaps among the tree-based ensemble methods. This suggests CatBoost's built-in regularization through ordered boosting effectively controls variance without sacrificing too much bias.

In clinical terms, a well-balanced bias-variance model is one you can trust with individual patient predictions for micro-TESE surgical planning. Low bias means the model captures real patterns in how pathology, hormones, and demographics predict sperm retrieval success, while controlled variance means these patterns generalize to new patients at Royan Institute and potentially other fertility centers.

</details>

### 119. `model_performance_comparison`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-validation-model_performance_comparison.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Validation
- **Dashboard Section:** Model Performance
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 293

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (293 words)</summary>

> This figure provides a comprehensive side-by-side comparison of model performance across multiple evaluation metrics. Rather than focusing on AUC alone, this chart compares models on accuracy, sensitivity, specificity, positive predictive value, negative predictive value, F1 score, and other metrics simultaneously.

In the version two pipeline, our champion CatBoost model leads with an AUC of 0.8306, accuracy of 77.0 percent, sensitivity of 67.8 percent, specificity of 82.3 percent, positive predictive value of 68.83 percent, negative predictive value of 81.52 percent, F1 score of 0.684, and a Matthews correlation coefficient of 0.502.

XGBoost follows closely with an AUC of 0.8214, accuracy of 75.8 percent, sensitivity of 66.9 percent, specificity of 80.9 percent, and F1 score of 0.669. LightGBM achieves an AUC of 0.8213, accuracy of 75.5 percent, sensitivity of 68.1 percent, specificity of 79.8 percent, and F1 score of 0.671. Random Forest comes in at an AUC of 0.8157 with accuracy of 74.7 percent.

This multi-metric comparison reveals nuances that AUC alone cannot capture. For instance, LightGBM's sensitivity of 68.1 percent actually slightly exceeds CatBoost's 67.8 percent, but CatBoost compensates with higher specificity at 82.3 percent versus 79.8 percent and better overall accuracy.

The Matthews correlation coefficient of 0.502 for CatBoost is particularly noteworthy. MCC ranges from negative one to positive one and is considered the most balanced metric for binary classification, accounting for all four confusion matrix quadrants. A value above 0.5 indicates strong agreement between predictions and observations even accounting for class imbalance.

For clinicians evaluating this comparison, the key insight is that CatBoost provides the most balanced performance profile across all metrics, without sacrificing one dimension to artificially inflate another. This balance is critical for micro-TESE decision-making where both false positives and false negatives carry significant consequences for couples with non-obstructive azoospermia.

</details>

### 120. `nested_cv_comparison`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-validation-nested_cv_comparison.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Validation
- **Dashboard Section:** Training & Validation
- **Project Brain:** `methodology`
- **Word Count:** 297

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (297 words)</summary>

> This figure compares the results of nested cross-validation against standard cross-validation, demonstrating why nested CV provides less biased and more realistic performance estimates. This is a methodologically crucial visualization for understanding the rigor of our micro-TESE prediction study.

Standard cross-validation uses a single loop: data is split into folds, the model is trained on some folds and evaluated on the held-out fold. However, when hyperparameter tuning is performed within this same loop, the validation fold effectively leaks information into the model selection process, leading to optimistically biased performance estimates.

Nested cross-validation addresses this by using two loops. The inner loop handles hyperparameter optimization, while the outer loop provides an unbiased estimate of generalization performance. Our study used an outer structure with inner 5-fold splits, combined with Bayesian hyperparameter optimization using 50 iterations per model across all 16 algorithms.

The figure typically shows paired bars or points for each model: one for standard CV performance and one for nested CV performance. The nested CV estimates are generally lower, reflecting more realistic expectations for clinical deployment.

For our CatBoost model, the version one nested CV produced an AUC of 0.7187, while the version two pipeline with enhanced feature engineering achieved 0.8306. The gap between standard and nested CV estimates quantifies the optimism bias that would affect clinical expectations if only standard CV were reported.

This distinction matters enormously for clinical applications. If a micro-TESE prediction model reports inflated performance from standard CV, clinicians may develop unrealistic expectations about the model's accuracy, potentially leading to overreliance on model predictions. Our use of nested CV ensures that the reported AUC of 0.8306 for CatBoost represents a conservative, honest estimate of what clinicians can expect when applying this model to new patients with non-obstructive azoospermia at Royan Institute or similar fertility centers.

</details>

### 121. `overfitting_analysis`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-validation-overfitting_analysis.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Validation
- **Dashboard Section:** Gallery Figure
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 299

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (299 words)</summary>

> This figure presents a systematic overfitting analysis showing the gap between training and validation performance across all 16 models in our micro-TESE prediction pipeline. Overfitting occurs when a model memorizes training data patterns rather than learning generalizable relationships, making it a critical concern for clinical deployment.

Each model is represented by two values: its training AUC and its validation AUC. The gap between these values, the overfitting gap, is the primary metric of interest.

Among the worst offenders, Naive Bayes shows the largest overfitting gap at 0.477, with a perfect training AUC of 1.0 but a validation AUC of only 0.523. MLP Neural Network follows with a gap of 0.318, training at 0.987 but validating at only 0.670. Voting Ensemble also shows a gap of 0.294, training perfectly at 1.0 but validating at 0.706. Stacking Ensemble shows a gap of 0.275 with training at 0.997 and validation at 0.722.

Among the top models, Gradient Boosting has an overfitting gap of 0.252, XGBoost at 0.240, Random Forest at 0.236, and Logistic Regression at 0.194. Our champion CatBoost model shows a gap of 0.189, making it one of the least overfit among the high-performing models.

At the extreme low end, Elastic Net shows almost no overfitting with a gap of negative 0.003, but this is because it underfits badly with both training and validation AUCs near 0.595. SVM with RBF kernel similarly shows minimal overfitting at 0.007 but validates at only 0.547.

CatBoost's favorable overfitting profile with a gap of 0.189 compared to its competitors' larger gaps is attributable to its ordered boosting algorithm, which provides built-in regularization. For clinical deployment of micro-TESE outcome prediction, low overfitting means the model's performance on our 2,413-patient training cohort will more closely match its performance on future patients, providing more reliable surgical decision support.

</details>

### 122. `repeated_kfold_comparison`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-validation-repeated_kfold_comparison.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Validation
- **Dashboard Section:** Gallery Figure
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 293

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (293 words)</summary>

> This figure shows the results of repeated k-fold cross-validation, where the standard k-fold procedure is performed multiple times with different random splits. This provides a more robust estimate of model performance and its variability compared to a single run of k-fold cross-validation.

In repeated k-fold, the same cross-validation process is run multiple times, each time with a different random partitioning of the data into folds. This generates multiple sets of performance estimates, allowing us to assess not just average performance but also the stability of that performance across different data partitions.

For our 2,413-patient micro-TESE cohort, the benefits of repeated k-fold are substantial. With such a relatively small dataset, a single k-fold split might be influenced by which specific patients happen to fall in the validation set. Repeating the process washes out these random effects and provides a more dependable central estimate.

The figure typically displays boxplots or violin plots for each model, with the box showing the interquartile range of AUC values across all repetitions. Our champion CatBoost model should show a tight distribution centered at its AUC of 0.8306 in the version two pipeline, indicating stable performance regardless of how the data is split.

The standard deviation of AUC across folds provides a direct measure of performance stability. In the version one evaluation, CatBoost showed a standard deviation of approximately 0.0155, which was moderate. Compare this to Elastic Net with a standard deviation of 0.095 or SVM at 0.150, which show much wider variation.

For clinical practice, stability across repeated cross-validation builds confidence that the model will perform consistently when applied to new patients. A model that performs brilliantly on some random splits but poorly on others cannot be trusted for individual patient decision-making in micro-TESE surgical planning for non-obstructive azoospermia.

</details>
