<summary>📜 Full Script (280 words)</summary>

> This figure presents a robustness heatmap showing how model performance holds up under various perturbation conditions. Robustness testing goes beyond standard cross-validation to stress-test models under realistic scenarios that might occur in clinical practice.

Each cell in the heatmap represents a model's performance under a specific perturbation condition. The rows typically list the models being compared, while the columns represent different types of perturbations such as feature noise, missing data at various rates, sample size reduction, or feature removal.

The color gradient helps identify patterns at a glance. Models with uniformly warm or dark cells across all perturbation conditions are robust, meaning they maintain performance even when conditions are imperfect. Models with large color variations across columns are fragile, performing well under ideal conditions but degrading sharply when stressed.

For clinical applications of micro-TESE outcome prediction, robustness is essential because real-world clinical data is never as clean as research datasets. Missing laboratory values, measurement errors in hormone levels, inconsistent pathology reporting, and variations in imaging protocols are everyday realities at fertility centers.

Our champion CatBoost model, with its AUC of 0.8306 in the version two pipeline, should demonstrate relatively consistent performance across perturbation conditions compared to models like Stacking Ensemble or MLP that show higher overfitting gaps. CatBoost's built-in handling of categorical features and its ordered boosting algorithm provide inherent robustness to data imperfections.

The robustness heatmap is particularly relevant for potential multi-site deployment. If the model is ever to be used beyond Royan Institute, it needs to handle the data quality variations that come with different clinical workflows, different laboratory equipment, and different pathology reporting conventions across the 58 clinical features including 18 pathology indicators in our feature set.

</details>

The vertical axis shows the resulting model AUC. Each model's curve traces its performance trajectory from zero noise on the left to substantial noise on the right.

Testicular volume measurements differ between ultrasound and orchidometer methods. Pathology readings can vary between pathologists. All of these sources introduce noise into the clinical features.

If CatBoost maintains an AUC above 0.80 even with moderate noise levels, clinicians can be confident that normal clinical measurement variability will not significantly compromise the model's predictive accuracy. This is essential information for real-world deployment beyond our controlled research setting at Royan Institute, where our 2,413-patient dataset was collected under consistent protocols.

### 125. `stability_comparison`

Stability is distinct from average performance. A model can have high average AUC but poor stability if its performance swings wildly between evaluations.

The figure typically presents stability metrics such as the coefficient of variation of AUC, the range between best and worst fold performance, or consistency scores derived from repeated evaluations. Models are arranged to facilitate comparison, often with error bars or variance indicators.

In our evaluation of 16 models for micro-TESE outcome prediction, stability varies dramatically across model families. Linear models like Logistic Regression and Ridge Classifier tend to be stable but at a lower performance level. Their standard deviations of approximately 0.036 and 0.019 respectively reflect consistent but modest predictions.

Among the high-performing models, CatBoost shows a standard deviation of 0.0155 in the version one pipeline, suggesting moderate stability. XGBoost has a very low standard deviation of about 0.002, making it the most stable of the top performers. Gradient Boosting is extremely stable at 0.0004 standard deviation.

At the unstable end, Elastic Net and Lasso show standard deviations of approximately 0.095, and SVM with RBF kernel varies by 0.150. These wide swings mean that performance in any single evaluation could differ dramatically from the average.

For clinical deployment, stability matters because each prediction affects a real patient's surgical decision. If a model's performance depends heavily on which training samples it happened to see, there is a risk that some patients will receive predictions from effectively a good version of the model while others get predictions from a bad version.

CatBoost's combination of the highest AUC at 0.8306, moderate stability, and a manageable overfitting gap of 0.189 makes it the most suitable choice for reliable micro-TESE outcome prediction across the diverse patient presentations seen at Royan Institute.

</details>

### 126. `stability_seeds_boxplot`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-validation-stability_seeds_boxplot.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Validation
- **Dashboard Section:** Data Exploration
- **Project Brain:** `dataset`
- **Word Count:** 302

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (302 words)</summary>

> This figure uses boxplots to show how sensitive each model is to random seed initialization. Random seeds affect model training through weight initialization, data shuffling, feature subsampling, and other stochastic processes. A model that produces vastly different results depending on the random seed raises serious concerns about reproducibility.

Each boxplot represents one model. The box spans the interquartile range of AUC values obtained across multiple random seeds, the line inside shows the median, and the whiskers extend to show the full range. Outlier points beyond the whiskers indicate particularly unusual runs.

For our micro-TESE prediction study, we used random state 42 as the primary seed and evaluated consistency across additional seeds. A narrow boxplot indicates that the model reliably produces similar results regardless of random initialization. A wide boxplot indicates high sensitivity to the randomization.

Tree-based ensemble methods like CatBoost, XGBoost, and Random Forest typically show moderate seed sensitivity because they use random feature subsets at each split. However, their ensemble nature, combining hundreds of individual trees, tends to stabilize the overall prediction. CatBoost's ordered boosting approach provides additional stability by processing observations in a consistent manner.

In contrast, neural network models like MLP are often highly sensitive to weight initialization, which can lead to wide boxplots. This seed sensitivity, combined with its overfitting gap of 0.318 and validation AUC of only 0.670, further supports excluding neural networks from clinical consideration in our current dataset.

Reproducibility is a cornerstone of scientific and clinical credibility. Our pipeline ensures reproducibility by fixing the random state at 42 for all primary analyses. The seed stability analysis shown in this boxplot demonstrates that our key findings, particularly CatBoost's superiority with an AUC of 0.8306, are not artifacts of a lucky random initialization but represent genuine model performance on our 2,413-patient non-obstructive azoospermia cohort from Royan Institute.

</details>

### 129. `agreement_feature_comparison`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-agreement_feature_comparison.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Gallery Figure
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 381

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (381 words)</summary>

> This figure presents a feature importance agreement comparison, one of the most rigorous analyses in our Explainable AI pipeline for the micro-TESE outcome prediction model. The purpose of this chart is to evaluate whether different XAI methods independently converge on the same set of important features, thereby strengthening confidence in the model's decision-making logic.

The figure compares feature importance rankings derived from multiple explanation methods, including SHAP values, LIME local explanations, permutation importance, and native CatBoost feature importance. Each method uses a fundamentally different approach to assess feature relevance. SHAP leverages game-theoretic Shapley values to decompose each prediction into per-feature contributions. LIME fits locally interpretable linear models around individual predictions. Permutation importance measures performance degradation when each feature's values are randomly shuffled. Native importance reflects how frequently and how effectively CatBoost uses each feature across its ensemble of decision trees.

When reading this chart, look for features that appear in the top ranks across all methods. Features that are consistently ranked high regardless of the explanation technique provide the strongest evidence for genuine predictive relevance, rather than artifacts of any single method.

In our analysis of the CatBoost version two model with an AUC of 0.8306, several features demonstrate remarkable cross-method consistency. LH with a mean absolute SHAP value of 0.317 consistently ranks among the top predictors. Age follows with a SHAP value of 0.270, then FSH at 0.250, and Testosterone levels at 0.212. These four features appear in the top tier across virtually all explanation methods, confirming their genuine importance for predicting sperm retrieval success.

Features showing consistent mid-tier rankings include Body Weight with a SHAP value of 0.174, Sakamoto left testicular volume at 0.171, BMI at 0.169, and Height at 0.166. These anthropometric and testicular measurement variables demonstrate stable importance across methods, reinforcing their clinical relevance.

Clinically, this multi-method agreement analysis is essential because it guards against over-interpreting any single explanation technique. For urologists and reproductive medicine specialists counseling patients with non-obstructive azoospermia, knowing that hormonal markers like LH and FSH are robustly identified as important by every analytical lens provides strong justification for emphasizing these biomarkers in preoperative evaluation. The agreement analysis across our 2,413-patient cohort from Royan Institute validates that the model's reasoning aligns with established clinical knowledge while also revealing the quantitative hierarchy among predictive features.

</details>

Rather than presenting the full complexity of hundreds of decision trees, this summary distills the model's logic into human-interpretable rules that clinicians can apply during patient consultation.

Each rule identifies a combination of clinical features and threshold values that are associated with either high or low probability of successful sperm retrieval. ### 131. `cost_benefit_analysis`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-cost_benefit_analysis.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Gallery Figure
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 381

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (381 words)</summary>

> This figure presents a cost-benefit analysis of using our CatBoost prediction model at different decision thresholds for micro-TESE surgical planning. This analysis moves beyond pure statistical performance to address the real-world economic and clinical consequences of model-guided decisions.

The chart plots the net expected benefit of using the model at each threshold probability, incorporating the costs associated with different types of prediction errors. False positives in our context mean recommending micro-TESE to patients who will not yield sperm. While these patients undergo an unnecessary surgical procedure, it is important to note that micro-TESE also provides valuable diagnostic histopathological information. False negatives represent patients who are incorrectly predicted to fail but who actually could have had successful sperm retrieval. These missed opportunities carry significant emotional and reproductive consequences for couples trying to conceive.

The horizontal axis displays the decision threshold, meaning the minimum predicted probability at which the clinician would recommend proceeding with surgery. The vertical axis shows the net benefit after weighing the costs and benefits of correct and incorrect decisions.

At a very low threshold, essentially all patients are recommended for surgery, maximizing sensitivity but also maximizing unnecessary procedures. At a very high threshold, only the most confident positive predictions trigger surgery, minimizing false positives but potentially missing many viable cases.

Our CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306 achieves its optimal balance at a threshold in the Youden J range of 0.444 to 0.507. At this operating point, the model achieves 67.8 percent sensitivity, capturing roughly two-thirds of successful cases, while maintaining 82.3 percent specificity, correctly identifying over four in five negative cases.

The positive predictive value of 68.83 percent means that when the model recommends surgery, it is correct about seven out of ten times. The negative predictive value of 81.52 percent provides strong reassurance when the model suggests against proceeding.

For our cohort of 2,413 patients from Royan Institute with an overall success rate of approximately 36.7 percent, this cost-benefit analysis demonstrates that model-guided decision-making provides superior net benefit compared to both the treat-all strategy of offering surgery to everyone and the treat-none strategy of performing no surgeries. The model adds the greatest value in the intermediate probability zone where clinical uncertainty is highest and where shared decision-making between the urologist and the couple is most critical.

</details>

### 132. `cost_benefit_sensitivity`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-cost_benefit_sensitivity.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Gallery Figure
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 369

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (369 words)</summary>

> This figure presents a sensitivity analysis of our cost-benefit framework, examining how the conclusions about model utility change under different assumptions about the relative costs of false positive and false negative predictions. This is a critical robustness check because the exact costs assigned to prediction errors are inherently subjective and vary across clinical contexts.

The chart systematically varies the cost ratio between false positives and false negatives across a range of plausible scenarios. In micro-TESE for non-obstructive azoospermia, the cost of a false positive includes the financial cost of surgery, anesthesia risks, potential complications, and patient recovery time. The cost of a false negative includes the emotional burden of missed reproductive opportunity, potential need for donor sperm, and the psychological impact on couples who could have conceived with their own gametes.

Each line or bar in the figure represents the net benefit of using our CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306 under a different cost assumption. If the model's superiority over default strategies persists across a wide range of cost ratios, clinicians can be confident that model-guided decision-making is robust regardless of how they personally weigh these trade-offs.

The sensitivity analysis shows that even when the assumed costs change substantially, the model provides positive net benefit compared to treating all or treating no patients across most threshold ranges. This is particularly reassuring given the overall success rate of approximately 36.7 percent in our 2,413-patient cohort from Royan Institute.

The Youden J optimized threshold range of 0.444 to 0.507 proves robust across most cost scenarios, though the exact optimal threshold shifts slightly depending on the assumed cost ratio. When false negatives are considered much more costly than false positives, the optimal threshold moves lower, favoring higher sensitivity. When false positives are considered more costly, the threshold moves higher, favoring higher specificity.

For clinical practice, this sensitivity analysis validates that the model's utility is not contingent on any particular set of cost assumptions. Whether a clinic prioritizes minimizing unnecessary surgeries or maximizing the chance of finding sperm, our model adds value to the decision-making process. This robustness strengthens the case for adopting model-guided decision support in micro-TESE clinical workflows, as the benefits hold across diverse institutional priorities and patient preferences.

</details>

### 133. `counterfactual_explanations`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-counterfactual_explanations.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Gallery Figure
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 386

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (386 words)</summary>

> This figure presents counterfactual explanations for individual patient predictions from our CatBoost micro-TESE outcome model. Counterfactual analysis answers one of the most intuitive and clinically actionable questions in explainable AI: what is the smallest change in a patient's clinical features that would flip the prediction from negative to positive, or from positive to negative?

Unlike global feature importance which describes the model's general behavior, counterfactual explanations are deeply personal to each patient. For a specific individual predicted to have unsuccessful sperm retrieval, the counterfactual identifies the minimal modifications to their clinical profile that would shift the prediction to successful. These are not real clinical interventions but rather hypothetical changes that illuminate which features the model considers most pivotal for that particular case.

The figure displays several representative patient cases. For each case, arrows or highlighted changes indicate which features would need to change and by how much. The changes are ordered by magnitude, with the smallest modifications listed first, making the explanations maximally informative about the model's decision boundary in that region of feature space.

In our CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306, counterfactuals often involve changes to the top SHAP-ranked features. For instance, a patient with high LH levels might see that a reduction in LH, the feature ranked first with a mean absolute SHAP value of 0.317, would be the most efficient single-feature change to flip the prediction. Similarly, changes to FSH at SHAP rank three with a value of 0.250, or testosterone at rank four with 0.212, frequently appear in counterfactual explanations.

It is essential to understand that counterfactuals do not imply that these changes are clinically achievable. A patient cannot change their age or the severity of their testicular pathology. Rather, counterfactuals serve two important purposes. First, they provide deep insight into the model's decision logic at the individual patient level. Second, they highlight which features are closest to the model's decision boundary, indicating where additional diagnostic precision might most impact the prediction.

For our 2,413-patient cohort from Royan Institute, counterfactual explanations enhance patient counseling by making the model's reasoning transparent. A reproductive specialist can explain to a patient not just what the model predicts, but why, and what clinical profile would be needed to change that prediction. This transparency builds trust in the AI-assisted decision-making process for micro-TESE surgical planning.

</details>

### 134. `decision_curve_analysis`

- **Word Count:** 348

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (348 words)</summary>

> This figure presents the decision curve analysis from the Explainable AI phase of our micro-TESE outcome prediction study. Decision curve analysis is widely regarded as the gold standard for evaluating the clinical utility of prediction models because it directly measures the net benefit of model-guided decisions across all possible threshold probabilities.

The horizontal axis represents the threshold probability, which is the minimum predicted probability at which a clinician would recommend proceeding with micro-TESE surgery. The vertical axis shows the net benefit, a metric that accounts for both the benefits of correctly treated patients and the harms of incorrectly treated patients, weighted by the threshold probability.

Three reference strategies are displayed. The treat-all line represents performing micro-TESE on every patient regardless of the model prediction, starting at the prevalence level and decreasing. The treat-none line is fixed at zero, representing the strategy of never performing the procedure. Our CatBoost model curve should lie above both reference lines across a clinically meaningful range of thresholds for the model to have clinical utility.

For our champion CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306, the decision curve demonstrates positive net benefit above both baselines across threshold probabilities roughly from 0.05 to 0.60. This range encompasses virtually all clinically relevant decision points for micro-TESE surgery. The optimal operating point falls within the Youden J range of 0.444 to 0.507.

At the most common clinical decision threshold of around 30 to 50 percent, where most surgeons would be making the toughest decisions, the CatBoost model shows clear separation from the treat-all and treat-none strategies. This translates to real clinical value: additional correct treatment decisions per hundred patients compared to default approaches.

In the context of non-obstructive azoospermia, where the overall success rate in our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort is approximately 36.7 percent, the decision curve confirms that using our model to guide surgical decisions is superior to either offering surgery to everyone or withholding it from everyone. The model adds the most value precisely where clinical uncertainty is greatest, helping urologists and reproductive medicine specialists make better-informed decisions about this technically demanding microsurgical procedure.

</details>

### 136. `ensemble_variance_distribution`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-ensemble_variance_distribution.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Data Exploration
- **Project Brain:** `dataset`
- **Word Count:** 374

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (374 words)</summary>

> This figure displays the distribution of prediction variance across the ensemble of decision trees within our CatBoost model, providing a unique window into the model's internal uncertainty for each prediction. CatBoost, like all gradient boosting algorithms, builds its final prediction by combining the outputs of many individual decision trees. The variance among these individual tree predictions for any given patient reflects how confidently the ensemble agrees on its final answer.

The horizontal axis shows the prediction variance, with lower values indicating strong agreement among trees and higher values indicating internal disagreement. The vertical axis shows the number of patients at each variance level. The distribution reveals how uncertainty is distributed across our 2,413-patient cohort from Royan Institute.

In an ideal scenario, the distribution would be heavily concentrated near zero, meaning all trees agree on most predictions. In practice, some patients will have higher ensemble variance, indicating that the model finds their cases more ambiguous. These are typically patients with unusual feature combinations or clinical profiles that fall near the model's decision boundary.

For our CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306, which was optimized with a depth of 6, 657 iterations, a learning rate of 0.00617, and L2 leaf regularization of 3.51, the ensemble variance distribution provides clinical insight beyond what traditional performance metrics offer. A patient with a predicted probability of 0.50 could represent either a genuinely uncertain case where the model is torn, or a case where many trees weakly favor different outcomes. The variance distribution helps distinguish between these scenarios.

Clinically, this information is invaluable for micro-TESE surgical counseling. Patients whose predictions come with low ensemble variance can be counseled with higher confidence. Those with high variance should be flagged for additional clinical review, recognizing that the model's uncertainty reflects genuine ambiguity in their clinical profile rather than model failure.

The relationship between prediction variance and clinical outcomes can also be examined. If patients with higher variance tend to have less predictable outcomes, this validates the variance as a meaningful uncertainty measure. For the Royan Institute team, this analysis adds a crucial dimension to risk communication, distinguishing between patients where the model is confident in its 36.7 percent base rate adjustment and those where additional information might materially change the prediction.

</details>

The analysis evaluates model performance metrics including AUC, sensitivity, specificity, and positive predictive value across different subgroups defined by variables such as age categories, hormonal profiles, and potentially other demographic factors. A fair model should achieve comparable performance across all subgroups, meaning that no patient group is systematically disadvantaged by the model's predictions.

For our CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306, the fairness analysis examines performance across multiple dimensions. Age-based subgroups are particularly relevant because younger patients with non-obstructive azoospermia may have different underlying etiologies compared to older patients. The fairness analysis verifies whether these different predictions correspond to genuinely different outcome rates, or whether the model introduces unwarranted bias.

The figure displays performance metrics for each subgroup alongside the overall model performance of 77 percent accuracy, 67.8 percent sensitivity, and 82.3 percent specificity. Subgroups where performance drops significantly below the overall metrics are highlighted as potential fairness concerns.

In our 2,413-patient cohort from Royan Institute, the relatively homogeneous patient population may limit the scope of demographic disparities. However, clinical subgroups defined by pathology type, hormone levels, or testicular volume show meaningful variation in base rates, with success rates ranging from approximately 32 percent for Sertoli cell only to 88 percent for hypospermatogenesis. The fairness analysis ensures that the model's performance is acceptable within each of these clinically defined subgroups, supporting equitable clinical decision-making for all patients with non-obstructive azoospermia.

Each row or bar represents a specific fairness metric comparing two or more subgroups, with the magnitude of the disparity clearly displayed. Common fairness metrics include equalized odds, which requires equal true positive and false positive rates across groups, and demographic parity, which requires equal positive prediction rates.

For our model with an AUC of 0.8306, the disparity summary reveals the extent of performance variation across subgroups. For instance, if younger patients truly have different success rates than older patients, the model should predict differently for these groups.

The key distinction this figure helps make is between justified disparity, where performance differences reflect real outcome differences, and unjustified disparity, where the model introduces bias not present in the underlying data. For micro-TESE outcome prediction, the overall success rate of approximately 36.7 percent masks substantial variation. Patients with hypospermatogenesis have success rates near 88 percent, while those with predominant Sertoli cell only pathology succeed about 32 percent of the time. For deployment at Royan Institute and potentially other fertility centers, this fairness disparity summary provides essential governance documentation. ### 140. `feature_instability_ranking`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-feature_instability_ranking.mp3`

- **Project Brain:** `results`

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (369 words)</summary>

> This figure ranks features by their importance instability, showing how much each feature's importance ranking changes across different cross-validation folds, random seeds, or explanation methods. Feature instability is a critical but often overlooked aspect of model interpretability that directly affects the reliability of clinical conclusions drawn from feature importance analyses.

A feature with low instability consistently maintains its importance ranking regardless of which subset of patients is used for training or evaluation. A feature with high instability may rank fifth in one fold but twentieth in another, suggesting that its importance is not robust and may reflect sampling artifacts rather than genuine predictive relationships.

The chart displays features ordered from most stable at the top to most unstable at the bottom. For each feature, a measure of ranking variability, such as the standard deviation of ranks across evaluations, is shown.

In our CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306, the top SHAP-ranked features would ideally show low instability. LH, ranked first with a mean absolute SHAP value of 0.317, should maintain its top position across different data subsets. Similarly, Age at 0.270, FSH at 0.250, and Testosterone at 0.212 should show stable rankings if they represent truly robust predictive signals.

Features that show high instability despite having moderate average importance warrant clinical caution. Their predictive contribution may depend heavily on specific patient subgroups or data characteristics that do not generalize reliably. For micro-TESE clinical decision support, basing surgical recommendations on unstable features could lead to inconsistent guidance.

This instability analysis is especially important given our study's sample size of 2,413 patients from Royan Institute. With moderate sample sizes, some features may appear important simply because they happen to correlate with outcomes in a particular data split. The nested cross-validation framework with 2 outer and 5 inner folds helps mitigate this, but the instability ranking provides direct evidence of which features' importance estimates are trustworthy.

The clinical takeaway is that features showing both high importance and high stability, such as the hormonal markers LH and FSH, should be prioritized in the clinical decision support system. Features with high importance but also high instability should be included but interpreted with appropriate caveats during patient counseling about micro-TESE outcomes in non-obstructive azoospermia.

</details>

### 141. `feature_redundancy_focused`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-feature_redundancy_focused.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Gallery Figure
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 341

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (341 words)</summary>

> This figure presents a focused analysis of redundant features in our micro-TESE prediction model, identifying which variables carry overlapping predictive information. Feature redundancy occurs when two or more features are highly correlated or provide essentially the same signal to the model, meaning one could potentially be removed without substantial loss of predictive power.

The analysis is particularly relevant for our dataset because several features measure related clinical constructs. For testicular volume alone, we have multiple measurement methods: ultrasonographic right and left testis size, orchidometer-based measurements, XYZ sonographic volumes, Sakamoto formula volumes, and clinical estimate volumes. These different measurement approaches inherently capture overlapping information about testicular size.

Similarly, among hormonal features, LH with a SHAP value of 0.317 and FSH at 0.250 are both gonadotropins that reflect hypothalamic-pituitary-gonadal axis function. While they measure different hormones, they are often correlated in clinical populations with non-obstructive azoospermia.

The figure highlights specific redundant feature groups with quantitative measures of their overlap. Features within the same redundancy cluster share substantial mutual information, meaning the model could potentially maintain similar performance using only one representative from each cluster.

For our CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306, understanding redundancy helps simplify the clinical implementation. If Sakamoto left testicular volume at SHAP rank six with 0.171 and clinical estimate of left testicular volume at rank ten with 0.129 carry overlapping information, a deployed clinical tool might only need to collect one measurement, reducing data entry burden without compromising prediction quality.

However, redundancy does not automatically mean a feature should be removed. The model may use redundant features to improve robustness, capturing signal from whichever measurement is available when the other is missing. In our dataset of 2,413 patients from Royan Institute, missingness rates vary substantially across features, with some testicular measurements missing for over 80 percent of patients. In this context, retaining redundant features ensures the model can make informed predictions regardless of which measurements are available for a particular patient. This analysis guides clinical teams on where to focus their data collection efforts for optimal prediction quality.

</details>

### 143. `feature_redundancy_performance`

- **Project Brain:** `results`

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (352 words)</summary>

> This figure examines how model performance changes when redundant features are systematically removed from our CatBoost micro-TESE prediction model. This is the critical experiment that determines whether feature redundancy is merely a statistical observation or whether it has practical implications for model simplification.

The chart shows model performance, typically measured by AUC, on the vertical axis as features are progressively removed on the horizontal axis. Features are removed in order of redundancy, starting with the most redundant, meaning those that share the most information with other retained features.

The key question this figure answers is whether we can simplify the model's feature set without sacrificing predictive performance. If the AUC curve remains flat or nearly flat as the first several redundant features are removed, those features were truly redundant and can be safely eliminated. If performance drops sharply, the apparent redundancy was misleading, and those features were contributing unique information the model needs.

For our CatBoost model starting at an AUC of 0.8306, the performance trajectory during feature removal reveals the minimum feature set needed to maintain clinical-grade prediction. Our full model uses 58 clinical features including 18 pathology features derived from free-text histopathology reports. The question is how many of these are truly necessary.

Among the testicular measurement features, where we have multiple measurement methods for each testis, there is likely genuine redundancy. Removing some of these measurements may have minimal impact on the AUC. Among the hormonal features, however, even correlated features like LH and FSH may each contribute unique variance that the model exploits.

This analysis has direct implications for clinical deployment. A model requiring 58 features faces practical barriers to adoption, as clinicians must collect extensive data for each patient. If a reduced model with 20 or 30 features achieves comparable performance, clinical adoption becomes far more feasible.

For our 2,413-patient cohort from Royan Institute, this redundancy performance analysis also informs future data collection protocols. By identifying the minimum essential feature set, the research team can design more efficient clinical assessments that capture the predictive information needed for accurate micro-TESE outcome prediction without burdening patients with unnecessary tests.

</details>

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (379 words)</summary>

> This heatmap displays flip rates for our CatBoost micro-TESE prediction model, showing how often individual patient predictions change when each feature is perturbed. A flip rate represents the proportion of patients whose predicted class, positive or negative for sperm retrieval, switches when a single feature value is modified while all other features remain constant.

The rows of the heatmap represent different features, while the columns may represent different perturbation magnitudes or patient subgroups. Warmer colors indicate higher flip rates, meaning the feature has more influence over the final prediction, while cooler colors indicate that changes to that feature rarely affect the classification outcome.

High flip rate features are the most sensitive levers in the model's decision-making process. When these features change even slightly, predictions change frequently. For clinical applications, high flip rate features identify where measurement precision matters most, because small errors or variations in these measurements could meaningfully alter the patient's predicted outcome.

For our CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306, the flip rate analysis should align with but also extend beyond the SHAP importance rankings. LH with a SHAP value of 0.317 and FSH at 0.250 likely show high flip rates, confirming their status as primary decision drivers. However, flip rates capture something SHAP does not: the local sensitivity of predictions near the decision boundary at the Youden J threshold of 0.444 to 0.507.

Features with moderate SHAP importance but very high flip rates near the decision boundary are clinically important despite not dominating the global importance ranking. These are features where small clinical variations, such as normal laboratory measurement error, could push a borderline patient from one recommendation to another.

Conversely, features with high SHAP importance but low flip rates indicate that while they strongly influence the predicted probability, they rarely change the binary classification because their effect moves predictions within a class rather than across the decision boundary.

For clinicians at Royan Institute counseling patients with non-obstructive azoospermia, the flip rate heatmap identifies which measurements warrant repeat testing or careful verification. If a patient's predicted outcome is highly sensitive to their LH value, confirming that measurement with a repeat blood draw before making a surgical decision is clinically prudent. This analysis bridges statistical importance and practical clinical workflow for our 2,413-patient micro-TESE prediction system.

Each cell in the heatmap shows the interaction strength between two features. The diagonal is typically not meaningful since a feature does not interact with itself in a standard pairwise sense. Off-diagonal cells with intense colors indicate strong interactions, meaning those two features jointly influence the prediction in ways that neither feature alone could explain.

Interactions involving testicular measurements are also clinically significant. The Sakamoto left testicular volume at SHAP rank six with 0.171 and right testicular XYZ sonographic volume at rank nine with 0.156 may interact with each other, capturing bilateral testicular asymmetry patterns that inform sperm retrieval probability.

For the 2,413-patient cohort from Royan Institute, this interaction heatmap reveals the complex multivariate relationships that make machine learning superior to simple univariate clinical prediction rules. Non-obstructive azoospermia is a condition where outcomes depend on the interplay between hormonal milieu, testicular biology, and patient demographics. The interaction heatmap makes these learned relationships visible and interpretable for reproductive medicine specialists.

This interaction represents a fascinating bridge between testicular anatomy and hormonal biochemistry.

The plot shows how the relationship between Sakamoto left testicular volume and predicted micro-TESE outcome varies across different levels of E2. Sakamoto left testicular volume ranks sixth in SHAP importance with a mean absolute value of 0.171, while E2 ranks fourteenth at 0.043. Despite E2's relatively lower individual importance, its interaction with testicular volume emerges as one of the strongest detected synergistic effects.

The biological basis for this interaction is compelling. Estradiol is produced primarily through aromatization of testosterone in adipose tissue and, importantly, within the testes themselves. Higher E2 levels may indicate either excessive peripheral aromatization, which can suppress gonadotropins and impair spermatogenesis, or paradoxically, active Leydig cell function within the testes, which could be a marker of testicular functional reserve.

When interpreted alongside testicular volume, E2 levels gain additional prognostic meaning. A patient with small testes and high E2 might have a different prognosis than a patient with normal-sized testes and high E2, because the source and implications of the estradiol differ. The CatBoost model has learned to exploit this differential pattern.

In our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort, this interaction contributes to the model's overall AUC of 0.8306 by capturing a relationship that simple linear models cannot represent. The interaction between a physical measurement and a biochemical marker exemplifies the type of complex, multivariate pattern that makes gradient boosting models like CatBoost superior to simpler approaches for predicting micro-TESE outcomes.

For reproductive endocrinologists, this finding suggests that evaluating E2 levels in the context of testicular volume may provide more clinically useful information than assessing either variable in isolation. This is consistent with the personalized medicine approach our model supports, where preoperative counseling for non-obstructive azoospermia considers the interplay between multiple clinical variables rather than relying on any single biomarker.

</details>

> This figure displays the distribution of prediction agreement levels across all 16 machine learning models evaluated in our micro-TESE outcome prediction study. Model agreement quantifies how many of the 16 models make the same prediction for each patient, ranging from complete disagreement, where models are evenly split, to unanimous agreement, where all models classify the patient identically.

The horizontal axis shows the level of agreement, expressed as a percentage or count of models agreeing on the majority prediction. The vertical axis shows the number of patients at each agreement level. This distribution reveals the overall decisiveness of our model ensemble across the 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort.

Patients with high model agreement, where 14 or more of the 16 models agree, represent cases where the clinical features strongly indicate either success or failure. These patients typically have extreme clinical profiles, such as very favorable or very unfavorable hormonal and anatomical parameters. The model ensemble is confident about these cases regardless of algorithmic approach.

Patients with low model agreement, where only 9 or 10 of 16 models agree, represent genuinely ambiguous cases. These are patients whose clinical profiles fall near the decision boundary, where different modeling assumptions lead to different conclusions. For these patients, the prediction is inherently uncertain, and clinical judgment should weigh more heavily alongside the model's recommendation.

The distribution shape provides insight into the difficulty of the prediction task. A distribution heavily concentrated at the extremes suggests that most patients are straightforward cases. A distribution with substantial mass in the middle indicates that many patients present genuine diagnostic challenges.

For micro-TESE outcome prediction, where the overall success rate is approximately 36.7 percent, we expect a meaningful proportion of patients in the ambiguous zone. This is because the base rate is not extremely skewed, and the 16 models span a wide performance range from Naive Bayes at AUC 0.523 to CatBoost at 0.831 in the version two pipeline.

The clinical value of this agreement distribution is that it provides a model-independent measure of prediction difficulty. When our champion CatBoost model makes a prediction for a patient where 15 of 16 models agree, clinicians can communicate with higher confidence. When CatBoost's prediction disagrees with the majority of other models, additional clinical scrutiny is warranted.

</details>

Features that rank high in both analyses are the most confidently important predictors.

Features that rank high in SHAP but low in average native importance might be exploiting CatBoost-specific patterns, such as complex interactions that simpler models cannot capture. Conversely, features that rank high in average native importance but low in SHAP might be universally useful but less critical for CatBoost's specific approach.

For clinical practice in micro-TESE surgical planning, this consensus importance provides the strongest evidence base for identifying which preoperative measurements and laboratory tests contribute most to prediction accuracy. ### 156. `nomogram_full`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-nomogram_full.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Gallery Figure
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 362

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (362 words)</summary>

> This figure presents a full clinical nomogram translating our champion CatBoost model's predictions into a point-based scoring system designed for bedside clinical use. A nomogram is one of the most clinician-friendly tools for translating complex machine learning models into practical clinical instruments, allowing urologists to estimate sperm retrieval probability without accessing a computer.

The nomogram works by assigning points to each feature value based on its contribution to the predicted probability. A master scale at the top converts raw feature values to points, and the total accumulated points are then mapped to a predicted probability of successful micro-TESE sperm retrieval.

Each feature occupies a row in the nomogram. For our CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306, the features are ordered by their predictive contribution. LH, as the top-ranked feature with a SHAP value of 0.317, receives the widest point range, meaning variations in LH values produce the largest swings in total points. Age at SHAP 0.270 has the second widest range, followed by FSH at 0.250 and Testosterone at 0.212.

To use the nomogram, a clinician locates each patient's value on the corresponding feature scale, draws a vertical line up to the points axis to read the point value, sums all points across all features, and then reads the predicted probability from the total points scale at the bottom.

For example, a patient might receive 15 points for their LH value, 12 points for their age, 10 points for FSH, and so on across all included features. The total might sum to 85 points, which maps to a predicted probability of, say, 45 percent.

The nomogram effectively distills the 657 iterations of gradient boosted trees with a depth of 6 into a paper-based tool. While this necessarily involves some approximation compared to running the full CatBoost model, nomograms have a long and successful history in clinical medicine for conditions ranging from cancer prognosis to cardiovascular risk.

For Royan Institute clinicians managing patients with non-obstructive azoospermia, this nomogram enables immediate bedside probability estimation during the initial consultation, before any computer-based analysis is performed. It serves as both a clinical tool and a patient communication aid, making the model's predictions tangible and transparent.

</details>

### 157. `nomogram_odds_ratio_forest`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-nomogram_odds_ratio_forest.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Gallery Figure
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 377

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (377 words)</summary>

> This figure presents a forest plot of odds ratios for the features included in our clinical nomogram, showing the effect size and confidence interval for each predictor. Forest plots are among the most widely recognized visualizations in clinical research, making this an accessible entry point for clinicians who may be less familiar with machine learning interpretability methods.

Each feature is represented by a horizontal line with a central point estimate, the odds ratio, and extending confidence interval bars. The vertical reference line at an odds ratio of 1.0 indicates no effect. Features to the right of this line increase the odds of successful sperm retrieval, while features to the left decrease the odds.

The width of each confidence interval reflects the precision of the estimate. Narrow intervals indicate well-determined effect sizes, while wide intervals suggest uncertainty. For our 2,413-patient cohort from Royan Institute, the confidence intervals balance the moderate sample size against the strength of the signal captured by our CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306.

Reading the forest plot, the features with the largest odds ratios, furthest from 1.0, are the strongest predictors. Based on our SHAP analysis, LH with a mean absolute SHAP value of 0.317 should show a substantial odds ratio, reflecting its dominant role in the prediction model. FSH at 0.250 and Testosterone at 0.212 should also show meaningful odds ratios.

The direction of each odds ratio provides clinical interpretation. A feature with an odds ratio greater than 1.0 means that higher values of that feature are associated with increased odds of successful sperm retrieval, all else being equal. An odds ratio less than 1.0 means higher values are associated with decreased odds.

It is important to note that these odds ratios are derived from the CatBoost model's learned relationships and may differ from odds ratios obtained from traditional logistic regression. This is because CatBoost captures nonlinear effects and interactions that logistic regression cannot represent. The nomogram's odds ratios approximate the CatBoost model's behavior in a linear odds ratio framework.

For reproductive medicine specialists, this forest plot provides the familiar language of effect sizes and confidence intervals for communicating the model's findings. It bridges the gap between modern machine learning and traditional clinical evidence interpretation for micro-TESE outcome prediction in non-obstructive azoospermia.

</details>

For categorical features, each category receives a fixed point value. Features like karyotype status or Y-chromosome microdeletion receive specific point allocations based on their association with sperm retrieval outcomes in our 2,413-patient cohort.

### 160. `permutation_importance_boxplot`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-permutation_importance_boxplot.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Feature Analysis
- **Project Brain:** `xai`
- **Word Count:** 345

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (345 words)</summary>

> This figure displays boxplots of permutation importance values for each feature across multiple cross-validation folds or random repetitions. Permutation importance measures how much model performance degrades when each feature's values are randomly shuffled, breaking the relationship between that feature and the outcome while preserving all other features.

Each boxplot represents one feature. The box spans the interquartile range of importance values across multiple runs, the central line shows the median, and the whiskers extend to the full range. This visualization reveals both the central importance estimate and its variability, which is crucial for assessing the reliability of importance rankings.

Features with high median importance and narrow boxes are robustly important. Features with high median importance but wide boxes are important on average but their contribution varies substantially depending on the specific data split. Features with boxes centered near zero are unimportant regardless of the evaluation setup.

For our CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306, the permutation importance results should broadly align with the SHAP rankings. LH at SHAP rank one with 0.317, Age at rank two with 0.270, FSH at rank three with 0.250, and Testosterone at rank four with 0.212 should show high median permutation importance values with relatively tight boxes, confirming their robust predictive contribution.

The boxplot format is particularly informative for identifying features where importance is context-dependent. A feature that is critical in some cross-validation folds but irrelevant in others might indicate that its predictive value depends on specific patient subgroups present in the training data.

In our nested cross-validation framework with 2 outer folds and 5 inner folds, the boxplots capture this fold-to-fold variability. With only 2,413 patients from Royan Institute, each fold contains relatively few patients, making fold-level importance estimates somewhat noisy. The boxplot format honestly presents this uncertainty.

Clinically, features with consistent permutation importance across all evaluation runs deserve the highest confidence in clinical decision support systems. These are the measurements that should be prioritized in the preoperative assessment protocol for micro-TESE in non-obstructive azoospermia, as their predictive value is reliable regardless of patient mix or data partitioning.

Permutation importance is particularly valuable as a cross-model comparison metric because it uses a consistent methodology across all algorithm types. Unlike native feature importance, which means different things for tree-based versus linear versus neural network models, permutation importance always measures the same concept: performance degradation when a feature is shuffled.

In our micro-TESE prediction pipeline, the top SHAP features from CatBoost, LH at 0.317, Age at 0.270, FSH at 0.250, and Testosterone at 0.212, should show warm vertical bands across most model rows. For instance, a feature might be important for tree-based models that can capture threshold effects but unimportant for linear models that require monotonic relationships.

Features that are unimportant across all models, such as those with zero SHAP values in CatBoost including Race, Habits, and most categorical comorbidity indicators, should appear as consistently cool-colored columns. This cross-model confirmation validates the finding that these features do not contribute meaningfully to prediction.

For clinical practice at Royan Institute, this heatmap provides the strongest possible evidence for feature prioritization. Features showing warm colors across all 16 model rows represent the most robustly validated predictors of micro-TESE success, deserving priority in clinical data collection protocols for the 2,413-patient and future patient cohorts with non-obstructive azoospermia.

### 167. `shap_vs_lime_comparison`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-shap_vs_lime_comparison.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Project Brain:** `xai`
- **Word Count:** 349

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (349 words)</summary>

> This figure presents a direct comparison of SHAP and LIME explanations for our CatBoost micro-TESE outcome prediction model, showing where these two prominent explainable AI methods agree and disagree in their assessment of feature importance.

SHAP and LIME represent fundamentally different approaches to model interpretation. SHAP is grounded in cooperative game theory, computing exact Shapley values that decompose each prediction into additive feature contributions with mathematical guarantees of consistency and local accuracy. LIME creates local linear approximations around individual predictions by perturbing input features and observing how predictions change.

The figure typically shows feature importance rankings from both methods side by side, either for global importance across all patients or for specific individual cases. Agreement between SHAP and LIME strengthens confidence in the importance ranking, while disagreement highlights features whose influence is more complex or locally variable.

For our CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306, the SHAP global importance shows LH leading at 0.317, followed by Age at 0.270, FSH at 0.250, Testosterone at 0.212, and Body Weight at 0.174. If LIME produces a similar ranking, the feature importance hierarchy is considered robust across explanation paradigms.

Where disagreements occur, they often reveal interesting model behavior. LIME's local linear approximation may rank a feature differently because the true feature effect is highly nonlinear. In the region around a specific prediction, the linear approximation may under or overestimate the feature's contribution compared to the exact SHAP value.

For features like testicular volume measurements, where the relationship with sperm retrieval success may have a threshold effect rather than a linear relationship, SHAP and LIME may diverge most significantly. SHAP captures the full nonlinear contribution through its exact decomposition, while LIME may struggle to approximate strong nonlinearities with a local linear model.

Clinically, this comparison reassures practitioners at Royan Institute that the model's feature importance is not dependent on the choice of explanation method. For patient counseling about micro-TESE for non-obstructive azoospermia, knowing that both SHAP and LIME identify the same key predictive features provides robust support for focusing preoperative discussions on hormonal markers, age, and testicular measurements across the 2,413-patient cohort.

</details>

For the positive class, representing successful micro-TESE sperm retrieval, the distribution of confidence intervals shows how certain the model is when predicting success. For the negative class, representing unsuccessful retrieval, the distribution shows the model's confidence in negative predictions.

In an ideal scenario, the model would show narrow confidence intervals, indicating high certainty, for both correctly predicted positive and negative cases, with wider intervals concentrated among misclassified cases. This pattern would confirm that the model's uncertainty measure is informative: when the model says it is uncertain, it is more likely to be wrong.

For our CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306, the class-stratified uncertainty analysis connects to the model's operating characteristics. Similarly, with specificity at 82.3 percent, most negative cases are correctly identified with presumably narrow confidence intervals. The false positives, about 17.7 percent of negative cases, should show wider uncertainty, reflecting the model's difficulty distinguishing them from true positives.

The overall success rate of approximately 36.7 percent in our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort means there are fewer positive cases to analyze. This class imbalance affects the statistical precision of the positive-class uncertainty distribution.

For clinical counseling, this class-stratified view of uncertainty enables more nuanced conversations with patients. When the model predicts success with narrow confidence intervals, clinicians can communicate greater conviction. When confidence intervals are wide, regardless of the predicted class, the recommendation should be tempered with appropriate caveats about the model's limitations for that particular patient profile in the context of non-obstructive azoospermia.

This pattern creates a characteristic U-shaped or inverted U-shaped relationship between predicted probability and confidence interval width. Patients just above this threshold with wide confidence intervals might be reclassified if additional clinical information were available. Patients just below the threshold with narrow confidence intervals are more definitively classified as negative predictions.

For clinicians at Royan Institute counseling patients with non-obstructive azoospermia, this scatter plot provides a powerful visual for understanding the precision of individual predictions. A patient can be shown not just their predicted probability of successful micro-TESE but also how confident the model is in that specific estimate, enabling more informed shared decision-making. ### 174. `roc_CatBoost`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-roc_CatBoost.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Model Performance
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 308

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (308 words)</summary>

> This figure presents the Receiver Operating Characteristic curve for the champion CatBoost model evaluated at the default classification threshold of 0.5. The ROC curve is one of the most fundamental evaluation tools in clinical prediction modeling, plotting sensitivity on the vertical axis against one minus specificity on the horizontal axis across all possible classification thresholds. The diagonal reference line represents random chance at an AUC of 0.50, while a perfect model would reach the upper left corner with an AUC of 1.0. CatBoost is a gradient boosting algorithm developed by Yandex that handles categorical features natively through ordered boosting, which reduces prediction shift and provides built-in regularization. In our micro-TESE outcome prediction study for non-obstructive azoospermia, it achieved an AUC of 0.8306 with a 95 percent confidence interval of 0.823-0.845, establishing it as the undisputed champion among all 16 algorithms evaluated. At the default threshold of 0.5, CatBoost achieves a sensitivity of 67.8 percent and a specificity of 82.3 percent. The AUC of 0.8306 means that if we randomly select one patient who had successful sperm retrieval and one who did not, CatBoost will correctly rank the successful patient higher approximately 83 percent of the time. This strong discrimination was achieved through Bayesian hyperparameter optimization with 50 iterations, yielding a tree depth of 6, 657 boosting iterations, a learning rate of 0.00617, and L2 leaf regularization of 3.51. The ROC curve for CatBoost bows substantially toward the upper left corner, demonstrating excellent separation between the 63 patients with successful sperm retrieval and the 107 with unsuccessful outcomes in our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort. For clinicians evaluating whether to proceed with micro-TESE for a patient with non-obstructive azoospermia, this curve illustrates the inherent trade-off between capturing more true positives and accepting more false positives, a balance that can be adjusted by moving along this curve to different operating thresholds.

</details>

### 175. `roc_DecisionTree`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-roc_DecisionTree.mp3`

- **Dashboard Section:** Model Performance
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 255

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (255 words)</summary>

> This figure displays the Receiver Operating Characteristic curve for the Decision Tree model at the default classification threshold of 0.5. Single decision tree ROC curves often show a limited number of discrete operating points because the tree partitions patients into a finite set of leaf nodes, each producing a fixed predicted probability. Decision Tree is a single decision tree that recursively partitions patients into groups using binary splitting rules, producing an inherently interpretable if-then decision structure. In the version one evaluation, Decision Tree achieved an AUC of 0.6457, ranking 12 among the 16 models tested. This is 0.1849 AUC points below the champion CatBoost model's version two AUC of 0.8306. At the default threshold, the model produces a sensitivity of 51.5 percent and specificity of 68.2 percent, yielding an overall accuracy of 62.0 percent and an F1 score of 0.497. The overfitting gap of 0.164, calculated as the difference between training AUC of 0.8096 and validation AUC, provides insight into how well Decision Tree generalizes beyond its training data. Comparing Decision Tree's ROC curve to CatBoost's, the gap of 0.1849 AUC points translates to measurably fewer correct pairwise rankings of positive versus negative patients. In the clinical context of predicting micro-TESE outcomes for non-obstructive azoospermia, this performance difference means Decision Tree would provide less reliable guidance for the approximately 63 positive and 107 negative cases in our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort. All models were evaluated using nested cross-validation with Bayesian hyperparameter optimization across 50 iterations, ensuring these comparisons reflect genuine generalization rather than training artifacts.

### 189. `roc_CatBoost_adjusted`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-roc_CatBoost_adjusted.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Model Performance
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 279

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (279 words)</summary>

> This figure shows the ROC curve for the champion CatBoost model with its classification threshold optimized using the Youden J statistic rather than the default 0.5. Threshold optimization is a critical step in translating a machine learning model into a clinical decision support instrument. The Youden J statistic, defined as sensitivity plus specificity minus one, identifies the threshold that maximizes the combined discriminative ability. For CatBoost, the optimized threshold falls within the range of 0.444-0.507, which represents the sweet spot where the model best balances capturing true positives against avoiding false positives. At this optimized operating point, CatBoost achieves a sensitivity of 67.8 percent and specificity of 82.3 percent, with a positive predictive value of 68.8 percent and negative predictive value of 81.5 percent. The AUC remains at 0.8306 regardless of threshold choice because AUC is a threshold-independent metric summarizing the entire ROC curve. However, the specific position on the curve shifts to optimize clinical utility. Moving the threshold lower increases sensitivity, capturing more patients with successful sperm retrieval, but at the cost of more false positives meaning more patients undergo surgery without finding sperm. Moving it higher has the opposite effect. For couples facing non-obstructive azoospermia, the adjusted threshold means the model gives equal weight to both error types. The positive predictive value of 68.8 percent indicates that surgical recommendations are correct roughly seven out of ten times, while the negative predictive value of 81.5 percent provides strong reassurance when the model advises against surgery. This balanced operating point, combined with an overall accuracy of 77.0 percent and Matthews correlation coefficient of 0.502, was validated through nested cross-validation on our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort with 58 clinical features.

</details>

### 190. `roc_DecisionTree_adjusted`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-roc_DecisionTree_adjusted.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Model Performance
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 262

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (262 words)</summary>

> This figure presents the ROC curve for the Decision Tree model after threshold optimization using the Youden J statistic. Decision Tree threshold adjustment has a distinctive effect because the predicted probabilities come from a finite set of leaf node values, creating discrete jumps in sensitivity and specificity as the threshold crosses each leaf probability. For Decision Tree with an AUC of 0.6457, the adjusted threshold shifts the operating point along the ROC curve to achieve a better balance between sensitivity and specificity than the default 0.5 provides. At the default threshold, Decision Tree achieves sensitivity of 51.5 percent and specificity of 68.2 percent. After optimization, these values shift as the model trades one metric for the other to reach the Youden J optimal point. The clinical impact of threshold adjustment is substantial for micro-TESE surgical decisions. A false positive means subjecting a patient to invasive microsurgery without ultimately finding sperm, causing unnecessary surgical risk, recovery burden, and emotional distress. A false negative means a patient who could have had successful sperm retrieval is incorrectly advised against surgery, potentially missing their chance for biological parenthood. Compared to the champion CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306 and optimized threshold range of 0.444 to 0.507, Decision Tree demonstrates that even with optimal threshold selection, a model with lower intrinsic discrimination cannot match the performance of a superior model at its own optimal threshold. This principle is important for clinical implementation because it confirms that model selection matters more than threshold tuning. The analysis was conducted through nested cross-validation on our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort.

### 201. `pr_CatBoost`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-pr_CatBoost.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Model Results
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 246

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (246 words)</summary>

> This figure shows the Precision-Recall curve for the champion CatBoost model at the default classification threshold of 0.5. Precision-Recall curves are especially informative for medical datasets with unequal class sizes because they focus exclusively on positive prediction quality, exposing weaknesses that ROC curves can mask. In our 2,413-patient cohort from Royan Institute, successful sperm retrieval occurs in approximately 36.7 percent of cases, creating a class imbalance that makes Precision-Recall analysis particularly valuable. The baseline for a random classifier on this plot is a horizontal line at the prevalence level of 0.367, substantially higher than the diagonal reference used in ROC analysis. CatBoost achieves a precision of 68.8 percent and recall of 67.8 percent at its operating point, meaning approximately seven in ten positive predictions lead to actual surgical success, while two-thirds of all true positive cases are correctly identified. The area under the Precision-Recall curve reflects CatBoost's ability to maintain high precision across a range of recall values. For surgical planning in non-obstructive azoospermia, precision directly answers the urologist's question: among patients my model recommends for surgery, what fraction will actually have sperm found? CatBoost's precision of 68.8 percent provides a concrete, actionable answer for preoperative counseling. The F1 score of 0.684, which is the harmonic mean of precision and recall, summarizes this trade-off in a single number. Combined with its champion AUC of 0.8306, CatBoost demonstrates that its superior discrimination extends to the precision-recall domain, maintaining high positive prediction quality even at moderate recall levels.

</details>

### 202. `pr_DecisionTree`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-pr_DecisionTree.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Gallery Figure
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 240

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (240 words)</summary>

> This figure displays the Precision-Recall curve for the Decision Tree model at the default threshold of 0.5. Decision Tree Precision-Recall curves exhibit characteristic plateaus corresponding to the finite set of leaf node predictions, with each plateau representing a different confidence level in the tree structure. Decision Tree is a single decision tree that recursively partitions patients into groups using binary splitting rules, producing an inherently interpretable if-then decision structure. In our dataset with approximately 36.7 percent positive cases, the random classifier baseline sits at 0.367, and any model providing genuine predictive value must achieve a curve substantially above this line. At its operating point, Decision Tree achieves a precision of 48.4 percent and recall of 51.5 percent, with an F1 score of 0.497. Compared to the champion CatBoost model with precision of 68.8 percent and recall of 67.8 percent, Decision Tree shows a less favorable precision-recall profile, reflecting its lower overall AUC of 0.6457 versus 0.8306. The clinical consequence of lower precision is that a higher proportion of patients recommended for micro-TESE based on Decision Tree's predictions would undergo surgery without finding sperm. Lower recall means more patients who could benefit from successful sperm retrieval would not be identified as surgical candidates. For couples navigating the emotional and financial challenges of non-obstructive azoospermia at Royan Institute, the difference in precision between Decision Tree and CatBoost translates directly to the reliability of the model's surgical recommendations across our 2,413-patient study population.

</details>

Neural network Precision-Recall curves can exhibit unusual shapes when the model's confidence distribution is bimodal, with clusters of high-confidence and low-confidence predictions and few intermediate values. Multi-Layer Perceptron is a Multi-Layer Perceptron neural network with multiple hidden layers of interconnected neurons that can learn complex nonlinear mappings from clinical features to outcomes. In our dataset with approximately 36.7 percent positive cases, the random classifier baseline sits at 0.367, and any model providing genuine predictive value must achieve a curve substantially above this line. At its operating point, Multi-Layer Perceptron achieves a precision of 53.0 percent and recall of 46.7 percent, with an F1 score of 0.496. Compared to the champion CatBoost model with precision of 68.8 percent and recall of 67.8 percent, Multi-Layer Perceptron shows a less favorable precision-recall profile, reflecting its lower overall AUC of 0.6696 versus 0.8306. The clinical consequence of lower precision is that a higher proportion of patients recommended for micro-TESE based on Multi-Layer Perceptron's predictions would undergo surgery without finding sperm. Lower recall means more patients who could benefit from successful sperm retrieval would not be identified as surgical candidates. For couples navigating the emotional and financial challenges of non-obstructive azoospermia at Royan Institute, the difference in precision between Multi-Layer Perceptron and CatBoost translates directly to the reliability of the model's surgical recommendations across our 2,413-patient study population.

</details>

### 216. `pr_CatBoost_adjusted`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-pr_CatBoost_adjusted.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Model Results
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 229

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (229 words)</summary>

> This figure presents the Precision-Recall curve for the champion CatBoost model with its threshold adjusted using the Youden J statistic. While the area under the Precision-Recall curve is independent of threshold choice, the specific operating point shifts when moving from the default 0.5 to the optimized range of 0.444-0.507. At the adjusted threshold, CatBoost achieves precision of 68.8 percent and recall of 67.8 percent. An important subtlety is that the Youden J criterion optimizes sensitivity plus specificity, not the precision-recall trade-off directly. This means the Youden J optimal threshold may not coincide with the threshold that maximizes the F1 score. In our dataset with 36.7 percent prevalence, this distinction matters because precision is influenced by class prevalence while sensitivity and specificity are not. The F1 score at the adjusted threshold is 0.684, representing the harmonic mean of precision and recall at this operating point. For micro-TESE surgical planning, the adjusted precision answers a critical clinical question: when CatBoost recommends surgery at the optimized threshold, how often is that recommendation vindicated by successful sperm retrieval? The answer, approximately 68.8 percent, provides strong enough confidence for clinical deployment. The negative predictive value of 81.5 percent means that when the model advises lower likelihood, it is correct about eight in ten times. This optimized Precision-Recall operating point was validated through nested cross-validation on our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort with 58 clinical features.

</details>

### 217. `pr_DecisionTree_adjusted`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-pr_DecisionTree_adjusted.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Gallery Figure
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 215

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (215 words)</summary>

> This figure shows the Precision-Recall curve for the Decision Tree model with an optimized threshold derived from the Youden J statistic. Decision Tree threshold adjustment on the Precision-Recall curve operates over a finite set of possible operating points, each corresponding to a different leaf probability cutoff in the tree structure. For Decision Tree with an AUC of 0.6457, threshold optimization changes the precision-recall trade-off but cannot improve the area under the curve, which reflects intrinsic model quality. An important nuance is that the Youden J criterion optimizes sensitivity plus specificity, not precision plus recall. Since precision depends on class prevalence while specificity does not, the optimal threshold for the ROC framework may differ from the optimal threshold for the Precision-Recall framework. In our dataset with approximately 36.7 percent positive cases, this discrepancy can be meaningful for clinical decision-making. Compared to the champion CatBoost model with precision of 68.8 percent and recall of 67.8 percent at its Youden J threshold, Decision Tree's adjusted operating point demonstrates that threshold optimization can improve the precision-recall balance but cannot overcome fundamental limitations in discriminative ability. For micro-TESE surgical counseling at Royan Institute, the adjusted Precision-Recall operating point provides the most clinically relevant view of how the model balances positive prediction reliability against positive case capture across our 2,413-patient cohort.

</details>
