Since precision depends on class prevalence while specificity does not, the optimal threshold for the ROC framework may differ from the optimal threshold for the Precision-Recall framework. In our dataset with approximately 36.7 percent positive cases, this discrepancy can be meaningful for clinical decision-making. Compared to the champion CatBoost model with precision of 68.8 percent and recall of 67.8 percent at its Youden J threshold, Voting Ensemble's adjusted operating point demonstrates that threshold optimization can improve the precision-recall balance but cannot overcome fundamental limitations in discriminative ability. For micro-TESE surgical counseling at Royan Institute, the adjusted Precision-Recall operating point provides the most clinically relevant view of how the model balances positive prediction reliability against positive case capture across our 2,413-patient cohort.

</details>

For XGBoost with an AUC of 0.8214, threshold optimization changes the precision-recall trade-off but cannot improve the area under the curve, which reflects intrinsic model quality. With an AUC of 0.8306, the confusion matrix reveals the following approximate distribution across our 2,413-patient cohort from Royan Institute. True positives number approximately 43, representing patients correctly identified as likely to have successful sperm retrieval during micro-TESE. These patients receive accurate positive prognoses that support their decision to proceed with surgery. True negatives number approximately 88, correctly identifying patients unlikely to yield sperm. ### 243. `cm_CatBoost_adjusted`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-cm_CatBoost_adjusted.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Model Results
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 237

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (237 words)</summary>

> This figure shows the confusion matrix for the champion CatBoost model after threshold optimization using the Youden J statistic. When the classification threshold moves from the default 0.5 to the optimized range of 0.444-0.507, the distribution of patients across the four confusion matrix cells shifts to reflect the new operating point. The AUC remains at 0.8306, but the balance between true positives, true negatives, false positives, and false negatives changes. At the optimized threshold, CatBoost achieves sensitivity of 67.8 percent and specificity of 82.3 percent, with a positive predictive value of 68.8 percent and negative predictive value of 81.5 percent. The Youden J criterion maximizes sensitivity plus specificity minus one, finding the point where the model best balances capturing successful micro-TESE cases against avoiding false alarms. Compared to the default threshold, the optimized threshold may shift some patients from false negative to true positive, correctly reclassifying additional surgical candidates, while potentially moving some true negatives to false positive status. This redistribution reflects the fundamental clinical trade-off: gaining more correctly identified positive cases always comes at some cost of additional false positives, and vice versa. The overall accuracy of 77.0 percent at the adjusted threshold provides the most realistic picture of CatBoost's expected performance when deployed as a clinical decision support tool for micro-TESE at Royan Institute. The Matthews correlation coefficient of 0.502 confirms balanced performance across all four confusion matrix quadrants for our 2,413-patient non-obstructive azoospermia cohort.

</details>

### 244. `cm_DecisionTree_adjusted`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-cm_DecisionTree_adjusted.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Gallery Figure
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 270

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (270 words)</summary>

> This figure presents the confusion matrix for the Decision Tree model after Youden J threshold optimization. Decision Tree threshold adjustment operates over a small set of possible confusion matrices, each corresponding to including or excluding specific leaf nodes from the positive prediction class. For Decision Tree with an AUC of 0.6457, the adjusted threshold shifts patients between categories compared to the default 0.5, seeking the mathematically optimal balance between sensitivity and specificity. In micro-TESE surgical planning, this redistribution has concrete clinical consequences. Each patient moved from false negative to true positive represents a correctly identified surgical candidate who might otherwise have missed their opportunity for biological parenthood. Each patient moved from true negative to false positive represents an additional patient undergoing invasive microsurgery without ultimately finding sperm. The Youden J criterion treats both error types equally, but individual fertility centers may weight them differently based on their clinical philosophy and patient population. Compared to the champion CatBoost model with its optimized threshold range of 0.444 to 0.507, Decision Tree's adjusted confusion matrix still demonstrates that threshold optimization alone cannot compensate for lower intrinsic discriminative ability. A model with lower AUC produces more classification errors even at its own optimal threshold compared to a superior model at its optimal threshold. This principle has practical implications for clinical deployment: investing in better feature engineering and model selection, as our version two pipeline demonstrates with CatBoost's improvement from AUC 0.7187 in version one to 0.8306 in version two, yields greater clinical benefit than threshold tuning alone. The analysis was performed on our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort with 58 clinical features using nested cross-validation.

</details>

### 255. `calibration_CatBoost`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-calibration_CatBoost.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Calibration & DCA
- **Project Brain:** `calibration_dca`
- **Word Count:** 244

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (244 words)</summary>

> This figure presents the calibration curve for the champion CatBoost model at the default classification threshold. Calibration addresses a fundamentally different question than discrimination. While AUC measures ranking ability, calibration tells us whether predicted probabilities are trustworthy as absolute numerical estimates. The calibration curve plots predicted probabilities on the horizontal axis against observed outcome frequencies on the vertical axis, with the diagonal representing perfect calibration. CatBoost achieves a Brier score of 0.2131, quantifying calibration quality on a scale from zero to one where lower is better. The Brier score decomposes into discrimination and calibration components. CatBoost's strong AUC of 0.8306 contributes to the discrimination component, while the calibration component reflects how faithfully predicted probabilities match reality. Examining the curve across probability ranges reveals CatBoost's calibration behavior. In lower bins, where predictions suggest less than 20 percent success probability, observed rates may deviate slightly. In the critical middle range around 30 to 50 percent, where most clinical decisions occur given our 36.7 percent base success rate, calibration is particularly important. At higher probability levels above 60 percent, CatBoost shows strong alignment between predictions and outcomes. For patient counseling during micro-TESE planning, calibration ensures that when a couple is told they have a 40 percent chance of finding sperm, that estimate is trustworthy rather than merely a ranking score. Post-hoc calibration techniques like Platt scaling or isotonic regression can further refine these estimates. This analysis was performed through nested cross-validation on our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort.

</details>

### 256. `calibration_DecisionTree`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-calibration_DecisionTree.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Calibration & DCA
- **Project Brain:** `calibration_dca`
- **Word Count:** 286

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (286 words)</summary>

> This figure shows the calibration curve for the Decision Tree model at the default threshold. Decision Tree calibration depends entirely on the purity of leaf nodes. Trees with pure leaves produce binary probabilities of zero or one, while impure leaves produce intermediate values based on the class ratio within each leaf. The calibration curve evaluates whether Decision Tree's predicted probabilities accurately reflect the true likelihood of successful sperm retrieval during micro-TESE. A perfectly calibrated model places all points on the diagonal reference line, meaning a predicted 30 percent probability corresponds to exactly 30 percent observed success. For Decision Tree with an AUC of 0.6457, the Brier score is not available for this model in the current analysis. The champion CatBoost model achieves a Brier score of 0.2131, providing the calibration benchmark for this study. Among all evaluated models, LightGBM achieved the best Brier score at 0.2006, demonstrating that the best-calibrated model is not necessarily the best-discriminating one, since CatBoost leads in AUC at 0.8306. For clinical deployment in micro-TESE counseling, both discrimination and calibration matter. Discrimination determines how well the model ranks patients by risk, while calibration determines whether the numerical probability estimates are accurate enough for patient communication. A model with excellent discrimination but poor calibration could correctly rank patients yet provide misleading probability numbers during preoperative counseling. Conversely, a perfectly calibrated model with poor discrimination would give accurate probabilities but fail to meaningfully differentiate between patients likely to succeed and those likely to fail. For couples navigating the emotionally charged decision about whether to proceed with micro-TESE for non-obstructive azoospermia, both aspects must meet clinical standards. This calibration analysis was conducted through nested cross-validation on our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort with 58 clinical features.

</details>

### 261. `calibration_LogisticRegression`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-calibration_LogisticRegression.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Calibration & DCA
- **Project Brain:** `calibration_dca`
- **Word Count:** 276

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (276 words)</summary>

> This figure shows the calibration curve for the Logistic Regression model at the default threshold. Logistic Regression is theoretically well-calibrated when its modeling assumptions are met, because it directly models the log-odds of the outcome, making its probability estimates inherently interpretable. The calibration curve evaluates whether Logistic Regression's predicted probabilities accurately reflect the true likelihood of successful sperm retrieval during micro-TESE. A perfectly calibrated model places all points on the diagonal reference line, meaning a predicted 30 percent probability corresponds to exactly 30 percent observed success. For Logistic Regression with an AUC of 0.7926, the Brier score is not available for this model in the current analysis. The champion CatBoost model achieves a Brier score of 0.2131, providing the calibration benchmark for this study. Among all evaluated models, LightGBM achieved the best Brier score at 0.2006, demonstrating that the best-calibrated model is not necessarily the best-discriminating one, since CatBoost leads in AUC at 0.8306. For clinical deployment in micro-TESE counseling, both discrimination and calibration matter. Discrimination determines how well the model ranks patients by risk, while calibration determines whether the numerical probability estimates are accurate enough for patient communication. A model with excellent discrimination but poor calibration could correctly rank patients yet provide misleading probability numbers during preoperative counseling. Conversely, a perfectly calibrated model with poor discrimination would give accurate probabilities but fail to meaningfully differentiate between patients likely to succeed and those likely to fail. For couples navigating the emotionally charged decision about whether to proceed with micro-TESE for non-obstructive azoospermia, both aspects must meet clinical standards. This calibration analysis was conducted through nested cross-validation on our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort with 58 clinical features.

</details>

- **Word Count:** 285

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (285 words)</summary>

> This figure shows the calibration curve for the Support Vector Machine model at the default threshold. SVM models do not produce calibrated probabilities natively. The probabilities used here are derived from Platt scaling, which fits a logistic function to the SVM decision values, adding a post-hoc calibration layer. The calibration curve evaluates whether Support Vector Machine's predicted probabilities accurately reflect the true likelihood of successful sperm retrieval during micro-TESE. A perfectly calibrated model places all points on the diagonal reference line, meaning a predicted 30 percent probability corresponds to exactly 30 percent observed success. For Support Vector Machine with an AUC of 0.5473, the Brier score is not available for this model in the current analysis. Among all evaluated models, LightGBM achieved the best Brier score at 0.2006, demonstrating that the best-calibrated model is not necessarily the best-discriminating one, since CatBoost leads in AUC at 0.8306. For clinical deployment in micro-TESE counseling, both discrimination and calibration matter. Discrimination determines how well the model ranks patients by risk, while calibration determines whether the numerical probability estimates are accurate enough for patient communication. A model with excellent discrimination but poor calibration could correctly rank patients yet provide misleading probability numbers during preoperative counseling. Conversely, a perfectly calibrated model with poor discrimination would give accurate probabilities but fail to meaningfully differentiate between patients likely to succeed and those likely to fail. For couples navigating the emotionally charged decision about whether to proceed with micro-TESE for non-obstructive azoospermia, both aspects must meet clinical standards. ### 270. `calibration_CatBoost_adjusted`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-calibration_CatBoost_adjusted.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Calibration & DCA
- **Project Brain:** `calibration_dca`
- **Word Count:** 236

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (236 words)</summary>

> This figure displays the calibration curve for the champion CatBoost model with the threshold adjusted using the Youden J statistic. An important conceptual point is that the calibration curve itself is independent of the classification threshold because it evaluates the full range of probability outputs rather than the binary decision. However, the threshold context changes which region of the calibration curve matters most clinically. Patients whose predicted probabilities fall near the optimized threshold of 0.444-0.507 are the borderline cases where calibration accuracy is most consequential. CatBoost's Brier score of 0.2131 quantifies overall probability accuracy. At the adjusted threshold, the model achieves sensitivity of 67.8 percent and specificity of 82.3 percent. Good calibration around the threshold region means that small threshold adjustments produce predictable, proportionate changes in the confusion matrix. Poor calibration near the threshold would mean that clinicians cannot reliably fine-tune the sensitivity-specificity balance by adjusting the cutoff, as the relationship between threshold changes and classification outcomes would be unpredictable. For clinical deployment, this reliability is essential. Fertility centers may wish to adjust the threshold based on their specific priorities, perhaps favoring sensitivity to ensure no viable candidates are missed, or favoring specificity to minimize unnecessary surgeries. CatBoost's calibration quality supports such threshold customization. When a patient's predicted probability sits near the decision boundary, well-calibrated probabilities enable more precise and honest counseling about the genuine uncertainty surrounding their micro-TESE prognosis across our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort.

</details>

### 271. `calibration_DecisionTree_adjusted`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-calibration_DecisionTree_adjusted.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Calibration & DCA
- **Project Brain:** `calibration_dca`
- **Word Count:** 251

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (251 words)</summary>

> This figure presents the calibration curve for the Decision Tree model with the threshold adjusted using Youden J optimization. Decision Tree calibration at the adjusted threshold depends on the specific leaf probabilities near the cutoff, and the discrete nature of these probabilities creates sharp rather than smooth calibration transitions. While the calibration curve is technically independent of threshold choice, the adjusted threshold context highlights which probability region is most clinically relevant. For Decision Tree with an AUC of 0.6457, calibration quality near the optimized threshold determines whether threshold adjustments produce predictable changes in the confusion matrix. Poor calibration near the decision boundary means clinicians cannot reliably fine-tune sensitivity-specificity balance, while good calibration supports confident threshold customization. The champion CatBoost model achieves a Brier score of 0.2131, supporting reliable threshold optimization at its range of 0.444 to 0.507. For clinical deployment at Royan Institute, calibration at the adjusted threshold directly affects the quality of probability estimates communicated during preoperative counseling. When a patient's predicted probability falls near the decision boundary, the calibration quality in that region determines whether the communicated estimate is trustworthy. A well-calibrated model at its adjusted threshold enables honest, precise counseling where a stated 45 percent chance truly means approximately 45 out of 100 similar patients achieve successful sperm retrieval. This level of probability accuracy is essential for informed consent discussions about micro-TESE surgery for non-obstructive azoospermia, where couples must weigh the benefits of potential biological parenthood against the invasiveness of the procedure across our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort.

</details>

Poor calibration near the decision boundary means clinicians cannot reliably fine-tune sensitivity-specificity balance, while good calibration supports confident threshold customization. The champion CatBoost model achieves a Brier score of 0.2131, supporting reliable threshold optimization at its range of 0.444 to 0.507. For clinical deployment at Royan Institute, calibration at the adjusted threshold directly affects the quality of probability estimates communicated during preoperative counseling. When a patient's predicted probability falls near the decision boundary, the calibration quality in that region determines whether the communicated estimate is trustworthy. A well-calibrated model at its adjusted threshold enables honest, precise counseling where a stated 45 percent chance truly means approximately 45 out of 100 similar patients achieve successful sperm retrieval. This level of probability accuracy is essential for informed consent discussions about micro-TESE surgery for non-obstructive azoospermia, where couples must weigh the benefits of potential biological parenthood against the invasiveness of the procedure across our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort.

</details>

### 282. `roc_comparison_all_models`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-roc_comparison_all_models.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Model Performance
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 281

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (281 words)</summary>

> This figure presents a panoramic comparison of Receiver Operating Characteristic curves for all 16 machine learning models evaluated in our micro-TESE outcome prediction study, overlaid on a single plot. This comprehensive visualization enables direct visual comparison of discriminative ability across the entire algorithmic landscape, from simple linear classifiers to complex ensemble methods. Each curve plots sensitivity against one minus specificity, with the diagonal reference line representing random chance at an AUC of 0.50. Starting from the top performers in the version two pipeline, CatBoost leads with an AUC of 0.8306 and a confidence interval of 0.823 to 0.845, its curve bowing most strongly toward the upper left corner of ideal performance. XGBoost follows at 0.8214 with a confidence interval of 0.814 to 0.835, then LightGBM at 0.8213 with 0.809 to 0.836, and Random Forest at 0.8157 with 0.809 to 0.829. These four gradient-boosted and tree-ensemble models form a clear top tier that substantially outperforms the remaining algorithms. Logistic Regression at 0.7926 represents the best linear model, demonstrating the performance ceiling of linear approaches on our complex clinical dataset. The middle tier includes Gradient Boosting at 0.7291, Stacking Ensemble at 0.7222, Extra Trees at 0.7100, and Voting Ensemble at 0.7060 in the version one evaluation. At the bottom, SVM at 0.5473 and Naive Bayes at just 0.5232 cluster near the random chance diagonal, indicating minimal discriminative ability for this prediction task. The visual separation between the top gradient boosting models and the remaining algorithms demonstrates why this algorithm family dominates clinical prediction modeling. All 16 models were evaluated using nested cross-validation with 50 iterations of Bayesian hyperparameter optimization each on our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort, ensuring these rankings reflect genuine generalization ability.

</details>

### 283. `roc_top5_with_CI`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-roc_top5_with_CI.mp3`

- **Dashboard Section:** Model Performance
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 270

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (270 words)</summary>

> This figure presents ROC curves for the top five models with confidence interval bands derived from bootstrap resampling, providing a statistically rigorous comparison that goes beyond simple point estimates. The shaded bands around each curve represent the range of ROC trajectories expected if the study were repeated with different patient samples from the same population. CatBoost, our champion model, shows its ROC curve centered at an AUC of 0.8306 with the confidence interval spanning 0.823 to 0.845, a remarkably narrow band of only 0.022 AUC units indicating highly stable discrimination. XGBoost's band centers at 0.8214 with limits of 0.814 to 0.835, showing similar stability to CatBoost. LightGBM centers at 0.8213 with a slightly wider interval of 0.809 to 0.836, reflecting marginally more variability across bootstrap samples. Random Forest achieves 0.8157 with the tightest interval at 0.809 to 0.829, demonstrating exceptional estimation precision. Logistic Regression rounds out the top five at 0.7926. The critical insight from overlapping confidence bands is about statistical significance. Where bands overlap substantially, the performance difference between models may not be statistically significant. CatBoost's lower bound of 0.823 sits above LightGBM's point estimate of 0.8213, suggesting CatBoost's advantage is consistent across samples. The separation between the top four tree-ensemble models and Logistic Regression is more pronounced, confirming the value of nonlinear modeling for this prediction task. For clinicians, confidence intervals answer a vital question: would this model maintain its performance with a different group of 2,413 patients? The narrow bands for our top models provide reassuring evidence that these performance estimates would replicate in future cohorts at Royan Institute and potentially other fertility centers treating non-obstructive azoospermia.

</details>

### 284. `pr_comparison_all_models`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-pr_comparison_all_models.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Model Results
- **Project Brain:** `results`
- **Word Count:** 266

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (266 words)</summary>

> This figure overlays Precision-Recall curves for all models in our micro-TESE prediction study, providing a comprehensive comparison that is particularly informative given our dataset's class imbalance. With successful sperm retrieval occurring in approximately 36.7 percent of our 2,413 patients from Royan Institute, the Precision-Recall framework provides a stricter evaluation than ROC analysis by focusing exclusively on positive prediction quality. The horizontal baseline at the prevalence level of 0.367 represents random classification. Any model with a curve substantially above this line demonstrates genuine predictive value for identifying surgical candidates. CatBoost's curve extends furthest into the upper right, maintaining precision of 68.83 percent at a recall of 67.8 percent, consistent with its champion AUC of 0.8306. XGBoost and LightGBM show similar curve trajectories, reflecting their closely matched AUCs of 0.8214 and 0.8213. As we move to weaker models, the curves droop more rapidly toward the prevalence baseline. Naive Bayes at AUC 0.5232 and SVM at 0.5473 show curves barely above the random baseline, confirming minimal positive prediction utility. The Precision-Recall comparison reveals a key clinical truth: maintaining high precision while achieving adequate recall requires strong underlying discrimination. Models that achieve high recall by predicting positive for nearly everyone, such as Naive Bayes with 82 percent sensitivity but only 23 percent specificity, pay a severe penalty in precision that the Precision-Recall curve makes immediately visible. For micro-TESE surgical planning where each positive prediction could lead to an invasive microsurgical procedure, the reliability of positive predictions is paramount. This Precision-Recall comparison validates CatBoost's selection as champion, demonstrating that its positive predictions are the most trustworthy among all 16 models evaluated.

</details>

Each two-by-two matrix shows true positives, true negatives, false positives, and false negatives for one model, creating a comprehensive panorama of how different algorithms distribute their predictions. The champion CatBoost model shows the most favorable pattern with approximately 43 true positives, 88 true negatives, 19 false positives, and 20 false negatives among 2,413 patients, reflecting its sensitivity of 67.8 percent and specificity of 82.3 percent at AUC 0.8306. XGBoost at AUC 0.8214 and LightGBM at 0.8213 produce similar but slightly less favorable distributions. The grid reveals striking differences in classification bias across model families. Naive Bayes at AUC 0.5232 exhibits extreme positive bias with sensitivity of 82 percent but specificity of only 22.6 percent, essentially recommending surgery for nearly every patient. Conversely, SVM at AUC 0.5473 shows extreme negative bias with specificity of 83.8 percent but sensitivity of only 29.9 percent, denying surgery to most patients including many who would benefit. K-Nearest Neighbors presents an interesting intermediate pattern with high sensitivity of 70.9 percent but low specificity of 48.3 percent. These bias patterns have direct clinical consequences. A model biased toward positive predictions maximizes the number of patients offered surgery but at the cost of many unsuccessful procedures. A model biased toward negative predictions minimizes unnecessary surgeries but misses many patients who could achieve biological parenthood through micro-TESE. CatBoost's confusion matrix achieves the best balance between these competing objectives, which is why it was selected for clinical deployment. This grid comparison, generated through nested cross-validation on our Royan Institute cohort of 2,413 non-obstructive azoospermia patients, provides clinicians with the most tangible evidence for model selection.

</details>

The diagonal reference line represents perfect calibration where predicted probabilities exactly match observed outcome frequencies. Among models with available Brier scores, LightGBM achieves the best at 0.2006, followed by Gradient Boosting at 0.2025, XGBoost at 0.2055, CatBoost at 0.2131, and Stacking Ensemble at 0.2259. A critical observation is that the best-calibrated model is not the best-discriminating model. LightGBM has the lowest Brier score but ranks third in version two AUC behind CatBoost at 0.8306 and XGBoost at 0.8214. This dissociation occurs because the Brier score combines discrimination and calibration components, and a model can achieve a low Brier score through excellent calibration even with somewhat lower discrimination. The overlay reveals important calibration patterns. Models with poor discrimination like Naive Bayes and SVM tend to produce narrow probability ranges, making their calibration curves appear close to the diagonal. However, this apparent calibration is misleading because these models predict similar probabilities for everyone, providing no clinical utility despite technically satisfying calibration criteria. Models with strong discrimination like CatBoost and XGBoost spread predictions more widely, making calibration imperfections more visible but the model far more clinically useful. For patient counseling in micro-TESE for non-obstructive azoospermia, calibration determines whether probability estimates are trustworthy enough for informed consent discussions. A couple told they have a 45 percent chance of sperm retrieval needs to trust that number represents reality, not just a relative ranking score. This overlay comparison across our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort confirms that the top gradient boosting models achieve both strong discrimination and reasonable calibration, supporting clinical deployment.

</details>

### 287. `ice_E2`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-ice_E2.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Project Brain:** `xai`
- **Word Count:** 331

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (331 words)</summary>

> This figure presents the Individual Conditional Expectation plot for Estradiol, commonly known as E2, showing how each individual patient's predicted probability of successful sperm retrieval changes as Estradiol levels vary while all other clinical features are held constant. Each thin line on this plot represents one patient from our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort, and the diversity of line trajectories reveals the heterogeneity in how E2 influences predictions across the patient population. Estradiol ranks fourteenth in global SHAP importance for our champion CatBoost model with a mean absolute SHAP value of 0.043. Despite this relatively modest global ranking, the ICE plot reveals important individual-level variation that aggregate statistics obscure. Some patients show a sharp decline in predicted probability as E2 increases, suggesting that for their particular clinical profile, elevated Estradiol is strongly associated with worse outcomes. Other patients show relatively flat lines, meaning their prediction is insensitive to E2 changes because other dominant features like LH or FSH are driving their prediction. A small subset of patients may even show slightly increasing prediction probabilities with rising E2, which could reflect the paradoxical clinical observation that some residual Estradiol production signals preserved Leydig cell function and therefore some residual testicular activity. The clinical relevance of this heterogeneity is profound. Estradiol is produced through aromatization of testosterone, and elevated levels can suppress gonadotropin release through negative feedback on the hypothalamic-pituitary axis. However, the impact of this suppression varies depending on the patient's overall hormonal milieu and testicular reserve. A young patient with adequate testicular volume may tolerate higher E2 levels without significant impact on sperm retrieval probability, while an older patient with compromised testicular function may see a dramatic decrease. This ICE plot underscores why personalized prediction matters in micro-TESE surgical planning for non-obstructive azoospermia. The CatBoost model with its AUC of 0.8306 captures these individual-level nuances through its ensemble of 657 gradient-boosted trees, each contributing to a patient-specific prediction trajectory that respects the complex interplay between Estradiol and the remaining 57 clinical features.

</details>

The ICE plot adds critical nuance to this global ranking by showing that pH's impact is far from uniform across patients. Normal seminal plasma pH typically ranges from 7.2 to 8.0, with values reflecting the combined secretions of the prostate, seminal vesicles, and other accessory glands. In men with non-obstructive azoospermia, abnormalities in seminal pH may indicate broader dysfunction of the reproductive tract. Looking at the ICE curves, some patients show a pronounced inverted U-shaped relationship, where prediction probability peaks at intermediate pH values and drops at extremes. This pattern suggests that both abnormally acidic and abnormally alkaline semen environments correlate with poorer prognosis for these individuals. Other patients display monotonically increasing or decreasing trajectories, reflecting how the interaction between pH and their specific hormonal profile, testicular volume, and other clinical features creates different response patterns. The heterogeneity visible in this ICE plot is clinically significant because it demonstrates that seminal pH cannot be interpreted in isolation. A pH of 7.8 might improve the predicted probability for one patient while having minimal effect on another whose prediction is dominated by extreme LH values or very small testicular volume. This is precisely the kind of complex, context-dependent relationship that our CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306 excels at capturing, and that simpler models or univariate clinical rules would miss entirely. The approximately 32 percent of patients with available seminal pH data in our 2,413-patient cohort provide sufficient signal for the model to learn these heterogeneous patterns.

### 292. `pdp_E2`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-pdp_E2.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Project Brain:** `xai`
- **Word Count:** 340

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (340 words)</summary>

> This Partial Dependence Plot for Estradiol, or E2, shows the average marginal effect of Estradiol levels on the predicted probability of successful micro-TESE sperm retrieval across our entire patient population. Unlike the ICE plot which shows individual patient trajectories, the PDP presents the mean of all those individual curves, revealing the overall direction and shape of E2's influence on the model's predictions. In our champion CatBoost model with an AUC of 0.8306, Estradiol ranks fourteenth in global SHAP importance with a mean absolute value of 0.043. The PDP curve translates this relatively modest global ranking into a visual representation of how the average prediction changes across the range of observed E2 values in our 2,413-patient cohort. The shape of the PDP curve for E2 likely reveals a non-monotonic relationship. At lower Estradiol levels, the average predicted probability may be relatively stable, reflecting that baseline E2 within the normal range does not strongly influence the model's average prediction. As E2 rises above normal thresholds, the average prediction may decrease, consistent with the clinical understanding that elevated Estradiol can suppress the hypothalamic-pituitary-gonadal axis through negative feedback, potentially impairing residual spermatogenesis. The clinical thresholds visible on this PDP are particularly informative for reproductive endocrinologists. If the curve shows a clear inflection point, this identifies the E2 level at which the average patient begins to see meaningful changes in predicted outcome. Such thresholds could inform clinical decisions about whether anti-estrogen therapy with agents like aromatase inhibitors or selective estrogen receptor modulators might improve outcomes before proceeding with micro-TESE. It is important to remember that the PDP shows the average effect, and as the corresponding ICE plot demonstrates, individual patients may deviate substantially from this average. The interaction between E2 and testicular volume was identified as the second and third most important feature interaction in our analysis, meaning that E2's clinical significance depends heavily on the patient's testicular measurements. Nevertheless, the PDP provides a useful summary of the population-level trend that clinicians can use as a starting reference point during preoperative counseling for non-obstructive azoospermia.

</details>

The microsurgeon's skill in identifying these focal regions during the procedure can sometimes overcome a statistically unfavorable clinical profile. This false negative case reinforces an important clinical principle: our CatBoost model with its AUC of 0.8306 and sensitivity of 67.8 percent should never serve as a sole gatekeeper for surgical access. Approximately 32.2 percent of positive cases, this patient included, are not captured by the model at its optimal threshold. Where Gradient Boosting diverges in feature importance, it may indicate features whose value is algorithm-dependent rather than intrinsically predictive. The overfitting gap of 0.252 for Gradient Boosting, calculated from a training AUC of 0.9807, suggests that some features may be overemphasized in the SHAP importance due to overfitting. Features that rank highly in Gradient Boosting's SHAP but not in CatBoost's more regularized framework should be interpreted with caution. > This figure shows the SHAP bar plot for LightGBM, ranking features by their mean absolute SHAP values in this Microsoft-developed gradient boosting framework. In the version two pipeline, LightGBM achieved an AUC of 0.8213 with a confidence interval of 0.809 to 0.836, making it virtually tied with XGBoost as the second and third best models behind champion CatBoost at 0.8306. The SHAP bar chart for LightGBM provides an independent assessment of feature importance from an algorithm that uses histogram-based binning and leaf-wise tree growth, fundamentally different architectural choices compared to CatBoost's ordered boosting with depth-wise growth. These algorithmic differences mean that agreement in feature importance rankings between LightGBM and CatBoost is particularly strong evidence for genuine predictive relevance. Comparing to CatBoost's top four SHAP features of LH at 0.317, Age at 0.270, FSH at 0.250, and Testosterone at 0.212, the LightGBM SHAP bar chart reveals whether these same features dominate or whether LightGBM's different learning strategy surfaces alternative priorities. Given that both models achieve very similar overall AUC values, features that rank highly in both represent the most robust predictive signals in our dataset. LightGBM's leaf-wise growth strategy tends to create deeper, more specialized trees that may capture fine-grained feature interactions differently than CatBoost's balanced depth-wise approach with a maximum depth of 6. This could result in LightGBM assigning higher importance to features involved in complex interactions while potentially underweighting features with simple monotonic relationships. It is important to note that while LightGBM was the top performer in the version one pipeline with an AUC of 0.7327, the version one results reflect a pipeline with a data processing issue that has been corrected in version two. The version two SHAP importance for LightGBM represents the authoritative feature ranking for this algorithm, evaluated on our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort with 58 clinical features including 18 pathology features derived from free-text histopathological reports.

</details>

### 306. `shap_bar_RandomForest`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-shap_bar_RandomForest.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Project Brain:** `xai`
- **Word Count:** 319

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (319 words)</summary>

> This figure presents the SHAP bar plot for the Random Forest model, which achieved an AUC of 0.8157 with a confidence interval of 0.809 to 0.829 in the version two pipeline, placing it fourth among our 16 evaluated models. Random Forest uses a fundamentally different ensemble strategy than gradient boosting: it builds hundreds of independent decision trees on bootstrap samples with random feature subsets and combines them through majority voting. The SHAP bar chart for Random Forest reveals which features this algorithm relies on most heavily. Because Random Forest constructs trees independently rather than sequentially, its feature importance reflects the inherent predictive value of each feature without the sequential correction dynamics that characterize gradient boosting methods. This makes Random Forest SHAP values a particularly clean measure of each feature's standalone predictive power. Comparing Random Forest's feature ranking to CatBoost's SHAP hierarchy of LH at 0.317, Age at 0.270, FSH at 0.250, and Testosterone at 0.212 reveals whether the gradient boosting advantage comes from better utilizing the same features or from extracting signal from additional features that Random Forest underweights. Features that rank highly in both algorithms are the most confidently important predictors of micro-TESE outcomes. Random Forest may show a more distributed importance profile compared to gradient boosting methods, with the gap between the top-ranked and lower-ranked features being smaller. This is characteristic of Random Forest's averaging mechanism, where many trees contribute partially to each feature's importance without the focusing effect of sequential error correction. With an overfitting gap of 0.236, Random Forest shows somewhat more memorization than CatBoost's 0.189 gap, which may slightly inflate the SHAP importance of features that happen to be predictive in the training data but less so in validation. Despite this, Random Forest provides a valuable complementary perspective on feature importance for our 2,413-patient micro-TESE prediction study, helping verify which clinical features are robustly predictive across different algorithmic paradigms in non-obstructive azoospermia outcome prediction.

</details>

This comparison validates the thorough model selection process in our study, where all 16 algorithms were evaluated using nested cross-validation with 50 iterations of Bayesian hyperparameter optimization on our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort. Even with optimal hyperparameters, SVM's fundamental architecture proved unsuitable for this prediction task.

If the coloring variable is LH or FSH, the plot shows how hormonal context modifies the testicular size-outcome relationship. A patient with favorable testicular volume but severely elevated gonadotropins might receive a smaller positive SHAP contribution than a patient with the same testicular volume but more moderate hormonal values. This interaction captures the clinical reality that testicular size and hormonal status jointly determine prognosis. The vertical spread at any given testicular size quantifies the interaction effect magnitude. Wide spreads at clinically ambiguous testicular sizes, where the interaction with other features determines the prediction direction, identify the measurement zones where additional clinical information is most valuable for refining the prediction. The dependence plot investigates whether this zero average masks conditional effects that emerge in specific clinical contexts. The horizontal axis displays the count of infertile family members, typically a discrete variable with values at zero, one, two, or more. The vertical axis shows the SHAP contribution, and the color encoding identifies an interacting feature. Family history of infertility is a marker of potential genetic susceptibility to reproductive dysfunction. Having multiple infertile relatives increases the likelihood that the patient's non-obstructive azoospermia has a heritable genetic basis, such as Y chromosome microdeletions or autosomal genetic variants affecting spermatogenesis. However, whether a genetic etiology improves or worsens micro-TESE prognosis depends on the specific genetic mechanism. The dependence plot may show that patients with no infertile family members cluster around zero SHAP contribution, while patients with one or more affected relatives show variable SHAP values depending on the interaction with other clinical features. If the color-encoded feature is Age or a hormonal marker, you can see how family history interacts with the patient's own clinical profile to influence the prediction. This severe sparsity explains the zero global SHAP value and limits the interpretability of any patterns visible on this dependence plot. The few available data points should be viewed as hypothesis-generating rather than confirmatory. For clinical practice at Royan Institute, this dependence plot confirms that while family history of infertility is a relevant clinical consideration, it does not contribute to the CatBoost model's predictions due to data limitations. The model's AUC of 0.8306 is achieved primarily through hormonal markers, testicular measurements, and demographics, with family history remaining an area for potential improvement with more complete data collection in future studies of non-obstructive azoospermia.

</details>

### 324. `shap_waterfall_RandomForest_Advanced-Paternal-Age`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-shap_waterfall_RandomForest_Advanced-Paternal-Age.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Project Brain:** `xai`
- **Word Count:** 409

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (409 words)</summary>

> This SHAP waterfall plot shows how the Random Forest model builds its prediction for an advanced paternal age patient, typically a man over 40 to 45 years old presenting with non-obstructive azoospermia. Advanced paternal age is associated with progressive testicular decline, reduced Leydig cell function, and cumulative oxidative damage to spermatogenic tissue. The waterfall begins at the base value, which represents the average predicted probability across all patients in our dataset, approximately corresponding to the overall sperm retrieval success rate of 36.7 percent. From this starting point, each feature pushes the prediction either higher, shown as red bars extending to the right, or lower, shown as blue bars extending to the left, until the final prediction value is reached at the end. Random Forest achieved an AUC of 0.8157 with a confidence interval of 0.809-0.829 in the version two pipeline. Random Forest builds its predictions through majority voting across hundreds of independently constructed decision trees, each trained on a bootstrap sample with random feature subsets. This fundamentally different ensemble strategy means that feature contributions in the waterfall may show a more distributed pattern compared to gradient boosting models, as no single tree dominates the prediction. Reading the waterfall from bottom to top, the features are ordered by their absolute contribution magnitude for this specific patient. The largest bars represent the features most responsible for pushing this patient's prediction away from the population average. For the advanced paternal age scenario, the dominant bars likely correspond to the patient's values on the top SHAP-ranked features from our champion CatBoost model: LH at global SHAP rank one with 0.317, Age at rank two with 0.270, FSH at rank three with 0.250, and Testosterone at rank four with 0.212. Because Random Forest's trees are built independently rather than sequentially, the waterfall shows contributions that reflect the aggregate voting pattern of many diverse tree perspectives rather than the refined sequential corrections of gradient boosting. Comparing this Random Forest waterfall to the CatBoost waterfall for the same patient reveals how different algorithms explain the same clinical case. Where both models assign large contributions to the same features in the same direction, clinicians can be most confident in the clinical interpretation. Where they disagree, it highlights areas where the algorithmic approach influences the explanation, reminding us that explanations are model-specific while the clinical reality is model-independent. This multi-model perspective enriches the interpretability of our micro-TESE prediction system for non-obstructive azoospermia across our 2,413-patient Royan Institute cohort.

</details>

### 331. `shap_waterfall_SVM_Advanced-Paternal-Age`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-shap_waterfall_SVM_Advanced-Paternal-Age.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Project Brain:** `xai`
- **Word Count:** 400

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (400 words)</summary>

> This SHAP waterfall plot presents the Support Vector Machine's step-by-step explanation for an advanced paternal age patient, typically a man over 40 to 45 years old presenting with non-obstructive azoospermia. Advanced paternal age is associated with progressive testicular decline, reduced Leydig cell function, and cumulative oxidative damage to spermatogenic tissue. The waterfall starts from the base value representing the average prediction, and each feature's contribution is displayed as a colored bar that cumulatively builds toward the final predicted probability. Red bars indicate features pushing toward a positive prediction of successful sperm retrieval, while blue bars push toward a negative prediction. SVM achieved an AUC of 0.5473 in the version one pipeline, ranking fifteenth among 16 models. SVM with a radial basis function kernel maps clinical features into a higher-dimensional space to find separating hyperplanes. With an AUC of only 0.5473, barely above chance, SVM's waterfall explanations should be interpreted with caution as the model itself has not learned reliable predictive patterns from our clinical data. For the advanced paternal age scenario, examining SVM's waterfall alongside the waterfalls from Random Forest and XGBoost provides a unique perspective. SVM's feature contributions may appear more muted or unpredictable compared to the gradient boosting models because the RBF kernel's decision boundary does not align well with the true clinical patterns in our data. The overfitting gap of only 0.007, with both training and validation AUCs near 0.55, confirms that SVM is fundamentally underfitting rather than having learned and overfitted to spurious patterns. SVM's waterfall contributions reflect the geometry of the decision boundary in the transformed feature space rather than learned clinical relationships. The near-chance performance means these contributions may not correspond to genuine clinical patterns. The contrast between SVM's waterfall and those from better-performing models like XGBoost at AUC 0.8214 is itself instructive. Features that show large, clinically coherent contributions in XGBoost but small or directionless contributions in SVM highlight aspects of the data that require nonlinear learning algorithms to capture. The top SHAP features from CatBoost, including LH at 0.317, Age at 0.270, and FSH at 0.250, may show greatly diminished contributions in SVM's waterfall, visually demonstrating why algorithm selection is critical for clinical prediction. While SVM's individual patient explanations should not guide clinical decisions given its poor overall performance, comparing its waterfall to higher-performing models deepens our understanding of what makes effective feature utilization possible in predicting micro-TESE outcomes for non-obstructive azoospermia.

</details>

SVM with a radial basis function kernel maps clinical features into a higher-dimensional space to find separating hyperplanes. With an AUC of only 0.5473, barely above chance, SVM's waterfall explanations should be interpreted with caution as the model itself has not learned reliable predictive patterns from our clinical data. For the false negative scenario, examining SVM's waterfall alongside the waterfalls from Random Forest and XGBoost provides a unique perspective. SVM's feature contributions may appear more muted or unpredictable compared to the gradient boosting models because the RBF kernel's decision boundary does not align well with the true clinical patterns in our data. The overfitting gap of only 0.007, with both training and validation AUCs near 0.55, confirms that SVM is fundamentally underfitting rather than having learned and overfitted to spurious patterns. SVM's waterfall contributions reflect the geometry of the decision boundary in the transformed feature space rather than learned clinical relationships. The near-chance performance means these contributions may not correspond to genuine clinical patterns. The contrast between SVM's waterfall and those from better-performing models like XGBoost at AUC 0.8214 is itself instructive. Features that show large, clinically coherent contributions in XGBoost but small or directionless contributions in SVM highlight aspects of the data that require nonlinear learning algorithms to capture. The top SHAP features from CatBoost, including LH at 0.317, Age at 0.270, and FSH at 0.250, may show greatly diminished contributions in SVM's waterfall, visually demonstrating why algorithm selection is critical for clinical prediction. While SVM's individual patient explanations should not guide clinical decisions given its poor overall performance, comparing its waterfall to higher-performing models deepens our understanding of what makes effective feature utilization possible in predicting micro-TESE outcomes for non-obstructive azoospermia.

</details>

### 338. `shap_waterfall_XGBoost_Advanced-Paternal-Age`
- **Audio File:** `public/audio/ai-assist/fig-xai-shap_waterfall_XGBoost_Advanced-Paternal-Age.mp3`
- **Dashboard Phase:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Dashboard Section:** Explainability (XAI)
- **Project Brain:** `xai`
- **Word Count:** 403

<details>
<summary>📜 Full Script (403 words)</summary>

> This SHAP waterfall plot reveals how XGBoost constructs its prediction for an advanced paternal age patient, typically a man over 40 to 45 years old presenting with non-obstructive azoospermia. Advanced paternal age is associated with progressive testicular decline, reduced Leydig cell function, and cumulative oxidative damage to spermatogenic tissue. Starting from the base value, which reflects the population average predicted probability near the 36.7 percent overall success rate, each feature's contribution is layered on cumulatively. Red bars push the prediction upward toward successful sperm retrieval, while blue bars pull it downward toward an unsuccessful prediction, and the final value at the top represents XGBoost's actual output for this patient. XGBoost is the second-best model in our version two pipeline with an AUC of 0.8214 and a confidence interval of 0.814-0.835, placing it just 0.0092 points behind the champion CatBoost at 0.8306. XGBoost is the second-best model in our pipeline at AUC 0.8214, just 0.0092 points behind the champion CatBoost. Its waterfall explanations are among the most reliable comparison points because XGBoost's near-identical performance suggests it has learned similar predictive patterns through its L1 and L2 regularized gradient boosting framework. For the advanced paternal age scenario, the waterfall reveals which specific feature values are most responsible for XGBoost's prediction. The largest bars correspond to the features where this patient's values deviate most significantly from population norms in ways that the model has learned to associate with positive or negative outcomes. Given XGBoost's close performance to CatBoost, these feature contributions should substantially agree with CatBoost's assessment. XGBoost's level-wise tree growth with L1 and L2 regularization may produce sharper feature contribution boundaries compared to CatBoost's ordered boosting, potentially showing more pronounced step-like contributions in the waterfall. The comparison between XGBoost's and CatBoost's waterfalls for this same patient is among the most clinically meaningful comparisons in our dashboard. Because both models achieve AUCs above 0.82, agreements in feature contributions represent robust clinical signals. If both models identify the same features driving this patient's prediction in the same direction, clinicians at Royan Institute can communicate these findings with high confidence during preoperative counseling. Minor differences in contribution magnitudes reflect the algorithmic nuances between XGBoost's L1 and L2 regularized trees and CatBoost's ordered boosting with L2 leaf regularization of 3.51. This waterfall enriches our understanding of the advanced paternal age archetype through XGBoost's complementary analytical lens, contributing to the comprehensive explainability framework for micro-TESE outcome prediction in non-obstructive azoospermia.

</details>
